The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Reducing Third Figure to First: Two Methods
Bajuri explains reduction methods for third figure moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which moods of the third figure can be reduced to the first figure by converting the minor premise, and which require conversion of the major or proof by hypothesis.

Core Idea
Not all third figure moods reduce the same way; some need conversion of the major or hypothetical proof.

The third figure of the categorical syllogism has six moods. While many can be reduced to the first figure by converting the minor premise, the fourth and sixth moods require alternative methods. This lesson walks through the conditions for reduction and the specific techniques for each mood, based on Bajuri's commentary on the Bannani.

Guided Path
1
Recall the conditions for first-figure productivity
The first figure requires an affirmative minor premise and a universal major premise. These conditions will determine whether a reduction via conversion succeeds or produces a sterile mood.
2
Identify the four moods reducible by converting the minor
The first, second, third, and fifth moods of the third figure can be reduced by converting the minor premise (making it the major) and, if necessary, converting the conclusion. Study Bajuri's examples for each.
For the second mood, the conclusion also needs conversion after the reduction.
3
Examine the fourth mood: convert the major instead
The fourth mood has a particular major, so converting the minor still leaves a particular major, violating the universal major condition. Instead, convert the major to make it the minor, then convert the conclusion.
Bajuri gives the example: 'Every human is an animal, some human is rational' → convert major to 'some rational is human'.
4
Understand the sixth mood: proof by hypothesis
The sixth mood has a particular negative major. Neither conversion of the minor nor the major works; instead, the conclusion is proven by assuming a hypothetical instance (dalil al-iftirad).
This method is not detailed in the passage, but it is the standard resort.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ يَكُنْ ظَاهِراً فِي كُلِّ مِنَ الضَّرْبِ الثَّانِي وَالرَّابع: لِأَنَّ الضَّرْبَ الثَّانِي مُرَكَّبٌ مِنْ سَالِبَةٍ كُلِّيَّةٍ صُغْرَى وَمُوجَبَةٍ كُلِّيَّةٍ كُبْرَى، وَلَوْ رُدَّ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ الكُبْرَى لَكَانَ مِنْ ضُرُوبِهِ العَقِيمَةِ؛ لِأَنَّ صُغْرَاهُ سَالِبَةٌ وَكُبْرَاهُ جُزْئِيَّةٌ، وَقَدْ تَقَدَّمَ أَنَّهُ يُشْتَرَطُ لِإِنتَاجِ الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ: إِيجَابُ الصُّغْرَى، وَكُلِّيَّةُ الكُبْرَى، فَلَا يُمْكِنُ رَدُّهُ بِذَلِكَ. وَإِنَّمَا يُمْكِنُ رَدُّهُ بِعَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى وَجَعْلَهَا كُبْرَى ثُمَّ عَكْسِ النَّتِيجَةِ، فَإِذَا قُلْتَ مَثَلاً: «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الحَجَرِ بِحَيَوَانٍ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانُ أَنْتَجَ: لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الحَجَرِ بِإِنْسَانٍ»، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ الصُّغْرَى وَجَعَلْتَهَا كُبْرَى، ثُمَّ عَكَسْتَ النَّتِيجَةَ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا: «كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الحَيَوَانِ بِحَجَرٍ يَنْتُجُ: لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ، فَتَعْكُسُ النَّتِيجَةَ إِلَى قَوْلِنَا: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الحَجَرِ بِإِنْسَانِ، وَهُوَ المَطْلُوبُ». وَلِأَنَّ الضَّرْبَ الرَّابِعَ مُرَكَّبٌ مِنْ سَالِبَةٍ جُزْئِيَّةٍ صُغْرَى وَمُوجَبَةٍ كُلِّيَّةٍ كُبْرَى، وَلَوْ رُدَّ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ الكُبْرَى، لَكَانَ مِنْ ضُرُوبِهِ العَقِيمَةِ؛ لِمَا تَقَدَّمَ، فَلَا يُمْكِنُ رَدُّهُ بِذَلِكَ، وَإِنَّمَا يُمْكِنُ رَدُّهُ بِدَلِيلِ الافْتِرَاضِ. وَقَوْلُهُ: وَالثَّالِثُ أُرْدُدْهُ بِعَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى لَا يَظْهَرُ إِلَّا فِي أَرْبَعَةِ أَضْرُبٍ مِنْ ضُرُوبِهِ: الأَوَّلُ، وَالثَّانِي، وَالثَّالِثُ، وَالخَامِسُ. فَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الأَوَّلِ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ يَنْتُجُ: بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقٌ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ صُغْرَاهُ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا: بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلٌّ إِنْسَانٍ نَاطِقٌ يَنْتُجُ المَطْلُوبُ بِعَيْنِهِ وَهُوَ: «بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقٌ». وَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الثَّانِي: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِفَرَسٍ يَنْتُجُ: بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِفَرَسٍ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ صُغْرَاهُ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا: بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِفَرَسٍ، يَنْتُجُ المَطْلُوبُ بِعَيْنِهِ وَهُوَ: بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِفَرَسٍ. وَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الثَّالِثِ: بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ جِسْمٌ، يَنْتُجُ: بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ جِسْمٌ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ صُغْرَاهُ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا: بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانُ، وَكُلٌّ حَيَوَانٍ جِسْمٌ يَنْتُجُ المَطْلُوبُ بِعَيْنِهِ وَهُوَ: «بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ جِسْمٌ». وَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الخَامِسِ: بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانُ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الحَيَوَانِ بِجَمَادٍ، يَنْتُجُ: بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِجَمَادٍ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ صُغْرَاهُ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا: بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الحَيَوَانِ بِجَمَادٍ يَنْتُجُ المَطْلُوبُ بِعَيْنِهِ وَهُوَ: بَعْضُ الْإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِجَمَادٍ». وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ يَكُنْ ظَاهِراً فِي كُلِّ مِنَ الضَّرْبِ الرَّابِعِ وَالسَّادِسِ: لِأَنَّ الضَّرْبَ الرَّابِعَ مُرَكَّبٌ مِنْ مُوجَبَةٍ كُلِّيَّةٍ صُغْرَى وَمُوجَبَةٍ جُزْئِيَّةٍ كُبْرَى، وَلَوْ رُدَّ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ الصُّغُرَى لَكَانَ مِنْ ضُرُوبِهِ العَقِيمَةِ؛ لأَنَّ كُبْرَاهُ جُزْئِيَّةٌ، وَقَدْ تَقَدَّمَ أَنَّهُ يُشْتَرَطُ لإنتاج الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ: كُلِّيَّةُ الكُبْرَى فَلَا يُمْكِنُ رَدُّهُ بِذَلِكَ. وَإِنَّمَا يُمْكِنُ رَدُّهُ بِعَكْسِ الكُبْرَى وَجَعَلَهَا صُغْرَى، ثُمَّ عَكْسِ النَّتِيجَةِ، فَإِذَا قُلْتَ مَثَلاً: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَبَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ نَاطِقٌ أَنْتَجَ: بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقٌ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ الكُبْرَى وَجَعَلْتَهَا صُغْرَى، ثُمَّ عَكَسْتَ النَّتِيجَةَ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا: بَعْضُ النَّاطِقِ إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانُ يَنْتُجُ: بَعْضُ النَّاطِقِ حَيَوَانٌ، فَتُعْكَسُ النَّتيجَةُ إِلَى قَوْلِنَا: بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقٌ، وَهُوَ المَطْلُوبُ. وَلِأَنَّ الضَّرْبَ السَّادِسَ مُرَكَّبٌ مِنْ مُوجَبَةٍ كُلِّيَّةٍ صُغْرَى وَسَالِبَةٍ جُزْئِيَّةٍ كُبْرَى، وَلَوْ رُدَّ إِلَى الشَّكْل الأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى، لَكَانَ مِنْ ضُرُوبِهِ العَقِيمَةِ لِمَا ذُكِرَ، فَلَا يُمْكِنُ رَدُّهُ بِذَلِكَ، وَإِنَّمَا يُمْكِنُ رَدُّهُ بِدَلِيلِ الافْتِرَاضِ.
It was not obvious in each of the second and fourth moods: because the second mood is composed of a universal negative minor and a universal affirmative major, and if it were reduced to the first figure by conversion of the major, it would be among its sterile moods; because its minor is negative and its major is particular, and it has been established that for the first figure to be productive, the minor must be affirmative and the major universal, so it cannot be reduced by that. Rather, it can be reduced by converting the minor and making it the major, then converting the conclusion. So if you say, for example: 'No stone is an animal, and every human is an animal' produces 'No stone is a human'; then if you want to reduce it to the first figure, you convert the minor and make it the major, then convert the conclusion by saying: 'Every human is an animal, and no animal is a stone' produces 'No human is a stone'; then you convert the conclusion to our statement: 'No stone is a human', which is the desired conclusion. And because the fourth mood is composed of a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, and if it were reduced to the first figure by conversion of the major, it would be among its sterile moods; for the reason mentioned, so it cannot be reduced by that. Rather, it can be reduced by the proof by hypothesis. And his statement: 'The third, reduce it by conversion of the minor' is only evident in four of its moods: the first, second, third, and fifth. An example of the first mood: 'Every human is an animal, and every human is rational' produces 'Some animal is rational'. If you want to reduce it to the first figure, you convert its minor by saying: 'Some animal is a human, and every human is rational' produces the very desired conclusion, namely: 'Some animal is rational'. An example of the second mood: 'Every human is an animal, and no human is a horse' produces 'Some animal is not a horse'. If you want to reduce it to the first figure, you convert its minor by saying: 'Some animal is a human, and no human is a horse' produces the very desired conclusion, namely: 'Some animal is not a horse'. An example of the third mood: 'Some animal is a human, and every animal is a body' produces 'Some human is a body'. If you want to reduce it to the first figure, you convert its minor by saying: 'Some human is an animal, and every animal is a body' produces the very desired conclusion, namely: 'Some human is a body'. An example of the fifth mood: 'Some animal is a human, and no animal is inanimate' produces 'Some human is not inanimate'. If you want to reduce it to the first figure, you convert its minor by saying: 'Some human is an animal, and no animal is inanimate' produces the very desired conclusion, namely: 'Some human is not inanimate'. It was not obvious in each of the fourth and sixth moods: because the fourth mood is composed of a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative major, and if it were reduced to the first figure by conversion of the minor, it would be among its sterile moods; because its major is particular, and it has been established that for the first figure to be productive, the major must be universal, so it cannot be reduced by that. Rather, it can be reduced by converting the major and making it the minor, then converting the conclusion. So if you say, for example: 'Every human is an animal, and some human is rational' produces 'Some animal is rational'. If you want to reduce it to the first figure, you convert the major and make it the minor, then convert the conclusion by saying: 'Some rational is human, and every human is animal' produces 'Some rational is animal'; then you convert the conclusion to our statement: 'Some animal is rational', which is the desired conclusion. And because the sixth mood is composed of a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major, and if it were reduced to the first figure by conversion of the minor, it would be among its sterile moods for the reason mentioned, so it cannot be reduced by that. Rather, it can be reduced by the proof by hypothesis.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the reduction of syllogisms in the third figure to the first figure. The author first explains why the second and fourth moods of the third figure are not obviously reducible by simple conversion of the major premise. For the second mood (universal negative minor, universal affirmative major), if we try to convert the major, the resulting first-figure syllogism would have a negative minor and a particular major — violating the conditions for productivity. Instead, we must convert the minor, make it the major, then convert the conclusion. An example is given using stone, animal, and human. For the fourth mood (particular negative minor, universal affirmative major), the same problem occurs; reduction requires the proof by hypothesis (dalil al-iftirad). The author then cites the rule that the third figure reduces by conversion of the minor only in four of its six moods: first, second, third, and fifth. He provides detailed examples for each: first mood (Every human is animal, every human is rational → Some animal is rational) reduces by converting the minor to 'Some animal is human' and keeping the major; second mood (Every human is animal, no human is horse → Some animal is not horse) reduces by converting the minor; third mood (Some animal is human, every animal is body → Some human is body) reduces by converting the minor to 'Some human is animal'; fifth mood (Some animal is human, no animal is inanimate → Some human is not inanimate) reduces by converting the minor. The author then explains why the fourth and sixth moods do not reduce by converting the minor. The fourth mood (universal affirmative minor, particular affirmative major) has a particular major, so converting the minor still gives a particular major, violating the requirement that the major be universal. Instead, we convert the major (making it the minor) and then convert the conclusion. An example with human, animal, rational illustrates this. The sixth mood (universal affirmative minor, particular negative major) similarly cannot be reduced by converting the minor; it requires the proof by hypothesis. This demonstrates the meticulous care logicians take to ensure all productive moods are reducible to the first figure, the standard of demonstration.

عكس الكبرى
دليل الافتراض

Checkpoint
Which moods of the third figure can be directly reduced to the first figure by converting the minor premise?
A) First, second, third, and fourth
B) First, second, third, and fifth
C) First, third, fourth, and sixth
D) All six moods

Key Takeaway
The third figure reduces to the first by converting the minor in four moods; the fourth and sixth need conversion of the major or proof by hypothesis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the third-figure syllogism: 'Every created thing is contingent, some created thing is the world → some contingent is the world.' Determine whether it reduces by converting the minor, and perform the reduction to the first figure. If not, explain why and suggest an alternative method.
?
Examine the mood of the third figure used by a theologian: 'No soul is eternal, every soul is created → some created is not eternal.' Which reduction method applies? Carry out the reduction step by step.

The Syllogism
intermediate
15 min
Reduction of the Six Modes of the Fourth Figure to the First
Author explains reduction of each of the six fourth-figure modes to the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce any valid fourth-figure syllogism to an equivalent first-figure syllogism by selecting and applying the appropriate conversion or order reversal technique.

Core Idea
Every fourth-figure mood can be reduced to the first figure, but the method depends on the mood's structure.

The fourth figure may seem distinct, but logicians proved that its valid moods are all reducible to the first figure. This reduction is not merely an exercise—it demonstrates that all valid syllogisms ultimately depend on the self-evident principles of the first figure. In this lesson, we follow al-Bajuri's step-by-step explanation of how each of the six fourth-figure moods is transformed, using conversion (aks), order reversal (aks al-tartib), and proof by supposition (dalil al-iftirad).

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood and its premises
For a given fourth-figure syllogism, determine the minor premise (contains the subject of the conclusion) and major premise (contains the predicate). Note the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of each.
The fourth figure has the middle term as predicate of the minor and subject of the major.
2
Select the reduction method based on the major premise
If the major is universal, reduction can be done by converting the minor or reversing order. If the major is particular, the major must be converted (and sometimes the conclusion converted) or proof by supposition used for particular negative majors.
Al-Bajuri classifies the methods: 'aks al-tartib' for the first three modes, and 'aks al-muqaddimat' for the fourth and fifth modes.
3
Apply the conversion or reversal
Perform the required conversion(s) following the rules of aks: universal affirmative → particular affirmative, universal negative → universal negative, particular affirmative → particular affirmative. For order reversal, swap the premises and then convert the conclusion.
Be careful: particular negative cannot be converted, hence the need for dalil al-iftirad in the sixth mode.
4
Verify the resulting syllogism is in the first figure
Check that in the reduced syllogism, the middle term is subject of the major and predicate of the minor. Also ensure the major premise is universal and the minor affirmative (for productive first-figure moods).
The first figure requires universal major and affirmative minor for direct production.
5
Confirm the conclusion matches the original
The reduced syllogism's conclusion may need further conversion to exactly match the original conclusion. For instance, from universal to particular via conversion.
Al-Bajuri often concludes with 'the very sought result' (al-matluub bi-aynihi) after these steps.

فصل في الأشكال (٩٩) وَتَنْتَهِي إِلَى ضَرُورَةٍ لِمَا مِنْ دَوْرٍ أَوْ تَسَلْسُل قَدْ لَزِما ; الباجوري الْحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِفَرَسٍ، يَنْتُجُ الْمَطْلُوبُ بِعَيْنِهِ وَهُوَ : بَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِفَرَسٍ. - وَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الثَّالِثِ : بَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ جِسْمٌ، يَنْتُجُ : بَعْضُ الْإِنْسَانِ جِسْمٌ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ صُغْرَاهُ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا : بَعْضُ الْإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانُ، وَكُلٌّ حَيَوَانٍ جِسْمٌ يَنْتُجُ الْمَطْلُوبُ بِعَيْنِهِ وَهُوَ: «بَعْضُ الْإِنْسَانِ جِسْمٌ». - وَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الْخَامِسِ : بَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانُ، وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْحَيَوَانِ بِجَمَادٍ، يَنْتُجُ : بَعْضُ الْإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِجَمَادٍ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ صُغْرَاهُ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا : بَعْضُ الْإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْحَيَوَانِ بِجَمَادٍ يَنْتُجُ الْمَطْلُوبُ بِعَيْنِهِ وَهُوَ: بَعْضُ الْإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِجَمَادٍ» . وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ يَكُنْ ظَاهِراً فِي كُلِّ مِنَ الضَّرْبِ الرَّابِعِ وَالسَّادِسِ : - لِأَنَّ الضَّرْبَ الرَّابِعَ مُرَكَّبٌ مِنْ مُوجَبَةٍ كُلِّيَّةٍ صُغْرَى وَمُوجَبَةٍ جُزْئِيَّةٍ كُبْرَى، وَلَوْ رُدَّ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى لَكَانَ مِنْ ضُرُوبِهِ الْعَقِيمَةِ ; لِأَنَّ كُبْرَاهُ جُزْئِيَّةٌ ، وَقَدْ تَقَدَّمَ أَنَّهُ يُشْتَرَطُ لِإِنْتَاجِ الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ : كُلِّيَّةُ الْكُبْرَى» فَلَا يُمْكِنُ رَدُّهُ بِذَلِكَ وَإِنَّمَا يُمْكِنُ رَدُّهُ بِعَكْسِ الْكُبْرَى وَجَعَلَهَا صُغْرَى ، ثُمَّ عَكْسِ النَّتِيجَةِ، فَإِذَا قُلْتَ مَثَلاً : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَبَعْضُ الْإِنْسَانِ نَاطِقٌ أَنْتَجَ : بَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقٌ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ الْكُبْرَى وَجَعَلْتَهَا صُغْرَى ، ثُمَّ عَكَسْتَ النَّتِيجَةَ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا : بَعْضُ النَّاطِقِ إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانُ يَنْتُجُ : بَعْضُ النَّاطِقِ حَيَوَانٌ، فَتُعْكَسُ النَّتِيجَةُ إِلَى قَوْلِنَا : بَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقٌ، وَهُوَ الْمَطْلُوبُ. - وَلِأَنَّ الضَّرْبَ السَّادِسَ مُرَكَّبٌ مِنْ مُوجَبَةٍ كُلِّيَّةٍ صُغْرَى وَسَالِبَةٍ جُزْئِيَّةٍ كُبْرَى، وَلَوْ رُدَّ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى، لَكَانَ مِنْ ضُرُوبِهِ الْعَقِيمَةِ لِمَا ذُكِرَ ، فَلَا يُمْكِنُ رَدُّهُ بِذَلِكَ، وَإِنَّمَا يُمْكِنُ رَدُّهُ بِدَلِيلِ الِافْتِرَاضِ، وَقَدْ ذَكَرَهُ الْإِمَامُ السَّنُوسِيُّ فِي شَرْحِ مُخْتَصَرِهِ بِمَا لَا يُنَاسِبُ ذِكْرُهُ هُنَا . وَقَوْلُهُ: «وَرَابِعُ ... إلخ» عَلَى التَّوْزِيعِ ; لِأَنَّ الشَّقَّ الْأَوَّلَ - أَعْنِي : «عَكْسَ التَّرْتِيبِ» - بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِثَلَاثَةِ أَضْرُبٍ مِنْ ضُرُوبِهِ : الْأَوَّلُ، وَالثَّانِي، وَالثَّالِثُ. - فَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الْأَوَّلِ أَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ نَاطِقٍ إِنْسَانٌ يَنْتُجُ : بَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقٌ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ تَرْتِيبَهُ ، ثُمَّ عَكَسْتَ النَّتِيجَةَ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا : كُلُّ نَاطِقٍ إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ يَنْتُجُ : كُلُّ نَاطِقٍ حَيَوَانٌ» ، فَتُعْكَسُ النَّتِيجَةُ إِلَى قَوْلِنَا : بَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقٌ ، وَهُوَ الْمَطْلُوبُ . - وَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الثَّانِي أَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَبَعْضُ النَّاطِقِ إِنْسَانُ يَنْتُجُ : بَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقٌ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ تَرْتِيبَهُ، ثُمَّ عَكَسْتَ النَّتِيجَةَ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا : بَعْضُ النَّاطِقِ إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ يَنْتُجُ : بَعْضُ النَّاطِقِ حَيَوَانٌ»، فَتُعْكَسُ النَّتِيجَةُ إِلَى قَوْلِنَا : بَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقُ، وَهُوَ الْمَطْلُوبُ . - وَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الثَّالِثِ أَنْ تَقُولَ: «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِجَمَادٍ، وَكُلُّ نَاطِقٍ إِنْسَانٌ يَنْتُجُ : لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْجَمَادِ بِنَاطِقٍ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ تَرْتِيبَهُ، ثُمَّ عَكَسْتَ النَّتِيجَةَ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا كُلُّ نَاطِقٍ إِنْسَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِجَمَادٍ يَنْتُجُ : لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ النَّاطِقِ بِجَمَادٍ ، فَتُعْكَسُ النَّتِيجَةُ إِلَى قَوْلِنَا : لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْجَمَادِ بِنَاطِقٍ، وَهُوَ الْمَطْلُوبُ . وَالشَّقُّ الثَّانِي - أَعْنِي: عَكْسَ الْمُقَدِّمَاتِ - : بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِلضَّرْبِ الرَّابِعِ وَالْخَامِسِ . - فَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الرَّابِعِ أَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْفَرَسِ بِإِنْسَانٍ» يَنْتُجُ : بَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِفَرَسٍ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا : بَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِفَرَسٍ يَنْتُجُ الْمَطْلُوبُ بِعَيْنِهِ وَهُوَ : بَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِفَرَسٍ. - وَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الْخَامِسِ أَنْ تَقُولَ: بَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْجَمَادِ بِحَيَوَانِ يَنْتُجُ : بَعْضُ الْإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِجَمَادٍ ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الْأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا بَعْضُ الْإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْحَيَوَانِ بِجَمَادٍ يَنْتُجُ الْمَطْلُوبُ بِعَيْنِهِ وَهُوَ : بَعْضُ الْإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِجَمَادٍ، وَهَذَا مَا يَسَّرَهُ اللهُ تَعَالَى عَلَى هَذِهِ الْأَبْيَاتِ، فَاحْفَظْهُ .
Chapter on the Figures. (99) And they end in necessity due to what has necessarily followed of circularity or infinite regress. al-Bajuri: '... animal is man, and nothing of man is horse,' produces the very sought result: 'Some animal is not a horse.' - Example of the third mode: 'Some animal is man, and every animal is body,' produces 'Some man is body.' If you want to reduce it to the first figure, convert its minor premise by saying thus: 'Some man is animal, and every animal is body' produces the very sought result: 'Some man is body.' - Example of the fifth mode: 'Some animal is man, and nothing of animal is inanimate,' produces 'Some man is not inanimate.' If you want to reduce it to the first figure, convert its minor premise by saying thus: 'Some man is animal, and nothing of animal is inanimate' produces the very sought result: 'Some man is not inanimate.' And the reason it was not apparent in each of the fourth and sixth modes: - Because the fourth mode is composed of a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative major, and if reduced to the first figure by conversion of the minor, it would be among its sterile modes because its major is particular, and it has preceded that for production of the first figure it is required that the major be universal. So it is not possible to reduce it by that. Rather it is possible to reduce it by converting the major and making it the minor, then converting the conclusion. For example, if you say: 'Every man is animal, and some man is rational' produces 'Some animal is rational.' Then if you want to reduce it to the first figure, convert the major and make it the minor, then convert the conclusion by saying thus: 'Some rational is man, and every man is animal' produces 'Some rational is animal.' Then the conclusion is converted to our statement: 'Some animal is rational,' which is the sought. - And because the sixth mode is composed of a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major, and if reduced to the first figure by conversion of the minor, it would be among its sterile modes for what was mentioned. So it is not possible to reduce it by that. Rather it is possible to reduce it by the proof of supposition (dalil al-iftirad), and Imam al-Sanusi mentioned it in his commentary on his Mukhtasar in a way that is not appropriate to mention here. And his statement: 'And fourth... etc.' is distributive; because the first branch - I mean 'reversal of order' - is for three of its modes: the first, second, and third. - Example of the first mode: you say: 'Every man is animal, and every rational is man' produces 'Some animal is rational.' If you want to reduce it to the first figure, reverse its order, then convert the conclusion by saying thus: 'Every rational is man, and every man is animal' produces 'Every rational is animal.' Then the conclusion is converted to our statement: 'Some animal is rational,' which is the sought. - Example of the second mode: you say: 'Every man is animal, and some rational is man' produces 'Some animal is rational.' If you want to reduce it to the first figure, reverse its order, then convert the conclusion by saying thus: 'Some rational is man, and every man is animal' produces 'Some rational is animal.' Then the conclusion is converted to our statement: 'Some animal is rational,' which is the sought. - Example of the third mode: you say: 'Nothing of man is inanimate, and every rational is man' produces 'Nothing of inanimate is rational.' If you want to reduce it to the first figure, reverse its order, then convert the conclusion by saying thus: 'Every rational is man, and nothing of man is inanimate' produces 'Nothing of rational is inanimate.' Then the conclusion is converted to our statement: 'Nothing of inanimate is rational,' which is the sought. And the second branch - I mean: conversion of the premises - is for the fourth and fifth modes. - Example of the fourth mode: you say: 'Every man is animal, and nothing of horse is man' produces 'Some animal is not horse.' If you want to reduce it to the first figure, convert both its premises by saying thus: 'Some animal is man, and nothing of man is horse' produces the very sought result: 'Some animal is not horse.' - Example of the fifth mode: you say: 'Some animal is man, and nothing of inanimate is animal' produces 'Some man is not inanimate.' If you want to reduce it to the first figure, convert both its premises by saying thus: 'Some man is animal, and nothing of animal is inanimate' produces the very sought result: 'Some man is not inanimate.' And this is what Allah the Exalted facilitated on these verses, so memorize it.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) by al-Bajuri on a matn line about the necessity of avoiding circularity and infinite regress, but the bulk of the text is explaining how to reduce the six modes (ضروب) of the fourth figure (الشكل الرابع) to the first figure (الشكل الأول). The author first gives examples of modes that can be reduced by converting the minor premise: the third and fifth modes. Then he explains why the fourth and sixth modes are not reducible in that way: the fourth mode has a particular major premise, violating the first figure's requirement that the major be universal; the sixth mode has a particular negative major, likewise problematic. For the fourth mode, al-Bajuri shows reduction by converting the major premise and then converting the conclusion. For the sixth mode, he mentions that reduction is possible via 'dalil al-iftirad' (proof by supposition), but he does not elaborate, referring instead to al-Sanusi's commentary. He then introduces two general methods: 'عكس الترتيب' (reversal of order) for the first three modes, and 'عكس المقدمات' (conversion of premises) for the fourth and fifth modes. He provides detailed examples for each mode, demonstrating the step-by-step reduction. The passage ends with an exhortation to memorize.

Key Terms
ضرب (darb) – mode
رد (radd) – reduction
عكس (aks) – conversion
صغرى (sughra) – minor premise
كبرى (kubra) – major premise
دليل الافتراض (dalil al-iftirad) – proof by supposition

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which mode of the fourth figure requires converting the major premise and then converting the conclusion to reduce it to the first figure?
A) The first mode
B) The second mode
C) The third mode
D) The fourth mode

Key Takeaway
The reduction of fourth-figure modes to the first figure is accomplished either by converting premises, reversing order, or using proof by supposition, depending on the mood's quantity and quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following fourth-figure syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden, and some drink is an intoxicant.' Identify its mood, determine the appropriate reduction method, and perform the reduction to the first figure, showing each step and the final conclusion.
?
Given a fourth-figure syllogism with a particular negative major (e.g., 'Every scholar is knowledgeable, and some being is not knowledgeable'), explain why converting the minor premise fails and outline how dalil al-iftirad would be applied to reduce it (without carrying out the full supposition).

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Reducing the First Three Moods of the Second Figure by Inverting Order
Author demonstrates reducing first three moods of second figure using عكس الترتيب.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce any of the first three moods of the second figure to the first figure by inverting the order of premises and converting the conclusion.

Core Idea
Inverting the order of premises transforms a second-figure syllogism into a valid first-figure syllogism.

In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises, making it less intuitive than the first figure. However, by simply swapping the premises (عكس الترتيب) and then converting the conclusion, we can map any of the first three moods to a perfect first-figure syllogism. This lesson will walk you through the process for the first, second, and third moods using examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood
Determine if the syllogism is the first, second, or third mood of the second figure. The first mood has universal affirmative major and universal affirmative minor; the second has universal affirmative major and particular affirmative minor; the third has universal negative major and universal affirmative minor.
2
Invert the order of premises
Swap the major and minor premises so that the minor premise becomes the major and vice versa. This creates a first-figure syllogism where the middle term becomes the subject of the first premise and predicate of the second.
For example, 'Every human is animal' and 'Every rational is human' become 'Every rational is human' and 'Every human is animal'.
3
Find the direct conclusion of the new syllogism
The first-figure syllogism will yield a different conclusion from the original. For the first mood, it yields 'Every rational is animal'; for the second, 'Some rational is animal'; for the third, 'Nothing of rational is inanimate'.
4
Convert the conclusion
Use simple conversion (عكس) to turn the new conclusion into the original desired conclusion. Universal affirmative becomes particular affirmative; universal negative stays the same. For the first mood, 'Every rational is animal' converts to 'Some animal is rational'; for the third, 'Nothing of rational is inanimate' remains 'Nothing of inanimate is rational'.
This matches the original conclusion.

وَتَنْتَهِي إِلَى ضَرُورَةٍ لِمَا مِنْ دَوْرِ أَوْ تَسَلْسُلِ قَدْ لَزِمِ. فَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الأَوَّلِ أَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ نَاطِقٍ إِنْسَانٌ يَنْتُجُ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقٌ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ تَرْتِيبَهُ ، ثُمَّ عَكَسْتَ النَّتِيجَةَ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا : كُلُّ نَاطِقٍ إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ يَنْتُجُ : كُلُّ نَاطِقٍ حَيَوَانٌ» ، فَتُعْكَسُ النَّتِيجَةُ إِلَى قَوْلِنَا : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقٌ ، وَهُوَ المَطْلُوبُ . وَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الثَّانِي أَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَبَعْضُ النَّاطِقِ إِنْسَانُ يَنْتُجُ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقٌ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ تَرْتِيبَهُ، ثُمَّ عَكَسْتَ النَّتِيجَةَ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا : بَعْضُ النَّاطِقِ إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ يَنْتُجُ : بَعْضُ النَّاطِقِ حَيَوَانٌ»، فَتُعْكَسُ النَّتِيجَةُ إِلَى قَوْلِنَا : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ نَاطِقُ، وَهُوَ المَطْلُوبُ . وَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الثَّالِثِ أَنْ تَقُولَ: «لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِجَمَادٍ، وَكُلُّ نَاطِقٍ إِنْسَانٌ يَنْتُجُ : لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الجَمَادِ بِنَاطِقٍ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ تَرْتِيبَهُ، ثُمَّ عَكَسْتَ النَّتِيجَةَ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا كُلُّ نَاطِقٍ إِنْسَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِجَمَادٍ يَنْتُجُ : لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ النَّاطِقِ بِجَمَادٍ ، فَتُعْكَسُ النَّتيجَةُ إِلَى قَوْلِنَا : لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الجَمَادِ بِنَاطِقٍ، وَهُوَ المَطْلُوبُ . وَالشَّقُ الثَّانِي - أَعْنِي: عَكْسَ المُقَدِّمَاتِ - : بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِلضَّرْبِ الرَّابِعِ وَالخَامِسِ . فَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الرَّابِعِ أَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الفَرَسِ بِإِنْسَانٍ» يَنْتُجُ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِفَرَسٍ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِفَرَسٍ يَنْتُجُ المَطْلُوبُ بِعَيْنِهِ وَهُوَ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ فَرَسٍ. وَمِثَالُ الضَّرْبِ الخَامِسِ أَنْ تَقُولَ: بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الجَمَادِ بِحَيَوَانِ يَنْتُجُ : بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِجَمَادٍ ، فَإِذَا أَرَدْتَ رَدَّهُ إِلَى الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ عَكَسْتَ مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ بِأَنْ تَقُولَ هَكَذَا بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ، وَلَا شَيْء مِنَ الحَيَوَانِ بِجَمَادٍ يَنْتُجُ المَعْلُوبُ بِعَيْنِهِ وَهُوَ : بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ لَيْسَ بِجَمَادٍ، وَهَذَا مَا يَسَّرَهُ اللهُ تَعَالَى عَلَى هَذِهِ الأَبْيَاتِ، فَاحْفَظْهُ .
And it ends in necessity, due to a cycle or an infinite regress that has occurred. So the example of the first mode is that you say: Every human is an animal, and every rational is a human, yields: Some animal is rational. So when you want to reduce it to the first figure, you invert its order, then invert the result by saying thus: Every rational is a human, and every human is an animal, yields: Every rational is an animal. Then the result is inverted to our saying: Some animal is rational, and that is the desired. And the example of the second mode is that you say: Every human is an animal, and some rational is a human, yields: Some animal is rational. So when you want to reduce it to the first figure, you invert its order, then invert the result by saying thus: Some rational is a human, and every human is an animal, yields: Some rational is an animal. Then the result is inverted to our saying: Some animal is rational, and that is the desired. And the example of the third mode is that you say: Nothing of the human is an inanimate, and every rational is a human, yields: Nothing of the inanimate is rational. So when you want to reduce it to the first figure, you invert its order, then invert the result by saying thus: Every rational is a human, and nothing of the human is an inanimate, yields: Nothing of the rational is an inanimate. Then the result is inverted to our saying: Nothing of the inanimate is rational, and that is the desired. And the second part — I mean: inverting the premises — applies to the fourth and fifth modes. So the example of the fourth mode is that you say: Every human is an animal, and nothing of the horse is a human, yields: Some animal is not a horse. So when you want to reduce it to the first figure, you invert both its premises by saying thus: Some animal is a human, and nothing of the human is a horse, yields the very desired, which is: Some animal is not a horse. And the example of the fifth mode is that you say: Some animal is a human, and nothing of the inanimate is an animal, yields: Some human is not an inanimate. So when you want to reduce it to the first figure, you invert both its premises by saying thus: Some human is an animal, and nothing of the animal is an inanimate, yields the very desired, which is: Some human is not an inanimate. And this is what Allah the Exalted facilitated upon these verses, so memorize it.

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the second figure of the categorical syllogism, specifically how to reduce its five moods to the first figure. The author explains two methods: inverting the order of premises (عكس الترتيب) and inverting the premises themselves (عكس المقدمات). The first three moods (first, second, third) are reduced by inverting the order, and then the conclusion is also inverted (converted) to match the original desired conclusion. For example, the first mood originally is 'Every A is B, every C is A, therefore some B is C'. By inverting order, we get 'Every C is A, every A is B, therefore every C is B', then converting the conclusion yields 'some B is C'. The second and third moods follow similarly. The fourth and fifth moods require inverting the premises themselves (i.e., converting each premise via simple conversion) to produce a first figure syllogism that yields the desired conclusion directly without further inversion. All examples use animals, humans, rational, horse, inanimate — terms from the Islamic sciences (e.g., human vs. animal). The author emphasizes memorization, indicating this is a foundational skill.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Reducing the Fourth and Fifth Moods of the Second Figure by Inverting Premises
Author demonstrates reducing fourth and fifth moods of second figure using عكس المقدمات.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce the fourth and fifth moods of the second figure to the first figure by inverting (converting) both premises.

Core Idea
Inverting the premises (عكس المقدمات) directly yields the desired conclusion without needing to convert the result.

The fourth and fifth moods of the second figure have a particular negative conclusion. Unlike the first three moods, simply swapping premises does not lead to a valid first-figure syllogism. Instead, we must convert each premise individually (عكس المقدمات) to obtain a first-figure syllogism that directly produces the desired conclusion. This lesson will guide you through the process using examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood
Determine if the syllogism is the fourth or fifth mood. The fourth mood has universal affirmative major and universal negative minor; the fifth has particular affirmative major and universal negative minor.
2
Invert both premises
Convert each premise using simple conversion: universal affirmative becomes particular affirmative; universal negative stays the same; particular affirmative stays particular affirmative. For the fourth mood: 'Every human is animal' becomes 'Some animal is human'; 'Nothing of horse is human' becomes 'Nothing of human is horse'. For the fifth mood: 'Some animal is human' becomes 'Some human is animal'; 'Nothing of inanimate is animal' becomes 'Nothing of animal is inanimate'.
The order of premises remains the same.
3
Construct a new first-figure syllogism
Use the converted premises in the same order. The middle term becomes the subject of the first premise and predicate of the second. For the fourth mood: 'Some animal is human' and 'Nothing of human is horse' is first figure. For the fifth mood: 'Some human is animal' and 'Nothing of animal is inanimate' is first figure.
4
Extract the conclusion
The first-figure syllogism yields the original desired conclusion directly, without further conversion. For the fourth mood, it gives 'Some animal is not a horse'; for the fifth, 'Some human is not inanimate'.
This method avoids the extra conversion step needed in the first three moods.

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the second figure of the categorical syllogism, specifically how to reduce its five moods to the first figure. The author explains two methods: inverting the order of premises (عكس الترتيب) and inverting the premises themselves (عكس المقدمات). The first three moods (first, second, third) are reduced by inverting the order, and then the conclusion is also inverted (converted) to match the original desired conclusion. The Conditional (Exceptive) Syllogism: Structure and Naming
Commentator defines conditional syllogism structure and naming.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two premises of a conditional (exceptive) syllogism and explain why the conditional is called 'major' and the exceptive 'minor'.

Core Idea
The exceptive syllogism links a conditional major and an exceptive minor; its name highlights the exceptive premise.

The conditional syllogism, also called the exceptive syllogism, is a fundamental argument form in Islamic logic. This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary introduces its composition, the naming of its premises, and how it relates to the categorical syllogism. We will dissect this definition to understand its logical structure and the reasoning behind the technical terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Locate in the Arabic passage where the commentator states that the exceptive syllogism is composed of two premises: a conditional premise (shartiyya) called the 'major' (kubra) and an exceptive premise (istithna'iyya) called the 'minor' (sughra).
The conditional premise is a conditional statement (e.g., 'If...then...'), while the exceptive premise affirms or denies one part of that conditional.
2
Understand the naming rationale
The commentator gives two reasons: (1) the exceptive premise has about half the wording of the conditional, and (2) when the syllogism is rearranged into the first figure of the conjunctive syllogism, the exceptive functions as the minor premise. Find these reasons in the text.
3
See the equivalence to categorical form
The commentator shows that the conditional syllogism 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal; but it is a human' is equivalent to the categorical syllogism 'This is a human; and everything that is human is an animal'. Observe how the same conclusion is reached.
This demonstrates that conditional reasoning can be reduced to categorical form, a key insight in Islamic logic.
4
Apply the analysis to an Islamic example
Construct a conditional syllogism using an example from Islamic law: 'If the prayer is invalid without purification, then purification is obligatory. The prayer is invalid without purification, therefore purification is obligatory.' Identify the major and minor premises.
Use this as a template for your own examples from fiqh or kalam.

فَصْلٌ فِي الاسْتِثْنَائِي . وَمِنْهُ مَا يُدْعَى بِـ : الِاسْتِثْنَائِي يُعْرَفُ بِـ: «الشَّرْطِي بِلَا امْتِرَاءِ . فَصْلٌ فِي الاسْتِثْنَائِي [ قَوْلُهُ : ] (فَصْلٌ فِي الاسْتِثْنَائِي لَعَلَّ المُصَنِّفَ تَرْجَمَ بِهَذِهِ التَّرْجَمَةِ لِطُولِ العَهْدِ، وَإِلَّا فَكَانَ مُقْتَضَى الظَّاهِرِ : أَنَّهُ لَا حَاجَةَ إِلَيْهَا ؛ لِأَنَّ قَوْلَهُ: «وَمِنْهُ ... إلخ» مَعْطُوفٌ عَلَى قَوْلِهِ : «فَمِنْهُ ... إلخ» كَمَا لَا يَخْفَى . وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ الِاسْتِثْنَائِيَّ مُؤَلَّفٌ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ : (۱) - إِحْدَاهُمَا : شَرْطِيَّةٌ، وَتُسَمَّى : «كُبْرَى». (٢) - وَالأُخْرَى : اسْتِثْنَائِيَّةٌ ، وَتُسَمَّى : صُغُرَى). - وَلِذَلِكَ يُسَمَّى بِاسْمَيْنِ كَمَا سَيَذْكُرُهُ المُصَنِّفُ؛ فَالأَوَّلُ هُوَ (۱) الِاسْتِثْنَائِيُّ ؛ لِاشْتِمَالِهِ عَلَى الاسْتِثْنَائِيَّةِ ، وَالثَّانِي هُوَ (٢) الشَّرْطِيُّ ؛ لِاشْتِمَالِهِ عَلَى الشَّرْطِيَّةِ. وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتِ الشَّرْطِيَّةُ : كُبْرَى، وَالاسْتِثْنَانِيَّةُ : صُغُرَى»؛ لِأَنَّ أَلْفَاظَ الِاسْتِثْنَانِيَّةِ عَلَى نَحْوِ النِّصْفِ مِنْ أَلْفَاظِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ ، وَأَيْضاً : لَوِ اعْتَبَرْتُهُمَا بِالتَّرْتِيبِ الاِقْتِرَانِيِّ ؛ بِأَنْ جَعَلْتَهُمَا عَلَى هَيْئَةِ الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ المُرَكَّب مِنْ حَمْلِيَّةٍ وَشَرْطِيَّةٍ ، لَوَجَدْتَ فِيهِ الاسْتِثْنَائِيَّةَ صُغْرَى وَالشَّرْطِيَّةَ كُبْرَى، فَإِذَا قُلْتَ مَثَلاً : كُلَّمَا كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً فَهُوَ حَيَوَانٌ ، لَكِنَّهُ إِنْسَانٌ وَجَدْتَهُ فِي قُوَّةِ قَوْلِكَ : هَذَا إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ مَا كَانَ إِنْسَاناً فَهُوَ حَيَوَانُ»، وَنَتِيجَتُهُ عَيْنُ نَتِيجَتِهِ، وَلَا يَخْتَلِفَانِ إِلَّا فِي تَقْدِيمِ الصُّغْرَى وَتَأْخِيرِهَا فِي اللَّفْظِ ؛ أَفَادَهُ المَلوِيُّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَمِنْهُ مَا يُدْعَى أَيْ: مِنَ القِيَاسِ مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ مَا يُسَمَّى بِالِاسْتِثْنَائِيِّ ؛ لِمَا تَقَدَّمَ مِن اشْتِمَالِهِ عَلَى الاسْتِثْنَائِيَّةِ المَذْكُورِ فِيهَا أَدَاةُ الاِسْتِثْنَاءِ، وَهِيَ : لَكِنْ ، وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ أَدَاةُ اسْتِثْنَاء .
Section on the Exceptive [Syllogism]. And among it is what is called the exceptive, known as the conditional without doubt. Section on the Exceptive [Syllogism] [His saying:] (Section on the Exceptive) Perhaps the author titled with this title due to long lapse of time; otherwise, the apparent implication is that there is no need for it, because his saying 'And among it...' etc. is conjoined to his saying 'And among it...' etc., as is not hidden. And know that the exceptive syllogism is composed of two premises: (1) one of them is conditional, and is called 'major', (2) and the other is exceptive, and is called 'minor'. Therefore it is called by two names as the author will mention: the first is the exceptive for including the exceptive premise, and the second is the conditional for including the conditional premise. And it is only that the conditional is called major and the exceptive is called minor because the words of the exceptive are about half the words of the conditional, and also if you consider them according to the conjunctive order by making them in the form of the first figure composed of a categorical and a conditional, you would find the exceptive as minor and the conditional as major. For example, if you say: Whenever this is a human, it is an animal, but it is human, you find it in the power of your saying: This is a human, and everything that is human is an animal, and its conclusion is the same as its conclusion, and they do not differ except in the presentation and delay of the minor in wording; this is what al-Malwi mentioned in his large [commentary]. His saying: (And among it what is called) i.e., from the syllogism insofar as it is what is called the exceptive, due to what preceded of its inclusion of the exceptive premise in which the exceptive tool is mentioned, and it is 'lakinna'; and it is only called the tool of exception.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage introduces the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i), also called the exceptive syllogism. The commentator Bajuri begins by noting that the author's section heading may be redundant because the words 'wa minhu' (and among it) already indicate continuity, but he acknowledges the author's choice. He then defines the structure: the conditional syllogism consists of two premises — a conditional premise (shartiyya) called the major (kubra), and an exceptive premise (istithna'iyya) called the minor (sughra). The reason for these names is twofold: first, the exceptive premise has about half the words of the conditional; second, when arranged in first figure form with a categorical and conditional, the exceptive functions as the minor. He illustrates with a classic example from the Islamic tradition: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal; but it is a human', which is equivalent to the categorical syllogism 'This is a human, and everything human is an animal', with the same conclusion. The only difference is word order. This shows the inherent connection between conditional and categorical reasoning. The commentator also explains the naming: the syllogism is called 'istithna'i' due to the exceptive premise (which uses the exception tool 'lakinna'), and 'sharti' due to the conditional premise.

Checkpoint
In an exceptive syllogism, why is the conditional premise called 'major' (kubra) and the exceptive premise 'minor' (sughra)?
A) Because the conditional premise is always longer in wording
B) Because the exceptive premise is about half the wording of the conditional and when arranged in first figure it becomes the minor
C) Because the conditional premise is more important logically
D) Because the exceptive premise is always negative

Key Takeaway
A conditional (exceptive) syllogism consists of a conditional major premise and an exceptive minor premise; its name derives from the exceptive premise, and it can be transformed into a categorical first-figure syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid conditional syllogism using an Islamic legal example (e.g., from fiqh or usul al-fiqh). Identify the conditional premise (major) and the exceptive premise (minor).
?
Take the example given in the passage: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal; but it is a human.' Convert it into a categorical first-figure syllogism and verify that the conclusion is the same.

The Syllogism
intermediate
6 min
Structure and Naming of the Conditional Syllogism (Qiyās Istithnā'ī)
The commentator explains the composition and naming of the istithnā'ī syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two premises of a conditional syllogism and explain why it is called both istithnā'ī and shartī.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism uses a conditional major and an exceptive minor to give a direct conclusion.

Before you can apply a conditional syllogism, you need to recognize its components. This lesson clarifies what the major and minor premises are in this type of syllogism, and why it has two names. The commentator also draws an analogy with the first figure of the categorical syllogism to help you understand the relationship between the premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
The commentator states that the istithnā'ī syllogism consists of two premises: one conditional (sharṭiyya) called the major, and one exceptive (istithnā'iyya) called the minor. Note that the exceptive premise is the one that uses an exceptive particle like 'lākin' (but).
2
Understand the names
The syllogism is called istithnā'ī because it includes the exceptive premise, and shartī because it includes the conditional premise. Both names refer to the same type of syllogism.
3
Compare with categorical first figure
The commentator explains that if you arrange the premises in the form of a first-figure categorical syllogism (with a categorical minor and a conditional major), the exceptive premise corresponds to the minor and the conditional to the major. They differ only in the order of words.
This analogy helps bridge your understanding from categorical to conditional syllogisms.
4
Learn why categorical is not called conditional
The categorical syllogism can also include a conditional premise, but it is not called shartī because it does not necessary do so; the majority of categoricals are purely categorical.
This clarifies the distinction.

فَصْلٌ فِي الاسْتِثْنَائِي
(١٠٠) وَمِنْهُ مَا يُدْعَى بِـ : الِاسْتِثْنَائِي يُعْرَفُ بِـ: «الشَّرْطِيْ بِلَا امْتِرَاءِ
[ قَوْلُهُ : ] (فَصْلٌ فِي الاسْتِثْنَائِي لَعَلَّ المُصَنِّفَ تَرْجَمَ بِهَذِهِ التَّرْجَمَةِ لِطُولِ العَهْدِ، وَإِلَّا فَكَانَ مُقْتَضَى الظَّاهِرِ : أَنَّهُ لَا حَاجَةَ إِلَيْهَا ؛ لِأَنَّ قَوْلَهُ: «وَمِنْهُ ... إلخ» مَعْطُوفٌ عَلَى قَوْلِهِ : «فَمِنْهُ ... إلخ» كَمَا لَا يَخْفَى . وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ الِاسْتِثْنَائِيَّ مُؤَلَّفٌ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ :
(١) - إِحْدَاهُمَا : شَرْطِيَّةٌ، وَتُسَمَّى : «كُبْرَى». (٢) - وَالأُخْرَى : اسْتِثْنَائِيَّةٌ ، وَتُسَمَّى : صُغُرَى). -
وَلِذَلِكَ يُسَمَّى بِاسْمَيْنِ كَمَا سَيَذْكُرُهُ المُصَنِّفُ؛ فَالأَوَّلُ هُوَ (١) الِاسْتِثْنَائِيُّ ؛ لِاشْتِمَالِهِ عَلَى الاسْتِثْنَائِيَّةِ ، وَالثَّانِي هُوَ (٢) الشَّرْطِيُّ ؛ لِاشْتِمَالِهِ عَلَى الشَّرْطِيَّةِ.
وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتِ الشَّرْطِيَّةُ : كُبْرَى، وَالاسْتِثْنَانِيَّةُ : صُغُرَى»؛ لِأَنَّ أَلْفَاظَ الِاسْتِثْنَانِيَّةِ عَلَى نَحْوِ النِّصْفِ مِنْ أَلْفَاظِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ ، وَأَيْضاً : لَوِ اعْتَبَرْتُهُمَا بِالتَّرْتِيبِ الاِقْتِرَانِيِّ ؛ بِأَنْ جَعَلْتَهُمَا عَلَى هَيْئَةِ الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ المُرَكَّب مِنْ حَمْلِيَّةٍ وَشَرْطِيَّةٍ ، لَوَجَدْتَ فِيهِ الاسْتِثْنَائِيَّةَ صُغْرَى وَالشَّرْطِيَّةَ كُبْرَى، فَإِذَا قُلْتَ مَثَلاً : كُلَّمَا كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً فَهُوَ حَيَوَانٌ ، لَكِنَّهُ إِنْسَانٌ وَجَدْتَهُ فِي قُوَّةِ قَوْلِكَ : هَذَا إِنْسَانٌ، وَكُلُّ مَا كَانَ إِنْسَاناً فَهُوَ حَيَوَانُ»، وَنَتِيجَتُهُ عَيْنُ نَتِيجَتِهِ، وَلَا يَخْتَلِفَانِ إِلَّا فِي تَقْدِيمِ الصُّغْرَى وَتَأْخِيرِهَا فِي اللَّفْظِ ؛ أَفَادَهُ المَلوِيُّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ (٣)
قَوْلُهُ : (يُعْرَفُ بِـ : (الشَّرْطِيْ) بِحَذْفِ اليَاءِ»، أَوْ ثُبُوتِهَا سَاكِنَةٌ؛ لِلْوَزْنِ، أَيْ: يُسَمَّى بِالشَّرْطِيّ ؛ لِاشْتِمَالِهِ عَلَى الشَّرْطِيَّةِ كَمَا مَرَّ . : وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ يُسَمَّ الاِقْتِرَانِيُّ بِذَلِكَ، مَعَ أَنَّهُ قَدْ يَتَرَكَّبُ مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ عَلَى الرَّاجِحِ ؛ لِعَدَمِ لُزُومِ ذَلِكَ (٤) فَإِنَّهُ قَدْ يَتَرَكَّبُ مِنْ مَحْضِ الحَمْلِيَّاتِ، بَلْ هَذَا هُوَ الْأَكْثَرُ فِيهِ، كَذَا يُؤْخَذُ مِنْ كَلَامِ المَلوِيِّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ» (٥) .
قَوْلُهُ : (بِلَا امْتِرَاءِ) أَيْ : بِلَا شَكٍّ .
قَوْلُهُ : (وَهْوَ الَّذِي دِلَّ ... إلخ (٦)) يَعْنِي : أَنَّ القِيَاسَ الِاسْتِثْنَائِي هُوَ: «الَّذِي دَلَّ عَلَى النَّتِيجَةِ بِالفِعْلِ، أَوْ عَلَى ضِدّهَا كَذَلِكَ».
فَالأَوَّلُ : إِذَا اسْتَثْنَيْتَ عَيْنَ المُقَدَّم ؛ كَمَا إِذَا قُلْتَ: كُلَّمَا كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ، لَكِنَّ الشَّمْسَ طَالِعَةٌ فَـ : «النَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ».
وَالثَّانِي : إِذَا اسْتَثْنَيْتَ نَقِيضَ التَّالِي ؛ كَمَا إِذَا قُلْتَ: كُلَّمَا كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ ، لَكِنَّ النَّهَارَ لَيْسَ بِمَوْجُودٍ فَ: «الشَّمْسُ لَيْسَتْ بِطَالِعَةٍ».
قَوْلُهُ : (أَوْ ضِدَّهَا (٧) المُرَادُ بِـ «الضّدّ» هُنَا : مَعْنَاهُ اللُّغَوِيُّ، وَهُوَ : «مُطْلَقُ المُنَافِي»، فَاندَفَعَ
Chapter on the Exceptive [Syllogism]
(100) And from it is what is called: the exceptive [type]; it is known as: 'the conditional without doubt.'
[His saying:] (Chapter on the exceptive [syllogism]) Perhaps the author titled with this title due to the length of time, otherwise the apparent requirement is that there is no need for it; because his saying 'and from it ... etc.' is conjoined to his saying 'so from it ... etc.' as is not hidden. And know that the exceptive [syllogism] is composed of two premises:
(1) One of them: a conditional, and it is called 'major'. (2) The other: an exceptive, and it is called 'minor'.
And therefore it is called by two names as the author will mention; the first is the exceptive due to its inclusion of the exceptive premise, and the second is the conditional due to its inclusion of the conditional premise.
And the conditional was only called major and the exceptive minor because the words of the exceptive are about half of the words of the conditional, and also: if you consider them in the categorical arrangement, by making them in the form of the first figure composed of a categorical and a conditional, you would find the exceptive minor and the conditional major. So if you say for example: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal, but it is a human,' you find it in the power of your saying: 'This is a human, and everything that is a human is an animal,' and its conclusion is the same as its conclusion, they differ only in the introduction and delay of the minor in expression; this was taught by al-Malwi in his Great [Commentary].
His saying: (It is known as: 'the conditional') with the omission of the ya or with its being quiescent for the meter, meaning: it is named conditional due to its inclusion of the conditional as passed. And the categorical was not called thus, even though it may be composed of a conditional on the preponderant view, because that is not necessary; for it may be composed of pure categoricals, rather this is the more frequent in it; this is taken from the words of al-Malwi in his Great [Commentary].
His saying: (Without doubt) i.e., without doubt.
His saying: (And it is that which indicates ... etc.) He means: that the exceptive syllogism is 'that which indicates the conclusion in actuality, or its opposite likewise.'
The first: when you take the very antecedent; as when you say: Whenever the sun is rising, then daytime exists, but the sun is rising, so: 'daytime exists.'
The second: when you take the opposite of the consequent; as when you say: Whenever the sun is rising, then daytime exists, but daytime does not exist, so: 'the sun is not rising.'
His saying: (or its opposite) The meaning of 'opposite' here is its lexical meaning, which is 'absolute contradictory', so it is repelled.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional syllogism (al-qiyās al-istithnā'ī) and explains its two valid forms. The commentator begins by noting that the author's chapter heading may be considered redundant, since the subsequent text is already conjoined. He then states that the istithnā'ī syllogism consists of two premises: a conditional premise (sharṭiyya) called the 'major' (kubrā), and an exceptive premise (istithnā'iyya) called the 'minor' (ṣughrā). The syllogism receives two names: 'istithnā'ī' because it contains the exceptive premise (which uses an exceptive particle like 'lākin'), and 'sharṭī' because it contains the conditional premise. The commentator explains why the conditional is called major and the exceptive minor: first, the exceptive premise has about half the words of the conditional; second, if one arranges them in the manner of the first figure of the categorical syllogism (composed of a categorical and a conditional), the exceptive corresponds to the minor and the conditional to the major. He illustrates with an example: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal; but it is a human' is equivalent in force to the categorical syllogism 'This is a human; everything human is an animal.' The only difference is the order of premises in wording. Next, he comments on the matn phrase 'without doubt' (bilā imtirā'), meaning without uncertainty. Then he explains the central definition: the exceptive syllogism is one that 'indicates the conclusion in actuality, or its opposite likewise.' This means the conclusion is directly derived, not merely potential. He gives two cases: (1) when one takes the very antecedent (muqaddam) from the conditional, the conclusion is the consequent (tālī); e.g., 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists; but the sun is rising, therefore daytime exists.' (2) When one takes the opposite (naqīḍ) of the consequent, the conclusion is the opposite of the antecedent; e.g., 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists; but daytime does not exist, therefore the sun is not rising.' Finally, he clarifies that 'opposite' here means any contradictory in the lexical sense. Throughout, the passage emphasizes that the istithnā'ī syllogism yields a conclusion directly and actually, unlike the categorical which often requires mental inference.

شرطي

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
The Two Valid Forms of the Conditional Syllogism
The commentator defines the istithnā'ī syllogism and gives examples of its two valid forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct valid conclusions for the two forms of the conditional syllogism: affirming the antecedent and denying the consequent.

Core Idea
The conditional syllogism yields the conclusion directly: either the consequent (when antecedent affirmed) or the negation of the antecedent (when consequent denied).

Now that you know the structure of a conditional syllogism, you need to learn how it actually works. The commentator defines the syllogism as one that 'indicates the conclusion in actuality, or its opposite.' This lesson unpacks that definition and gives you two concrete inference patterns. You will practice applying them to Islamic examples.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
The commentator says the istithnā'ī syllogism is 'that which indicates the conclusion in actuality, or its opposite likewise.' This means the conclusion is directly derived from the premises, not merely potential.
2
First form: Affirming the antecedent
If you affirm the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conditional, you conclude the consequent (tālī). Example: 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists. But the sun is rising. Therefore daytime exists.'
This is the first valid form.
3
Second form: Denying the consequent
If you deny the consequent (take its opposite), you conclude the negation of the antecedent. Example: 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists. But daytime does not exist. Therefore the sun is not rising.'
This is the second valid form.
4
Identify the invalid forms
The commentator only gives these two valid forms. Note that affirming the consequent or denying the antecedent are invalid (fallacies).
This lesson focuses on the valid ones.

قَوْلُهُ : (أَوْ ضِدَّهَا (٧) المُرَادُ بِـ «الضّدّ» هُنَا : مَعْنَاهُ اللُّغَوِيُّ، وَهُوَ : «مُطْلَقُ المُنَافِي»، فَاندَفَعَ
Chapter on the Exceptive [Syllogism]
(100) And from it is what is called: the exceptive [type]; it is known as: 'the conditional without doubt.'
[His saying:] (Chapter on the exceptive [syllogism]) Perhaps the author titled with this title due to the length of time, otherwise the apparent requirement is that there is no need for it; because his saying 'and from it ... etc.' is conjoined to his saying 'so from it ... etc.' as is not hidden. And know that the exceptive [syllogism] is composed of two premises:
(1) One of them: a conditional, and it is called 'major'. (2) The other: an exceptive, and it is called 'minor'.
And therefore it is called by two names as the author will mention; the first is the exceptive due to its inclusion of the exceptive premise, and the second is the conditional due to its inclusion of the conditional premise.
And the conditional was only called major and the exceptive minor because the words of the exceptive are about half of the words of the conditional, and also: if you consider them in the categorical arrangement, by making them in the form of the first figure composed of a categorical and a conditional, you would find the exceptive minor and the conditional major. So if you say for example: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal, but it is a human,' you find it in the power of your saying: 'This is a human, and everything that is a human is an animal,' and its conclusion is the same as its conclusion, they differ only in the introduction and delay of the minor in expression; this was taught by al-Malwi in his Great [Commentary].
His saying: (It is known as: 'the conditional') with the omission of the ya or with its being quiescent for the meter, meaning: it is named conditional due to its inclusion of the conditional as passed. And the categorical was not called thus, even though it may be composed of a conditional on the preponderant view, because that is not necessary; for it may be composed of pure categoricals, rather this is the more frequent in it; this is taken from the words of al-Malwi in his Great [Commentary].
His saying: (Without doubt) i.e., without doubt.
His saying: (And it is that which indicates ... etc.) He means: that the exceptive syllogism is 'that which indicates the conclusion in actuality, or its opposite likewise.'
The first: when you take the very antecedent; as when you say: Whenever the sun is rising, then daytime exists, but the sun is rising, so: 'daytime exists.'
The second: when you take the opposite of the consequent; as when you say: Whenever the sun is rising, then daytime exists, but daytime does not exist, so: 'the sun is not rising.'
His saying: (or its opposite) The meaning of 'opposite' here is its lexical meaning, which is 'absolute contradictory', so it is repelled.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional syllogism (al-qiyās al-istithnā'ī) and explains its two valid forms. The commentator begins by noting that the author's chapter heading may be considered redundant, since the subsequent text is already conjoined. He then states that the istithnā'ī syllogism consists of two premises: a conditional premise (sharṭiyya) called the 'major' (kubrā), and an exceptive premise (istithnā'iyya) called the 'minor' (ṣughrā). The syllogism receives two names: 'istithnā'ī' because it contains the exceptive premise (which uses an exceptive particle like 'lākin'), and 'sharṭī' because it contains the conditional premise. The commentator explains why the conditional is called major and the exceptive minor: first, the exceptive premise has about half the words of the conditional; second, if one arranges them in the manner of the first figure of the categorical syllogism (composed of a categorical and a conditional), the exceptive corresponds to the minor and the conditional to the major. He illustrates with an example: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal; but it is a human' is equivalent in force to the categorical syllogism 'This is a human; everything human is an animal.' The only difference is the order of premises in wording. Next, he comments on the matn phrase 'without doubt' (bilā imtirā'), meaning without uncertainty. Then he explains the central definition: the exceptive syllogism is one that 'indicates the conclusion in actuality, or its opposite likewise.' This means the conclusion is directly derived, not merely potential. He gives two cases: (1) when one takes the very antecedent (muqaddam) from the conditional, the conclusion is the consequent (tālī); e.g., 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists; but the sun is rising, therefore daytime exists.' (2) When one takes the opposite (naqīḍ) of the consequent, the conclusion is the opposite of the antecedent; e.g., 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists; but daytime does not exist, therefore the sun is not rising.' Finally, he clarifies that 'opposite' here means any contradictory in the lexical sense. Throughout, the passage emphasizes that the istithnā'ī syllogism yields a conclusion directly and actually, unlike the categorical which often requires mental inference.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Valid Moods of the Conditional Syllogism
Author defines conditional syllogism and explains its two productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid conditional syllogism using modus ponens (affirming the antecedent) or modus tollens (denying the consequent) and explain the logical grounding for each.

Core Idea
In a conditional, affirming the antecedent or denying the consequent necessarily yields a true conclusion.

This lesson unpacks the two valid moods of the conditional syllogism as explained by the author. You will learn how the logical relationship of malzum (necessary condition) and lazim (necessary consequent) guarantees these inferences, and you will practice constructing them with Islamic examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Locate the conjunctive conditional (shartiyya muttasila) in the argument. Break it into its antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali). In the passage, the example is: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal.' The antecedent is 'this is a human', the consequent is 'it is an animal'.
2
Check the exceptive premise (istithna')
Determine whether the exceptive premise affirms the antecedent or denies the consequent. For modus ponens: 'but it is a human' affirms the antecedent. For modus tollens: 'but it is not an animal' denies the consequent.
3
Apply the valid rule
If the exceptive premise affirms the antecedent, conclude the consequent (modus ponens). If it denies the consequent, conclude the denial of the antecedent (modus tollens). The passage gives: 'therefore it is an animal' and 'therefore it is not a human'.
4
Verify the logical rationale
The antecedent is the malzum (necessary condition) for the consequent (lazim). Affirming the malzum necessitates affirming the lazim. Denying the lazim necessitates denying the malzum. This relationship guarantees validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الاستثنائي (۱۰۱) وَهُوَ الَّذِي دِلَّ عَلَى النَّتِيْجَةِ أَوْضِدِّهَا بِالفِعْلِ لَا بِالقُوَّةِ (۱۰۲) فَإِنْ يَكُ الشَّرْطِيُّ ذَا انْصَالِ: أَنْتَجَ وَضْعُ ذَاكَ وَضْعَ النَّالِي (۱۰۳) وَرَفْعُ تَالٍ رَفْعَ أَوَّلِ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ فِي عَكْسِهِمَا لِمَا انْجَلَى

قَوْلُهُ : (فَإِنْ يَكُ الشَّرْطِيُّ ... إلخ) غَرَضُهُ بِذَلِكَ : بَيَانُ كَيْفِيَّةِ إِنْتاجِ القِيَاسِ الشَّرْطِي. وَقَوْلُهُ : (ذَا اتَّصَالِ) أَيْ : بِأَنْ كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّةٌ مُتَّصِلَةٌ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (أَنْتَجَ وَضْعُ ذَاكَ وَضْعَ التَّالِي أَيْ : أَنْتَجَ إِنْبَاتُ المُقَدَّمِ فِي الِاسْتِثْنَائِيَّةِ إِثْبَاتَ التَّالِي فِي النَّتِيجَةِ ؛ لِأَنَّ المُقَدَّمَ مَلْزُومٌ لِلتَّالِي، وَتُبُوتُ المَلْزُومِ يَقْتَضِي ثُبُوتَ لَازِمِهِ، وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلَّمَا كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً كَانَ حَيَوَاناً ، لَكِنَّهُ إِنْسَانٌ» فَـ : «هُوَ حَيَوَانٌ». وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَرَفْعُ تَالٍ رَفْعَ أَوَّلِ أَيْ : وَأَنْتَجَ نَفْيُ التَّالِي فِي الاسْتِثْنَائِيَّةِ نَفْيَ المُقَدَّمِ فِي النَّتِيجَةِ ؛ لِأَنَّ التَّالِي لَازِمٌ لِلمُقَدَّمِ ، وَانْتِفَاءُ اللَّازِمِ يَقْتَضِي انْتِفَاءَ المَلْزُومِ، وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلَّمَا كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً كَانَ حَيَوَاناً ، لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانِ فَـ: «لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانِ». قَوْلُهُ : (وَلَا يَلْزَمُ فِي عَكْسِهِمَا يَعْنِي : أَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ الإِنْتَاجُ فِي عَكْسِ وَضْعِ المُقَدَّمِ وَهُوَ وَضْعُ التَّالِي، وَلَا فِي عَكْسِ رَفْعِ التَّالِي وَهُوَ رَفْعُ المُقَدَّمِ.
Chapter on the Exceptional [Syllogism] (101) It is the one that indicates the conclusion or its opposite actually, not potentially. (102) If the conditional is conjunctive: positing that [antecedent] produces positing the consequent, (103) and removing a consequent removes the first, and it does not follow in their converse, as is clear.

His saying: (If the conditional... etc.) His purpose thereby is to explain the manner of production of the conditional syllogism. His saying: (conjunctive) i.e., that it be a conjunctive conditional. His saying: (positing that produces positing the consequent) i.e., positing the antecedent in the exceptive [premise] produces affirming the consequent in the conclusion, because the antecedent is a necessary condition for the consequent, and the affirmation of the necessary condition necessitates the affirmation of its necessary consequent. An example of that is that you say: Whenever this is a human, it is an animal; but it is a human, therefore it is an animal. And his saying: (and removing a consequent removes the first) i.e., denying the consequent in the exceptive premise produces denying the antecedent in the conclusion, because the consequent is a necessary consequent of the antecedent, and the negation of the necessary consequent necessitates the negation of the necessary condition. An example of that is that you say: Whenever this is a human, it is an animal; but it is not an animal, therefore it is not a human. His saying: (and it does not follow in their converse) means that production does not follow in the converse of positing the antecedent, which is positing the consequent, nor in the converse of removing the consequent, which is removing the antecedent.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional syllogism (al-qiyas al-istithna'i) and explains its two valid moods and two invalid ones. The author begins by stating that the conditional syllogism is one that indicates the conclusion or its opposite in actuality, not potentially. This means the conclusion is explicitly stated in the syllogism itself, unlike the conjunctive syllogism where the conclusion is implicit. The passage then specifies that if the conditional premise is conjunctive (muttasil), i.e., an 'if-then' statement, then positing the antecedent (wad' al-muqaddam) yields positing the consequent (wad' al-tali). This is because the antecedent is a necessary condition (malzum) for the consequent (lazim). The example given: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal. But it is a human, therefore it is an animal.' This illustrates the modus ponens form. Conversely, denying the consequent (raf' al-tali) yields denying the antecedent (raf' al-muqaddam). This is because the consequent is a necessary consequent (lazim) of the antecedent, so its negation necessitates the negation of the antecedent. The example: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal. But it is not an animal, therefore it is not a human.' This is modus tollens. The author then notes that the 'converse' of these two moods are invalid: positing the consequent does not produce positing the antecedent, and denying the antecedent does not produce denying the consequent. This guards against the fallacy of affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent. All examples are from the Islamic tradition: the relationship between 'human' and 'animal' is used, which is a standard example in logic (insan/hayawan).

Key Takeaway
The only valid moods in a conditional with a conjunctive major premise are modus ponens (affirming antecedent → affirming consequent) and modus tollens (denying consequent → denying antecedent).

The Syllogism

Invalid Moods of the Conditional Syllogism: Affirming the Consequent and Denying the Antecedent
Author explains the non-productive moods of the conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacies of affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent in conditional arguments and explain why they are invalid.

Core Idea
Affirming the consequent or denying the antecedent breaks the logical connection: the consequent can be true even if the antecedent is false.

The author warns that the converse of the two valid moods yields no conclusion. This lesson examines these fallacies, showing why they do not guarantee truth, and how to spot them using Islamic examples.

Guided Path
1
Recall the valid moods
Remember that in a conditional syllogism, affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) and denying the consequent (modus tollens) are valid. Now we examine their converses.
2
Consider affirming the consequent
Take the conditional 'If it is wine, it is forbidden'. If we affirm the consequent 'it is forbidden', we cannot conclude 'it is wine' because many things are forbidden besides wine (e.g., pork). This is the fallacy of affirming the consequent.
3
Consider denying the antecedent
From the same conditional, if we deny the antecedent 'it is not wine', we cannot conclude 'it is not forbidden' because non-wine can still be forbidden (e.g., pork). This is the fallacy of denying the antecedent.
4
Check the logical rationale
The conditional only states that the antecedent is sufficient for the consequent, not necessary. Therefore, the consequent can be true without the antecedent, and the antecedent can be false while the consequent is true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الاستثنائي (۱۰۱) وَهُوَ الَّذِي دِلَّ عَلَى النَّتِيْجَةِ أَوْضِدِّهَا بِالفِعْلِ لَا بِالقُوَّةِ (۱۰۲) فَإِنْ يَكُ الشَّرْطِيُّ ذَا انْصَالِ: أَنْتَجَ وَضْعُ ذَاكَ وَضْعَ النَّالِي (۱۰۳) وَرَفْعُ تَالٍ رَفْعَ أَوَّلِ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ فِي عَكْسِهِمَا لِمَا انْجَلَى

قَوْلُهُ : (فَإِنْ يَكُ الشَّرْطِيُّ ... إلخ) غَرَضُهُ بِذَلِكَ : بَيَانُ كَيْفِيَّةِ إِنْتاجِ القِيَاسِ الشَّرْطِي. وَقَوْلُهُ : (ذَا اتَّصَالِ) أَيْ : بِأَنْ كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّةٌ مُتَّصِلَةٌ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (أَنْتَجَ وَضْعُ ذَاكَ وَضْعَ التَّالِي أَيْ : أَنْتَجَ إِنْبَاتُ المُقَدَّمِ فِي الِاسْتِثْنَائِيَّةِ إِثْبَاتَ التَّالِي فِي النَّتِيجَةِ ؛ لِأَنَّ المُقَدَّمَ مَلْزُومٌ لِلتَّالِي، وَتُبُوتُ المَلْزُومِ يَقْتَضِي ثُبُوتَ لَازِمِهِ، وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلَّمَا كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً كَانَ حَيَوَاناً ، لَكِنَّهُ إِنْسَانٌ» فَـ : «هُوَ حَيَوَانٌ». وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَرَفْعُ تَالٍ رَفْعَ أَوَّلِ أَيْ : وَأَنْتَجَ نَفْيُ التَّالِي فِي الاسْتِثْنَائِيَّةِ نَفْيَ المُقَدَّمِ فِي النَّتِيجَةِ ؛ لِأَنَّ التَّالِي لَازِمٌ لِلمُقَدَّمِ ، وَانْتِفَاءُ اللَّازِمِ يَقْتَضِي انْتِفَاءَ المَلْزُومِ، وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلَّمَا كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً كَانَ حَيَوَاناً ، لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانِ فَـ: «لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانِ». قَوْلُهُ : (وَلَا يَلْزَمُ فِي عَكْسِهِمَا يَعْنِي : أَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ الإِنْتَاجُ فِي عَكْسِ وَضْعِ المُقَدَّمِ وَهُوَ وَضْعُ التَّالِي، وَلَا فِي عَكْسِ رَفْعِ التَّالِي وَهُوَ رَفْعُ المُقَدَّمِ.
Chapter on the Exceptional [Syllogism] (101) It is the one that indicates the conclusion or its opposite actually, not potentially. (102) If the conditional is conjunctive: positing that [antecedent] produces positing the consequent, (103) and removing a consequent removes the first, and it does not follow in their converse, as is clear.

His saying: (If the conditional... etc.) His purpose thereby is to explain the manner of production of the conditional syllogism. His saying: (conjunctive) i.e., that it be a conjunctive conditional. His saying: (positing that produces positing the consequent) i.e., positing the antecedent in the exceptive [premise] produces affirming the consequent in the conclusion, because the antecedent is a necessary condition for the consequent, and the affirmation of the necessary condition necessitates the affirmation of its necessary consequent. An example of that is that you say: Whenever this is a human, it is an animal; but it is a human, therefore it is an animal. And his saying: (and removing a consequent removes the first) i.e., denying the consequent in the exceptive premise produces denying the antecedent in the conclusion, because the consequent is a necessary consequent of the antecedent, and the negation of the necessary consequent necessitates the negation of the necessary condition. An example of that is that you say: Whenever this is a human, it is an animal; but it is not an animal, therefore it is not a human. His saying: (and it does not follow in their converse) means that production does not follow in the converse of positing the antecedent, which is positing the consequent, nor in the converse of removing the consequent, which is removing the antecedent.

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional syllogism (al-qiyas al-istithna'i) and explains its two valid moods and two invalid ones. The author begins by stating that the conditional syllogism is one that indicates the conclusion or its opposite in actuality, not potentially. This means the conclusion is explicitly stated in the syllogism itself, unlike the conjunctive syllogism where the conclusion is implicit. But it is a human, therefore it is an animal.' This illustrates the modus ponens form. Conversely, denying the consequent (raf' al-tali) yields denying the antecedent (raf' al-muqaddam). This is because the consequent is a necessary consequent (lazim) of the antecedent, so its negation necessitates the negation of the antecedent. The example: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal. But it is not an animal, therefore it is not a human.' This is modus tollens. The author then notes that the 'converse' of these two moods are invalid: positing the consequent does not produce positing the antecedent, and denying the antecedent does not produce denying the consequent. This guards against the fallacy of affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent. All examples are from the Islamic tradition: the relationship between 'human' and 'animal' is used, which is a standard example in logic (insan/hayawan).
