The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Valid and Invalid Moods of the Conditional Syllogism
Explanation of conditional syllogism's valid moods and invalid reverses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two valid moods (modus ponens and modus tollens) and two invalid reverses (affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent) of a conjunctive conditional syllogism, and explain why the reverses fail using the concept of generality.

Core Idea
In conditional logic, affirming the antecedent or denying the consequent yields certainty; the reverses are fallacies.

This lesson explores the conditional syllogism (al-istithnā'ī), where the major premise is a conditional statement. You will learn the two valid moods that produce certain conclusions and the two invalid reverses that do not, and understand why they fail based on the logical relationship between antecedent and consequent. The commentary of al-Bajuri clarifies the reasoning using examples from everyday logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of a conditional syllogism
A conditional syllogism consists of a major premise that is a conditional statement (e.g., 'If A then B') and a minor premise that affirms or denies one part of that conditional. The conclusion then follows or does not follow depending on the mood.
2
Learn the first valid mood: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens)
If the minor premise affirms the antecedent (the 'if' part), then the consequent (the 'then' part) is necessarily affirmed in the conclusion. Example: 'If it is a human, it is an animal. It is a human; therefore it is an animal.'
This works because the antecedent is a sufficient condition for the consequent.
3
Learn the second valid mood: denying the consequent (modus tollens)
If the minor premise denies the consequent, then the antecedent is necessarily denied. Example: 'If it is a human, it is an animal. It is not an animal; therefore it is not a human.'
This works because if the consequent is false, the antecedent cannot be true (contrapositive).
4
Understand why affirming the consequent is invalid
If the minor premise affirms the consequent, no conclusion follows. For example, 'If it is a human, it is an animal. It is an animal; therefore it is a human' is invalid because the consequent (animal) is more general than the antecedent (human). Many things that are animals are not humans.
The invalidity is formal, not material. Even if sometimes it happens to be true, the logical form does not guarantee it.
5
Understand why denying the antecedent is invalid
If the minor premise denies the antecedent, no conclusion follows. Example: 'If it is a human, it is an animal. It is not a human; therefore it is not an animal' is invalid because the antecedent is more specific. Many non-humans are animals.
This is the reverse of modus tollens and is equally fallacious.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قَالَ الباجُورِيُّ: قَوْلُهُ (بِالفِعْلِ) أَيْ: بِأَنْ يَكُونَ ذَلِكَ مَذْكُوراً فِيهِ بِصُورَتِهِ. وَقَوْلُهُ (لَا بِالقُوَّةِ) تَصْرِيحُ بِمَا عُلِمَ. وَقَوْلُهُ (فَإِنْ يَكُ الشَّرْطِيُّ ... إلخ) حَيَوَاناً لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانِ فَلَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانِ. وَقَوْلُهُ (وَلَا يَلْزَمُ فِي عَكْسِهِمَا) يَعْنِي أَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ الإِنْتَاجُ فِي عَكْسِ وَضْعِ المُقَدَّمِ وَهُوَ وَضْعُ التَّالِي وَلَا فِي عَكْسِ رَفْعِ التَّالِي وَهُوَ رَفْعُ المُقَدَّمِ فَإِذَا قُلْتَ مَثَلاً كُلَّمَا كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً كَانَ حَيَوَاناً لَكِنَّهُ حَيَوَانٌ لَمْ يُنْتِجِ أَنَّهُ إِنْسَانُ وَلَا أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ وَإِذَا قُلْتَ مَثَلاً كُلَّمَا كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً كَانَ حَيَوَاناً لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ لَمْ يُنْتِجِ أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانٍ وَلَا أَنَّهُ حَيَوَانٌ. وَلَا يَرِدُ نَحْوَ كُلَّمَا كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً كَانَ نَاطِقاً لِأَنَّ اسْتِلْزَامَ وَضْعِ التَّالِي فِيهِ لِوَضْعِ المُقَدَّمِ وَرَفْعُ المُقَدَّمِ رَفْعُ التَّالِي لَيْسَ لِصُورَةِ القِيَاسِ بَلْ لِحُصُوصِ المَادَّةِ. وَقَوْلُهُ لِمَا انْجَلَى أَيْ لِمَا اتَّضَحَ مِنْ أَنَّهُ قَدْ يَكُونُ التَّالِي أَعَمَّ مِنَ المُقَدَّمِ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ كُلَّمَا كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً كَانَ حَيَوَانَا وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ وَضْعِ الأَعَمِّ وَضْعُ الأَخَصُ وَلَا رَفْعُهُ وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ رَفْعِ الأَخَصُ رَفْعُ الأَعَمِّ وَلَا وَضْعُهُ.
Chapter on the excluding (syllogism). It is that which indicates the conclusion or its opposite actually, not potentially. If the conditional is conjunctive: positing that (the antecedent) yields positing the consequent, and removing the consequent removes the first, and the reverse of the two does not necessarily (yield), as is clear. Al-Bajuri said: His statement 'actually' means that it is mentioned in it in its form. His statement 'not potentially' is an affirmation of what is known. His statement 'if the conditional... etc.' intends thereby to explain the manner of production of the conditional syllogism. His statement 'conjunctive' means that it is a conjunctive conditional. His statement 'positing that (the antecedent) yields positing the consequent' means that affirming the antecedent in the excluding (premise) yields affirming the consequent in the conclusion, because the antecedent is a necessary condition for the consequent, and the establishment of the necessary condition necessitates the establishment of its necessary consequent. An example is that you say: Whenever this is a human, it is an animal; but it is a human; therefore it is an animal. His statement 'and removing the consequent removes the first' means that negating the consequent in the excluding (premise) yields negating the antecedent in the conclusion, because the consequent is a necessary consequent of the antecedent, and the negation of the necessary consequent necessitates the negation of the necessary condition. An example is that you say: Whenever this is a human, it is an animal; but it is not an animal; therefore it is not a human. His statement 'and the reverse of the two does not necessarily (yield)' means that production does not necessarily occur in the reverse of positing the antecedent, which is positing the consequent, nor in the reverse of removing the consequent, which is removing the antecedent. For example, if you say: Whenever this is a human, it is an animal; but it is an animal, it does not yield that it is a human nor that it is not a human. And if you say: Whenever this is a human, it is an animal; but it is not a human, it does not yield that it is not an animal nor that it is an animal. And it is not refuted by (examples) like 'Whenever this is a human, it is rational', because the entailment of positing the consequent in it for positing the antecedent, and removing the antecedent for removing the consequent, is not due to the form of the syllogism but due to the specificity of the matter. His statement 'as is clear' means because it is obvious that the consequent may be more general than the antecedent, as in your statement: Whenever this is a human, it is an animal. It does not follow from positing the more general the positing of the more specific nor its removal, nor does it follow from removing the more specific the removal of the more general nor its positing.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the conditional syllogism (al-istithnā'ī), focusing on the conjunctive type (muttasil). The matn begins by defining the conditional syllogism as one that yields the conclusion or its opposite actually, not potentially—meaning the conclusion is explicitly contained in the premises. Then it states two valid moods for the conjunctive conditional: if you affirm the antecedent (the condition), you affirm the consequent; and if you deny the consequent, you deny the antecedent. This is because the antecedent is a necessary condition (malzūm) and the consequent is its necessary consequent (lāzim); establishing the necessary condition entails its necessary consequent, and negating the necessary consequent entails negation of the necessary condition. The author provides the classic example: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal'—affirming 'human' gives 'animal', denying 'animal' gives 'not human'. The matn then warns that the reverse of these two moods (i.e., affirming the consequent or denying the antecedent) does not necessarily yield a valid conclusion. The commentary explains why: the consequent may be more general than the antecedent (e.g., 'animal' is more general than 'human'), so from 'it is an animal' we cannot infer it is human or not, and from 'it is not human' we cannot infer it is not animal or is animal. It also notes that examples like 'human' and 'rational' (where the terms are coextensive) are not counterexamples because the validity there comes from the material (the specific subject matter) not the logical form. This passage teaches the core rules of conditional logic, emphasizing that the validity of the moods depends on the logical relationship of implication, not on accidental equality.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a valid mood for a conjunctive conditional syllogism?
A) Affirming the antecedent and affirming the consequent.
B) Affirming the consequent and affirming the antecedent.
C) Denying the antecedent and denying the consequent.
D) Denying the consequent and affirming the antecedent.

Key Takeaway
In a conjunctive conditional syllogism, the only valid inferences are affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) and denying the consequent (modus tollens); the reverses are formally invalid because the consequent may be more general than the antecedent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conditional syllogism in modus tollens using a fiqh example. For instance: 'If a prayer is missing an essential pillar, it is invalid. This prayer is not invalid (i.e., it is valid), therefore it does not miss any essential pillar.' Write your own example.
?
Identify the fallacy in the following argument: 'If a substance is khamr, then it is intoxicating. This substance is intoxicating, therefore it is khamr.' Explain why it is invalid using the concept of generality.

Disjunctive Syllogism: Types and Rules
Author classifies disjunctive syllogisms into three types and states the general rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a disjunctive conditional into one of three types based on exclusivity and determine the valid inferences for each type.

Core Idea
Not all 'either/or' statements are alike—the logical inference changes with the type of exclusivity.

Disjunctive syllogisms are built from 'either...or' statements, but the precise meaning of 'or' matters enormously. The matn gives a general rule: affirming one disjunct denies the other. Al-Bajuri's commentary unpacks this by distinguishing three kinds of disjunction: those that prevent conjunction (both true), those that prevent emptiness (both false), and those that prevent both. Understanding these types is crucial for applying the rule correctly in Islamic legal reasoning and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn's rule
The matn states: 'positing this yields removing that, and the reverse is likewise.' This means affirming one disjunct yields denying the other, and denying one yields affirming the other. But this rule does not apply to all disjunctions uniformly.
The 'reverse' mentioned here is not always valid—it depends on the type of disjunction.
2
Understand the three types of disjunctive conditionals
Al-Bajuri clarifies that disjunctive conditionals are of three types: (1) مانعة الجمع والخلو (mutually exclusive and exhaustive), (2) مانعة الجمع فقط (mutually exclusive only), and (3) مانعة الخلو فقط (exhaustive only). Each places different constraints on truth values.
The most specific type is مانعة الجمع والخلو, which combines both restrictions.
3
See how the rule applies differently to each type
For type 1 (both preventers), the matn's rule holds fully: affirming one denies the other, and denying one affirms the other. For type 2 (preventer of conjunction only), affirming one denies the other, but denying one does NOT affirm the other (both could be false). For type 3 (preventer of emptiness only), denying one affirms the other, but affirming one does NOT deny the other (both could be true).
This difference is crucial: the general rule must be qualified.
4
Note the number of conclusions each type yields
Al-Bajuri states that type 1 yields four conclusions (affirm A→deny B, affirm B→deny A, deny A→affirm B, deny B→affirm A). Types 2 and 3 yield only two conclusions each (for type 2: affirm A→deny B and affirm B→deny A; for type 3: deny A→affirm B and deny B→affirm A).
The full details of these conclusions are explained later in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ يَكُنْ مُنْفَصِلاً: فَوَضْعُ ذَا يُنْتِجُ رَفْعَ ذَاكَ، وَالعَكْسُ كَذَا.

قَالَ الباجُورِيُّ: قَوْلُهُ (وَإِنْ يَكُنْ مُنْفَصِلاً) أَيْ بِأَنْ كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّةٌ مُنْفَصِلَةٌ. وَقَدْ تَقَدَّمَ أَنَّهَا إِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ مَانِعَةَ الجَمْعِ وَالخُلُوِّ مَعاً وَهَذَا القِسْمُ هُوَ الأَخَصُّ وَإِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ مَانِعَةَ الجَمْعِ فَقَط وَإِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ مَانِعَةَ الخُلُوِّ فَقَط. وَلِذَلِكَ كَانَ القِيَاسُ المُنْفَصِلُ ثَلَاثَةَ أَقْسَامِ: الأَوَّلُ مَانِعُهُمَا وَهُوَ الأَخَصُّ وَهُوَ مَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ المُنْفَصِلَةُ مَانِعَتَهُمَا. وَالثَّانِي مَانِعُ الجَمْعِ فَقَط وَهُوَ مَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ المُنْفَصِلَةُ مَانِعَةَ الجَمْعِ فَقَط. وَالثَّالِثُ مَانِعُ الخُلُوِّ فَقَط وَهُوَ مَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ المُنْفَصِلَةُ مَانِعَةَ الخُلُوِّ فَقَط. وَقَدْ بَيَّنَ المُصَنِّفُ كَيْفِيَّةَ إِنْتَاجِ كُلِّ مِنْ هَذِهِ الأَقْسَامِ عَلَى التَّرْتِيبِ المَذْكُورِ فَذَكَرَ لِلأَوَّلِ أَرْبَعَ نَتَائِجَ وَلِكُلٍّ مِنَ الثَّانِي وَالثَّالِثِ نَتِيجَتَيْنِ كَمَا سَيَأْتِي بَيَانُهُ. قَوْلُهُ (فَوَضْعُ ذَا يُنْتِجُ رَفْعَ ذَاكَ) أَيْ فَإِنْبَاتُ أَحَدِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ يُنْتِجُ نَفْي الآخَرِ لِأَنَّهُ يَمْتَنِعُ ...
And if it is disjunctive: then positing this yields removing that, and the reverse is likewise. Al-Bajuri said: His statement 'if it is disjunctive' means that it is a disjunctive conditional. It has preceded that it is either mutually exclusive and exhaustive (preventing both conjunction and emptiness) and this type is the most specific; or preventing conjunction only; or preventing emptiness only. Therefore the disjunctive syllogism is three types: the first is the preventer of both, which is the most specific, and it is that whose disjunctive conditional prevents both. The second is the preventer of conjunction only, and it is that whose disjunctive conditional prevents conjunction only. The third is the preventer of emptiness only, and it is that whose disjunctive conditional prevents emptiness only. And the author has explained the manner of production of each of these types in the mentioned order, so he mentioned for the first four conclusions, and for each of the second and third two conclusions, as will come in its explanation. His statement 'then positing this yields removing that' means that affirming one of the two sides yields negation of the other, because it is impossible …
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage presents the disjunctive syllogism (al-munfaṣil). The matn states the general rule: affirming one disjunct yields denial of the other, and conversely (denying one yields affirming the other). The commentary then classifies disjunctive conditionals into three types based on the logical relationship between the disjuncts: (1) مانعة الجمع والخلو (mutually exclusive and exhaustive), where the disjuncts cannot both be true nor both false; (2) مانعة الجمع فقط (mutually exclusive only), where they cannot both be true but can both be false; and (3) مانعة الخلو فقط (exhaustive only), where they cannot both be false but can both be true. The commentary notes that the first type yields four conclusions (affirming one denies the other, and denying one affirms the other, both ways), while the second and third yield only two conclusions each. For example, in a مانعة الجمع فقط, if you affirm one disjunct, you deny the other, but denying one does not allow you to affirm the other (since both could be false). The passage sets up the classification; the specific yields are to be explained later.

منفصل
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
مانعة الجمع والخلو

Key Takeaway
Disjunctive syllogisms require careful identification of the exclusivity type to know which inferences are valid.

Invalid Moods of Conditional Syllogism: Affirming the Consequent and Denying the Antecedent
Bajuri explains the invalidity of affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent in conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain why affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent are invalid in conditional syllogisms, and distinguish between formal validity and material entailment.

Core Idea
Affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent are invalid because the logician's tool is form, not matter.

In this passage, Bajuri clarifies a common pitfall in conditional reasoning. While modus ponens and modus tollens are valid, many students mistakenly think that from 'If A then B' and B, we can infer A. Bajuri shows why this is false using the Islamic logical example of human and animal, and explains that apparent counterexamples only work due to the specific matter, not the logical form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four moods of conditional syllogism
Recall that a conditional syllogism has four possible moods: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens, valid), denying the consequent (modus tollens, valid), affirming the consequent (invalid), and denying the antecedent (invalid). Focus on the two invalid ones.
These are also called 'fallacies of the conditional' in Western logic.
2
Apply Bajuri's example for affirming the consequent
Take the conditional 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal.' Suppose you affirm the consequent: 'It is an animal.' Bajuri states that you cannot conclude either 'it is a human' or 'it is not a human.' Check why: animal is more general than human, so being an animal does not guarantee being human.
This illustrates that the inference is formally invalid.
3
Apply Bajuri's example for denying the antecedent
Now deny the antecedent: 'It is not a human.' From this you cannot conclude 'it is not an animal' or 'it is an animal.' Again, because animal includes many non-human things, not being human does not rule out being an animal.
This is the other invalid mood.
4
Examine the apparent counterexample
Bajuri considers 'Whenever this is a human, it is rational.' Here, affirming the consequent (it is rational) does imply it is a human, and denying the antecedent (it is not a human) implies it is not rational. But he warns that this is not due to the logical form (sura) but due to the specific matter (madda) — the terms are coextensive.
The validity of such cases depends on content, not form, so they do not save the invalid moods.
5
Distinguish form (sura) and matter (madda)
The formal validity of a syllogism depends solely on its structure (sura), not on the meanings of the terms (madda). The two invalid moods are formally invalid regardless of the matter. Only modus ponens and modus tollens are always valid by form.
This distinction is key in Islamic logic for evaluating arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(103) وَرَفْعُ تَالٍ رَفْعَ أَوَّلٍ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ فِي عَكْسِهِمَا لِمَا انْجَلَى
الباجوري
التَّالِي، وَلَا فِي عَكْسِ رَفْعِ التَّالِي وَهُوَ رَفْعُ المُقَدَّمِ ; فَإِذَا قُلْتَ مَثَلاً : كُلَّمَا كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً كَانَ حَيَوَاناً ، لَكِنَّهُ حَيَوَانٌ، لَمْ يُنْتِجُ : «أَنَّهُ إِنْسَانُ وَلَا : أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ ، وَإِذَا قُلْتَ مَثَلاً : كُلَّمَا كَانَ :
هَذَا إِنْسَاناً كَانَ حَيَوَاناً ، لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانِ لَمْ يُنْتِجُ : «أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِحَيَوَانٍ وَلَا : «أَنَّهُ حَيَوَانٌ». وَلَا يَرِدُ نَحْوَ : كُلَّمَا كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً كَانَ نَاطِقاً»; لِأَنَّ اسْتِلْزَامَ وَضْعِ التَّالِي فِيهِ لِوَضْعِ المُقَدَّمِ، وَرَفْعُ المُقَدَّمِ رَفْعُ التَّالِي لَيْسَ لِصُورَةِ القِيَاسِ، بَلْ لِحُصُوصِ المَادَّةِ .
قَوْلُهُ : لِمَا انْجَلَى أَيْ: لِمَا اتَّضَحَ مِنْ أَنَّهُ قَدْ يَكُونُ التَّالِي أَعَمَّ مِنَ المُقَدَّمِ; كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: كُلَّمَا كَانَ هَذَا إِنْسَاناً ، كَانَ حَيَوَانَا، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ وَضْعِ الأَعَمِّ وَضْعُ الأَخَصٌ وَلَا رَفْعُهُ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ رَفْعِ الأَخَصُ رَفْعُ الأَعَمِّ وَلَا وَضْعُهُ.
(103) And negating the consequent (entails) negating the antecedent, and it does not follow in their converse because it is clear. Al-Bajuri: The consequent, and not in the converse of negating the consequent which is negating the antecedent; for if you say for example: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal; but it is an animal,' it does not produce 'it is a human' nor 'it is not a human.' And if you say for example: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal; but it is not a human,' it does not produce 'it is not an animal' nor 'it is an animal.' And it is not countered by something like: 'Whenever this is a human, it is rational'; because the entailment of affirming the consequent there for affirming the antecedent, and negating the antecedent negating the consequent, is not due to the form of the syllogism, but due to the specificity of the matter. His saying: 'because it is clear' means: because it is obvious that the consequent may be more general than the antecedent; as in your saying: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal'; and from affirming the more general, the less general is not necessarily affirmed nor denied, and from denying the less general, the more general is not necessarily denied nor affirmed.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a verse from al-Sullam al-Munawraq dealing with conditional syllogisms (qiyas sharti muttasil). The author, al-Bajuri, explains the two invalid moods of the conditional: affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent. He begins by citing the matn: 'Raising the consequent raises the antecedent'—meaning that if you negate the consequent, you can negate the antecedent (modus tollens). However, he immediately clarifies that the converse of that valid mood—namely, raising (negating) the antecedent does not necessarily raise the consequent, and similarly, affirming the consequent does not affirm the antecedent. He provides two examples using the standard Islamic logical example: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal.' If you affirm the animal (consequent), you cannot conclude that it is human (invalid). If you deny the human (antecedent), you cannot conclude that it is not an animal (invalid). He then addresses a potential counterexample: 'Whenever this is a human, it is rational.' Here, affirming the consequent does entail the antecedent, but that is due to the specific matter (i.e., the nature of the terms: being rational is coextensive with human) not due to the logical form. Therefore, the invalidity remains general. He further explains that the reason these moods are invalid is that the consequent is often more general than the antecedent (e.g., animal is more general than human). Affirming the more general does not entail affirming the less general, and denying the less general does not entail denying the more general. This lesson guards against the common mistake of thinking that from 'if A then B' and B true, we can infer A; or from not-A, we can infer not-B.

Key Terms
رفع
وضع
مقدم
تال
صورة القياس
مادة

C) It is not a human, therefore it is not an animal.

Key Takeaway
Affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent are formally invalid in conditional syllogisms; any appearance of validity stems from the specific matter, not the logical form.

Exclusive Disjunction (Mani' al-Jam' wa al-Khuluww)
Bajuri classifies disjunctive conditionals into three types and gives the valid conclusions for each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the exclusive disjunction (both conjunction and disjunction prevented) and derive all four valid conclusions from a given exclusive disjunctive premise.

Core Idea
In an exclusive disjunction, affirming one side forces denial of the other, and denying one side forces affirmation of the other.

The first and most specific type of disjunctive conditional prohibits both the conjunction (both parts true) and the disjunction (both parts false). This is the exclusive 'or' (xor). Al-Bajuri explains that from such a premise, we can infer from affirmation to negation and from negation to affirmation. For example, number is either even or odd. In this lesson, we will learn to apply these inferences using Islamic examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining characteristic
Recall that in exclusive disjunction (mani' al-jam' wa al-khuluw), both parts cannot be true together and cannot be false together.
This is the most specific type of disjunctive conditional.
2
Recognize valid inference patterns
There are two valid inference patterns: (a) from affirming one part, negate the other; (b) from negating one part, affirm the other.
Both patterns are valid because they prevent both conjunction and disjunction.
3
Apply affirmation to negation
Given 'Number is either even or odd' and 'It is even', conclude 'It is not odd'.
This is the first valid mode.
4
Apply negation to affirmation
Given 'Number is either even or odd' and 'It is not even', conclude 'It is odd'.
This is the second valid mode.
5
Note the four conclusions
The two patterns yield four conclusions: affirming one denies the other (two conclusions), denying one affirms the other (two conclusions).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(104) وَإِنْ يَكُنْ مُنْفَصِلاً: فَوَضْعُ ذَا يُنْتِجُ رَفْعَ ذَاكَ، وَالعَكْسُ كَذَا
الباجوري
قَوْلُهُ : (وَإِنْ يَكُنْ مُنْفَصِلاً ... إلخ) أَيْ: بِأَنْ كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّةٌ مُنْفَصِلَةٌ، وَقَدْ تَقَدَّمَ أَنَّهَا : إِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ مَانِعَةَ الجَمْعِ وَالخُلُوِّ مَعاً ، وَهَذَا القِسْمُ هُوَ الأَخَصُّ ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ مَانِعَةَ الجَمْعِ فَقَط ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ مَانِعَةَ الخُلُوِّ فَقَط : وَلِذَلِكَ كَانَ القِيَاسُ المُنْفَصِلُ ثَلَاثَةَ أَقْسَامِ : الأَوَّلُ: مَانِعُهُمَا، وَهُوَ الأَخَصُّ، وَهُوَ : مَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ المُنْفَصِلَةُ مَانِعَتَهُمَا. الثَّانِي: مَانِعُ الجَمْعِ فَقَط، وَهُوَ : مَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ المُنْفَصِلَةُ مَانِعَةَ الجَمْعِ فَقَط. الثَّالِثُ : مَانِعُ الخُلُوِّ فَقَط، وَهُوَ : مَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ المُنْفَصِلَةُ مَانِعَةَ الخُلُوِّ فَقَط. وَقَدْ بَيَّنَ المُصَنِّفُ كَيفيَّةَ إِنْتَاحِ كُلِّ مِنْ هَذِهِ الأَقْسَامِ عَلَى التَّرْتِيبِ المَذْكُورِ، فَذَكَرَ لِلأَوَّلِ أَرْبَعَ نَتَائِجَ، وَلِكُلٍّ مِنَ الثَّانِي وَالثَّالِثِ نَتِيجَتَيْنِ، كَمَا سَيَأْتِي بَيَانُهُ.
قَوْلُهُ : (فَوَضْعُ ذَا يُنْتِجُ رَفْعَ ذَاكَ) أَيْ: فَإِنْبَاتُ أَحَدِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ يُنْتِجُ نَفْي الآخَرِ; لأَنَّهُ يَمْتَنِعُ اجْتِمَاعُهُمَا، فَإِذَا قُلْتَ مَثَلاً: «العَدَدُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ زَوْجاً وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ فَرْداً، لَكِنَّهُ زَوْجٌ أَنْتَجَ : أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِفَرْدِ»، أَوْ قُلْتَ : لَكِنَّهُ فَرْدٌ» أَنْتَجَ : «أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِزَوْجٍ ». وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَالعَكْسُ كَذَا) أَيْ: رَفْعُ ذَا يُنْتِجُ وَضْعَ ذَاكَ، فَرَفْعُ أَحَدِهِمَا يُنْتِجُ وَضْعَ الْآخَرِ; لِأَنَّهُ يَمْتَنِعُ ارْتِفَاعُهُمَا; فَإِذَا قُلْتَ فِي المِثَالِ المَذْكُورِ : لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِزَوْجٍ أَنْتَجَ: «أَنَّهُ فَرْدٌ»، أَوْ قُلْتَ: «لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِفَرْدٍ» أَنْتَجَ : «أَنَّهُ زَوْجُ». وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ لَا إِيطَاءَ فِي هَذِهِ القَافِيَةِ; لِاخْتِلَافِ المَعْنَى المُسْتَعْمَلُ فِيهِ اسْمُ الإِشَارَةِ فِي الشَّطْرِ الأَوَّلِ وَالشَّطْرِ الثَّانِي.

(105) وَذَاكَ فِي الأَخَصَّ، ثُمَّ إِنْ يَكُنْ مَانِعَ جَمْعِ فَبِوَضْعِ ذَا زُكِنْ (106) رَفْعُ لِذَاكَ دُونَ عَكْسٍ، وَإِذَا مَانِعَ رَفْعِ كَانَ فَهْوَ عَكْسُ ذَا
قَوْلُهُ : (وَذَاكَ فِي الأَخَصٌ يَعْنِي: أَنَّ مَجْمُوعَ ذَلِكَ فِي الشَّرْطِيِّ الحَقِيقِيِّ الَّذِي هُوَ أَخَصُّ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ; بِنَاءً عَلَى مَا قَدَّمَهُ المُصَنِّفُ مِنْ أَنَّ الحَقِيقِيَّة أَخَصُّ مِنْ كُلِّ مِنْ مَانِعَةِ الجَمْعِ وَمَانِعَةِ الخُلُوِّ . قَوْلُهُ : (ثُمَّ إِنْ يَكُنْ مَانِعَ جَمْعِ ... إلخ) يُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّ «ثُمَّ» لِلتَّرْتِيبِ الذِّكْرِيِّ، وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّهَا لِلتَّرْتِيبِ فِي الشَّرَفِ; لِأَنَّ الحَقِيقِيَّ أَشْرَفُ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (فَبِوَضْعِ ذَا ... إلخ) أَيْ: فَبِوَضْعِ أَحَدِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ فُهِمَ رَفْعُ الآخَرِ ; لِأَنَّهُ يَمْتَنِعُ اجْتِمَاعُهُمَا، فَإِذَا قُلْتَ مَثَلاً : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ هَذَا الجِسْمُ أَبْيَضَ وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ أَسْوَدَ، لَكِنَّهُ أَبْيَضُ ) أَنْتَجَ: «أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِأسْوَدَ، أَوْ قُلْتَ لَكِنَّهُ أَسْوَدُ أَنْتَجَ: «أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِأَبْيَضَ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (دُونَ عَكْسِ) أَيْ : لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَمْتَنِعُ ارْتِفَاعُهُمَا، فَلَا يَنْتُجُ رَفْعُ أَحَدِهِمَا وَضْعَ الآخَرِ، فَلَوْ قُلْتَ فِي المِثَالِ المَذْكُورِ : لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِأبْيَضَ، لَمْ يُنْتِجُ : «أَنَّهُ أَسْوَدُ، وَلَوْ قُلْتَ : لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِاسْوَدَ لَمْ يُنْتِجُ : أَنَّهُ أَبْيَضُ).
(104) And if it is disjunctive: then affirming this one produces negating that one, and the converse is likewise. Al-Bajuri: His saying: (And if it is disjunctive... etc.) means: that it is a disjunctive conditional. It has been mentioned that it is either exclusive of both conjunction and disjunction together, and this is the most specific; or exclusive of conjunction only; or exclusive of disjunction only. Therefore the disjunctive syllogism has three types: First: that which excludes both, and it is the most specific, and it is that whose disjunctive conditional excludes both. Second: that which excludes conjunction only, and it is that whose disjunctive conditional excludes conjunction only. Third: that which excludes disjunction only, and it is that whose disjunctive conditional excludes disjunction only. And the author has explained the manner of conclusion of each of these types in the mentioned order, so he mentioned for the first four conclusions, and for each of the second and third two conclusions, as will come later. His saying: (affirming this one produces negating that one) means: affirming one of the two sides produces negation of the other; because their conjunction is impossible. So if you say for example: 'Number: either it is even or it is odd; but it is even,' it produces: 'it is not odd'; or you say: 'but it is odd,' it produces: 'it is not even.' And his saying: (and the converse is likewise) means: negating this one produces affirming that one; so negating one of them produces affirming the other; because their disjunction is impossible (i.e., they cannot both be absent). So if you say in the mentioned example: 'but it is not even,' it produces: 'it is odd'; or you say: 'but it is not odd,' it produces: 'it is even.' And know that there is no redundancy in this rhyme due to the difference of the meaning in which the demonstrative pronoun is used in the first hemistich and the second hemistich. (105) And that is in the most specific; then if it is exclusive of conjunction, then by affirming this one is understood (106) the negation of that one without converse; and if it is exclusive of disjunction, then it is the converse of that. His saying: (And that is in the most specific) means: that the entirety of that is in the real conditional which is more specific than others; based on what the author presented that the real is more specific than each of exclusive of conjunction and exclusive of disjunction. His saying: (then if it is exclusive of conjunction... etc.) has the possibility that 'then' is for sequential mention, and the possibility that it is for sequential honor; because the real is more honorable than others. And his saying: (by affirming this one... etc.) means: by affirming one of the two sides, the negation of the other is understood; because their conjunction is impossible. So if you say for example: 'Either this body is white or it is black; but it is white,' it produces: 'it is not black'; or you say: 'but it is black,' it produces: 'it is not white.' And his saying: (without converse) means: because their disjunction is not impossible (i.e., they can both be absent), so negating one of them does not produce affirming the other. So if you say in the mentioned example: 'but it is not white,' it does not produce: 'it is black'; and if you say: 'but it is not black,' it does not produce: 'it is white.'
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the section of al-Sullam al-Munawraq dealing with disjunctive syllogisms (qiyas sharti munfasil). Al-Bajuri first explains the three types of disjunctive conditionals: (1) exclusive disjunction (mani' al-jam' wa al-khuluww) where both cannot be true and both cannot be false; (2) exclusive of conjunction only (mani' al-jam' faqat) where both cannot be true but both can be false; (3) exclusive of disjunction only (mani' al-khuluww faqat) where both cannot be false but both can be true. He then details the valid inferences. For the first type (most specific), from affirming one side you can negate the other, and from negating one side you can affirm the other—giving four valid conclusions. He illustrates with the example of numbers: 'Number is either even or odd.' From 'it is even', conclude 'it is not odd'; from 'it is not even', conclude 'it is odd', etc. For the second type (exclusive of conjunction only), only the inference from affirming one side to negating the other is valid; negating one side does not force affirmation of the other because both can be false. He uses the example of a body: 'Either it is white or black' (assuming these are mutually exclusive in terms of being both true? Actually, a body can be neither white nor black, so it's a case of 'mani' al-jam' faqat' – they cannot both be true, but can both be false). From 'it is white', conclude 'it is not black'; but from 'it is not white', you cannot conclude 'it is black' (it could be red). For the third type (exclusive of disjunction only), the valid inference is the reverse: from negating one side, affirm the other; but affirming one side does not negate the other because both can be true. The commentary also clarifies that the demonstrative pronouns in the matn are used with different references in the two hemistichs to avoid redundancy. This lesson is crucial for understanding the distinct logical behaviors of different disjunctive relations.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a valid inference from an exclusive disjunction (mani' al-jam' wa al-khuluw)?
A) If it is known that one side is true, the other may also be true.
B) If it is known that one side is true, the other must be false.
C) If it is known that one side is false, the other may be false.
D) Both B and C are correct.

Key Takeaway
The exclusive disjunction yields four valid conclusions: two from affirmation to negation and two from negation to affirmation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using an exclusive disjunction from Islamic law: 'The contract is either valid or void.' If it is known that the contract is valid, what can you conclude? If it is known that it is not void, what can you conclude? Write both conclusions.
?
Construct an exclusive disjunction on a topic from 'Aqida (e.g., about Allah's attributes) and show the valid inferences.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Disjunction Exclusive of Conjunction Only (Mani' al-Jam' faqat)
Bajuri classifies disjunctive conditionals into three types and gives the valid conclusions for each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the disjunction that prevents both parts being true but allows both to be false, and derive the valid inference from affirmation to negation.

Core Idea
When a disjunction only prevents conjunction, affirming one side denies the other, but denying one side does not affirm the other.

The second type of disjunctive conditional prohibits the conjunction of its parts (both cannot be true) but does not prevent their disjunction (both can be false). This is typical of opposites that have a middle ground. Al-Bajuri illustrates with the example of a body being white or black: both cannot be true, but it can be neither (e.g., red). In this lesson, we will focus on this type and the single valid inference pattern: from affirmation to negation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining characteristic
In exclusive of conjunction only (mani' al-jam' faqat), both parts cannot be true together, but they can be false together.
This is the second type of disjunctive conditional.
2
Recognize the valid inference pattern
Affirming one side implies the negation of the other. Negating one side does not imply the affirmation of the other.
Only one mode is valid: from affirmation to negation.
3
Apply affirmation to negation
Given 'This body is either white or black' and 'It is white', conclude 'It is not black'.
This is the only valid inference for this type.
4
Test the invalid pattern
If we say 'It is not white', we cannot conclude 'It is black' because the body could be red (neither white nor black).
Negation does not yield affirmation.
5
Generalize to other examples
Provide another example: 'This drink is either wine or vinegar.' From 'It is wine', conclude 'It is not vinegar'; from 'It is not wine', cannot conclude it is vinegar (it could be milk).

(104) وَإِنْ يَكُنْ مُنْفَصِلاً: فَوَضْعُ ذَا يُنْتِجُ رَفْعَ ذَاكَ، وَالعَكْسُ كَذَا
الباجوري
قَوْلُهُ : (وَإِنْ يَكُنْ مُنْفَصِلاً ... إلخ) أَيْ: بِأَنْ كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّةٌ مُنْفَصِلَةٌ، وَقَدْ تَقَدَّمَ أَنَّهَا : إِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ مَانِعَةَ الجَمْعِ وَالخُلُوِّ مَعاً ، وَهَذَا القِسْمُ هُوَ الأَخَصُّ ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ مَانِعَةَ الجَمْعِ فَقَط ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ مَانِعَةَ الخُلُوِّ فَقَط : وَلِذَلِكَ كَانَ القِيَاسُ المُنْفَصِلُ ثَلَاثَةَ أَقْسَامِ : الأَوَّلُ: مَانِعُهُمَا، وَهُوَ الأَخَصُّ، وَهُوَ : مَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ المُنْفَصِلَةُ مَانِعَتَهُمَا. الثَّانِي: مَانِعُ الجَمْعِ فَقَط، وَهُوَ : مَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ المُنْفَصِلَةُ مَانِعَةَ الجَمْعِ فَقَط. الثَّالِثُ : مَانِعُ الخُلُوِّ فَقَط، وَهُوَ : مَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ المُنْفَصِلَةُ مَانِعَةَ الخُلُوِّ فَقَط. وَقَدْ بَيَّنَ المُصَنِّفُ كَيفيَّةَ إِنْتَاحِ كُلِّ مِنْ هَذِهِ الأَقْسَامِ عَلَى التَّرْتِيبِ المَذْكُورِ، فَذَكَرَ لِلأَوَّلِ أَرْبَعَ نَتَائِجَ، وَلِكُلٍّ مِنَ الثَّانِي وَالثَّالِثِ نَتِيجَتَيْنِ، كَمَا سَيَأْتِي بَيَانُهُ.
قَوْلُهُ : (فَوَضْعُ ذَا يُنْتِجُ رَفْعَ ذَاكَ) أَيْ: فَإِنْبَاتُ أَحَدِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ يُنْتِجُ نَفْي الآخَرِ; لأَنَّهُ يَمْتَنِعُ اجْتِمَاعُهُمَا، فَإِذَا قُلْتَ مَثَلاً: «العَدَدُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ زَوْجاً وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ فَرْداً، لَكِنَّهُ زَوْجٌ أَنْتَجَ : أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِفَرْدِ»، أَوْ قُلْتَ : لَكِنَّهُ فَرْدٌ» أَنْتَجَ : «أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِزَوْجٍ ». وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَالعَكْسُ كَذَا) أَيْ: رَفْعُ ذَا يُنْتِجُ وَضْعَ ذَاكَ، فَرَفْعُ أَحَدِهِمَا يُنْتِجُ وَضْعَ الْآخَرِ; لِأَنَّهُ يَمْتَنِعُ ارْتِفَاعُهُمَا; فَإِذَا قُلْتَ فِي المِثَالِ المَذْكُورِ : لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِزَوْجٍ أَنْتَجَ: «أَنَّهُ فَرْدٌ»، أَوْ قُلْتَ: «لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِفَرْدٍ» أَنْتَجَ : «أَنَّهُ زَوْجُ». وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ لَا إِيطَاءَ فِي هَذِهِ القَافِيَةِ; لِاخْتِلَافِ المَعْنَى المُسْتَعْمَلُ فِيهِ اسْمُ الإِشَارَةِ فِي الشَّطْرِ الأَوَّلِ وَالشَّطْرِ الثَّانِي.

(105) وَذَاكَ فِي الأَخَصَّ، ثُمَّ إِنْ يَكُنْ مَانِعَ جَمْعِ فَبِوَضْعِ ذَا زُكِنْ (106) رَفْعُ لِذَاكَ دُونَ عَكْسٍ، وَإِذَا مَانِعَ رَفْعِ كَانَ فَهْوَ عَكْسُ ذَا
قَوْلُهُ : (وَذَاكَ فِي الأَخَصٌ يَعْنِي: أَنَّ مَجْمُوعَ ذَلِكَ فِي الشَّرْطِيِّ الحَقِيقِيِّ الَّذِي هُوَ أَخَصُّ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ; بِنَاءً عَلَى مَا قَدَّمَهُ المُصَنِّفُ مِنْ أَنَّ الحَقِيقِيَّة أَخَصُّ مِنْ كُلِّ مِنْ مَانِعَةِ الجَمْعِ وَمَانِعَةِ الخُلُوِّ . قَوْلُهُ : (ثُمَّ إِنْ يَكُنْ مَانِعَ جَمْعِ ... إلخ) يُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّ «ثُمَّ» لِلتَّرْتِيبِ الذِّكْرِيِّ، وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّهَا لِلتَّرْتِيبِ فِي الشَّرَفِ; لِأَنَّ الحَقِيقِيَّ أَشْرَفُ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (فَبِوَضْعِ ذَا ... إلخ) أَيْ: فَبِوَضْعِ أَحَدِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ فُهِمَ رَفْعُ الآخَرِ ; لِأَنَّهُ يَمْتَنِعُ اجْتِمَاعُهُمَا، فَإِذَا قُلْتَ مَثَلاً : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ هَذَا الجِسْمُ أَبْيَضَ وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ أَسْوَدَ، لَكِنَّهُ أَبْيَضُ ) أَنْتَجَ: «أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِأسْوَدَ، أَوْ قُلْتَ لَكِنَّهُ أَسْوَدُ أَنْتَجَ: «أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِأَبْيَضَ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (دُونَ عَكْسِ) أَيْ : لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَمْتَنِعُ ارْتِفَاعُهُمَا، فَلَا يَنْتُجُ رَفْعُ أَحَدِهِمَا وَضْعَ الآخَرِ، فَلَوْ قُلْتَ فِي المِثَالِ المَذْكُورِ : لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِأبْيَضَ، لَمْ يُنْتِجُ : «أَنَّهُ أَسْوَدُ، وَلَوْ قُلْتَ : لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِاسْوَدَ لَمْ يُنْتِجُ : أَنَّهُ أَبْيَضُ).
(104) And if it is disjunctive: then affirming this one produces negating that one, and the converse is likewise. Al-Bajuri: His saying: (And if it is disjunctive... etc.) means: that it is a disjunctive conditional. It has been mentioned that it is either exclusive of both conjunction and disjunction together, and this is the most specific; or exclusive of conjunction only; or exclusive of disjunction only. Therefore the disjunctive syllogism has three types: First: that which excludes both, and it is the most specific, and it is that whose disjunctive conditional excludes both. Second: that which excludes conjunction only, and it is that whose disjunctive conditional excludes conjunction only. Third: that which excludes disjunction only, and it is that whose disjunctive conditional excludes disjunction only. And the author has explained the manner of conclusion of each of these types in the mentioned order, so he mentioned for the first four conclusions, and for each of the second and third two conclusions, as will come later. His saying: (affirming this one produces negating that one) means: affirming one of the two sides produces negation of the other; because their conjunction is impossible. So if you say for example: 'Number: either it is even or it is odd; but it is even,' it produces: 'it is not odd'; or you say: 'but it is odd,' it produces: 'it is not even.' And his saying: (and the converse is likewise) means: negating this one produces affirming that one; so negating one of them produces affirming the other; because their disjunction is impossible (i.e., they cannot both be absent). So if you say in the mentioned example: 'but it is not even,' it produces: 'it is odd'; or you say: 'but it is not odd,' it produces: 'it is even.' And know that there is no redundancy in this rhyme due to the difference of the meaning in which the demonstrative pronoun is used in the first hemistich and the second hemistich. (105) And that is in the most specific; then if it is exclusive of conjunction, then by affirming this one is understood (106) the negation of that one without converse; and if it is exclusive of disjunction, then it is the converse of that. His saying: (And that is in the most specific) means: that the entirety of that is in the real conditional which is more specific than others; based on what the author presented that the real is more specific than each of exclusive of conjunction and exclusive of disjunction. His saying: (then if it is exclusive of conjunction... etc.) has the possibility that 'then' is for sequential mention, and the possibility that it is for sequential honor; because the real is more honorable than others. And his saying: (by affirming this one... etc.) means: by affirming one of the two sides, the negation of the other is understood; because their conjunction is impossible. So if you say for example: 'Either this body is white or it is black; but it is white,' it produces: 'it is not black'; or you say: 'but it is black,' it produces: 'it is not white.' And his saying: (without converse) means: because their disjunction is not impossible (i.e., they can both be absent), so negating one of them does not produce affirming the other. So if you say in the mentioned example: 'but it is not white,' it does not produce: 'it is black'; and if you say: 'but it is not black,' it does not produce: 'it is white.'
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the section of al-Sullam al-Munawraq dealing with disjunctive syllogisms (qiyas sharti munfasil). Al-Bajuri first explains the three types of disjunctive conditionals: (1) exclusive disjunction (mani' al-jam' wa al-khuluww) where both cannot be true and both cannot be false; (2) exclusive of conjunction only (mani' al-jam' faqat) where both cannot be true but both can be false; (3) exclusive of disjunction only (mani' al-khuluww faqat) where both cannot be false but both can be true. He then details the valid inferences. For the first type (most specific), from affirming one side you can negate the other, and from negating one side you can affirm the other—giving four valid conclusions. He illustrates with the example of numbers: 'Number is either even or odd.' From 'it is even', conclude 'it is not odd'; from 'it is not even', conclude 'it is odd', etc. For the second type (exclusive of conjunction only), only the inference from affirming one side to negating the other is valid; negating one side does not force affirmation of the other because both can be false. He uses the example of a body: 'Either it is white or black' (assuming these are mutually exclusive in terms of being both true? Actually, a body can be neither white nor black, so it's a case of 'mani' al-jam' faqat' – they cannot both be true, but can both be false). From 'it is white', conclude 'it is not black'; but from 'it is not white', you cannot conclude 'it is black' (it could be red). For the third type (exclusive of disjunction only), the valid inference is the reverse: from negating one side, affirm the other; but affirming one side does not negate the other because both can be true. The commentary also clarifies that the demonstrative pronouns in the matn are used with different references in the two hemistichs to avoid redundancy. This lesson is crucial for understanding the distinct logical behaviors of different disjunctive relations.

Checkpoint
For a disjunction that is exclusive of conjunction only (mani' al-jam' faqat), which of the following inferences is valid?
A) Affirming one side allows denying the other.
B) Denying one side allows affirming the other.
C) Both A and B.
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
The exclusive of conjunction only type has exactly two valid conclusions: from affirming one disjunct to negating the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the disjunction: 'This fruit is either sweet or sour.' If it is known that the fruit is sweet, what can you conclude? If it is known that it is not sweet, can you conclude that it is sour? Why or why not?
?
In the context of halal and haram, is the statement 'This food is either halal or haram' an example of exclusive of conjunction only? Explain.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Disjunction Exclusive of Disjunction Only (Mani' al-Khuluww faqat)
Bajuri classifies disjunctive conditionals into three types and gives the valid conclusions for each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the disjunction that prevents both parts being false but allows both to be true, and derive the valid inference from negation to affirmation.

Core Idea
In a disjunction that only prevents disjunction, denying one side forces affirmation of the other, but affirming one side does not deny the other.

The third type of disjunctive conditional prohibits the disjunction of its parts (both cannot be false) but does not prevent their conjunction (both can be true). This is inclusive 'or' in modern logic. Al-Bajuri notes that this type is the converse of the second: the valid inference is from negation to affirmation. In this lesson, we will work with this type using an example from arithmetic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining characteristic
In exclusive of disjunction only (mani' al-khuluww faqat), both parts cannot be false together, but they can be true together.
This is the third type of disjunctive conditional.
2
Recognize the valid inference pattern
Negating one side implies affirming the other. Affirming one side does not imply negating the other.
Only one mode is valid: from negation to affirmation.
3
Apply negation to affirmation
Given 'Either this number is less than 10 or it is greater than 5' and 'It is not less than 10', conclude 'It is greater than 5'.
Since numbers not less than 10 are >=10, they are >5.
4
Test the invalid pattern
If we say 'It is less than 10', we cannot conclude 'It is greater than 5' because the number could be 3 (less than 10 but not greater than 5).
Affirmation does not yield negation.
5
Generalize to an Islamic example
In Fiqh, consider: 'Either this act is permissible or it is disliked.' Both can be true (e.g., divorce is permissible and disliked). From 'It is not permissible', we can conclude 'It is disliked'? Actually if it is not permissible, it could be haram, so careful. Better to use the number example for clarity.

(104) وَإِنْ يَكُنْ مُنْفَصِلاً: فَوَضْعُ ذَا يُنْتِجُ رَفْعَ ذَاكَ، وَالعَكْسُ كَذَا
الباجوري
قَوْلُهُ : (وَإِنْ يَكُنْ مُنْفَصِلاً ... إلخ) أَيْ: بِأَنْ كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّةٌ مُنْفَصِلَةٌ، وَقَدْ تَقَدَّمَ أَنَّهَا : إِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ مَانِعَةَ الجَمْعِ وَالخُلُوِّ مَعاً ، وَهَذَا القِسْمُ هُوَ الأَخَصُّ ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ مَانِعَةَ الجَمْعِ فَقَط ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ مَانِعَةَ الخُلُوِّ فَقَط : وَلِذَلِكَ كَانَ القِيَاسُ المُنْفَصِلُ ثَلَاثَةَ أَقْسَامِ : الأَوَّلُ: مَانِعُهُمَا، وَهُوَ الأَخَصُّ، وَهُوَ : مَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ المُنْفَصِلَةُ مَانِعَتَهُمَا. الثَّانِي: مَانِعُ الجَمْعِ فَقَط، وَهُوَ : مَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ المُنْفَصِلَةُ مَانِعَةَ الجَمْعِ فَقَط. الثَّالِثُ : مَانِعُ الخُلُوِّ فَقَط، وَهُوَ : مَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ المُنْفَصِلَةُ مَانِعَةَ الخُلُوِّ فَقَط. وَقَدْ بَيَّنَ المُصَنِّفُ كَيفيَّةَ إِنْتَاحِ كُلِّ مِنْ هَذِهِ الأَقْسَامِ عَلَى التَّرْتِيبِ المَذْكُورِ، فَذَكَرَ لِلأَوَّلِ أَرْبَعَ نَتَائِجَ، وَلِكُلٍّ مِنَ الثَّانِي وَالثَّالِثِ نَتِيجَتَيْنِ، كَمَا سَيَأْتِي بَيَانُهُ.
قَوْلُهُ : (فَوَضْعُ ذَا يُنْتِجُ رَفْعَ ذَاكَ) أَيْ: فَإِنْبَاتُ أَحَدِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ يُنْتِجُ نَفْي الآخَرِ; لأَنَّهُ يَمْتَنِعُ اجْتِمَاعُهُمَا، فَإِذَا قُلْتَ مَثَلاً: «العَدَدُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ زَوْجاً وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ فَرْداً، لَكِنَّهُ زَوْجٌ أَنْتَجَ : أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِفَرْدِ»، أَوْ قُلْتَ : لَكِنَّهُ فَرْدٌ» أَنْتَجَ : «أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِزَوْجٍ ». وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَالعَكْسُ كَذَا) أَيْ: رَفْعُ ذَا يُنْتِجُ وَضْعَ ذَاكَ، فَرَفْعُ أَحَدِهِمَا يُنْتِجُ وَضْعَ الْآخَرِ; لِأَنَّهُ يَمْتَنِعُ ارْتِفَاعُهُمَا; فَإِذَا قُلْتَ فِي المِثَالِ المَذْكُورِ : لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِزَوْجٍ أَنْتَجَ: «أَنَّهُ فَرْدٌ»، أَوْ قُلْتَ: «لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِفَرْدٍ» أَنْتَجَ : «أَنَّهُ زَوْجُ». وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ لَا إِيطَاءَ فِي هَذِهِ القَافِيَةِ; لِاخْتِلَافِ المَعْنَى المُسْتَعْمَلُ فِيهِ اسْمُ الإِشَارَةِ فِي الشَّطْرِ الأَوَّلِ وَالشَّطْرِ الثَّانِي.

(105) وَذَاكَ فِي الأَخَصَّ، ثُمَّ إِنْ يَكُنْ مَانِعَ جَمْعِ فَبِوَضْعِ ذَا زُكِنْ (106) رَفْعُ لِذَاكَ دُونَ عَكْسٍ، وَإِذَا مَانِعَ رَفْعِ كَانَ فَهْوَ عَكْسُ ذَا
قَوْلُهُ : (وَذَاكَ فِي الأَخَصٌ يَعْنِي: أَنَّ مَجْمُوعَ ذَلِكَ فِي الشَّرْطِيِّ الحَقِيقِيِّ الَّذِي هُوَ أَخَصُّ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ; بِنَاءً عَلَى مَا قَدَّمَهُ المُصَنِّفُ مِنْ أَنَّ الحَقِيقِيَّة أَخَصُّ مِنْ كُلِّ مِنْ مَانِعَةِ الجَمْعِ وَمَانِعَةِ الخُلُوِّ . قَوْلُهُ : (ثُمَّ إِنْ يَكُنْ مَانِعَ جَمْعِ ... إلخ) يُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّ «ثُمَّ» لِلتَّرْتِيبِ الذِّكْرِيِّ، وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنَّهَا لِلتَّرْتِيبِ فِي الشَّرَفِ; لِأَنَّ الحَقِيقِيَّ أَشْرَفُ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (فَبِوَضْعِ ذَا ... إلخ) أَيْ: فَبِوَضْعِ أَحَدِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ فُهِمَ رَفْعُ الآخَرِ ; لِأَنَّهُ يَمْتَنِعُ اجْتِمَاعُهُمَا، فَإِذَا قُلْتَ مَثَلاً : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ هَذَا الجِسْمُ أَبْيَضَ وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ أَسْوَدَ، لَكِنَّهُ أَبْيَضُ ) أَنْتَجَ: «أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِأسْوَدَ، أَوْ قُلْتَ لَكِنَّهُ أَسْوَدُ أَنْتَجَ: «أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِأَبْيَضَ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (دُونَ عَكْسِ) أَيْ : لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَمْتَنِعُ ارْتِفَاعُهُمَا، فَلَا يَنْتُجُ رَفْعُ أَحَدِهِمَا وَضْعَ الآخَرِ، فَلَوْ قُلْتَ فِي المِثَالِ المَذْكُورِ : لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِأبْيَضَ، لَمْ يُنْتِجُ : «أَنَّهُ أَسْوَدُ، وَلَوْ قُلْتَ : لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِاسْوَدَ لَمْ يُنْتِجُ : أَنَّهُ أَبْيَضُ).
(104) And if it is disjunctive: then affirming this one produces negating that one, and the converse is likewise. Al-Bajuri: His saying: (And if it is disjunctive... etc.) means: that it is a disjunctive conditional. It has been mentioned that it is either exclusive of both conjunction and disjunction together, and this is the most specific; or exclusive of conjunction only; or exclusive of disjunction only. Therefore the disjunctive syllogism has three types: First: that which excludes both, and it is the most specific, and it is that whose disjunctive conditional excludes both. Second: that which excludes conjunction only, and it is that whose disjunctive conditional excludes conjunction only. Third: that which excludes disjunction only, and it is that whose disjunctive conditional excludes disjunction only. And the author has explained the manner of conclusion of each of these types in the mentioned order, so he mentioned for the first four conclusions, and for each of the second and third two conclusions, as will come later. His saying: (affirming this one produces negating that one) means: affirming one of the two sides produces negation of the other; because their conjunction is impossible. So if you say for example: 'Number: either it is even or it is odd; but it is even,' it produces: 'it is not odd'; or you say: 'but it is odd,' it produces: 'it is not even.' And his saying: (and the converse is likewise) means: negating this one produces affirming that one; so negating one of them produces affirming the other; because their disjunction is impossible (i.e., they cannot both be absent). So if you say in the mentioned example: 'but it is not even,' it produces: 'it is odd'; or you say: 'but it is not odd,' it produces: 'it is even.' And know that there is no redundancy in this rhyme due to the difference of the meaning in which the demonstrative pronoun is used in the first hemistich and the second hemistich. (105) And that is in the most specific; then if it is exclusive of conjunction, then by affirming this one is understood (106) the negation of that one without converse; and if it is exclusive of disjunction, then it is the converse of that. His saying: (And that is in the most specific) means: that the entirety of that is in the real conditional which is more specific than others; based on what the author presented that the real is more specific than each of exclusive of conjunction and exclusive of disjunction. His saying: (then if it is exclusive of conjunction... etc.) has the possibility that 'then' is for sequential mention, and the possibility that it is for sequential honor; because the real is more honorable than others. And his saying: (by affirming this one... etc.) means: by affirming one of the two sides, the negation of the other is understood; because their conjunction is impossible. So if you say for example: 'Either this body is white or it is black; but it is white,' it produces: 'it is not black'; or you say: 'but it is black,' it produces: 'it is not white.' And his saying: (without converse) means: because their disjunction is not impossible (i.e., they can both be absent), so negating one of them does not produce affirming the other. So if you say in the mentioned example: 'but it is not white,' it does not produce: 'it is black'; and if you say: 'but it is not black,' it does not produce: 'it is white.'
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the section of al-Sullam al-Munawraq dealing with disjunctive syllogisms (qiyas sharti munfasil). Al-Bajuri first explains the three types of disjunctive conditionals: (1) exclusive disjunction (mani' al-jam' wa al-khuluww) where both cannot be true and both cannot be false; (2) exclusive of conjunction only (mani' al-jam' faqat) where both cannot be true but both can be false; (3) exclusive of disjunction only (mani' al-khuluww faqat) where both cannot be false but both can be true. He then details the valid inferences. For the first type (most specific), from affirming one side you can negate the other, and from negating one side you can affirm the other—giving four valid conclusions. He illustrates with the example of numbers: 'Number is either even or odd.' From 'it is even', conclude 'it is not odd'; from 'it is not even', conclude 'it is odd', etc. For the second type (exclusive of conjunction only), only the inference from affirming one side to negating the other is valid; negating one side does not force affirmation of the other because both can be false. He uses the example of a body: 'Either it is white or black' (assuming these are mutually exclusive in terms of being both true? Actually, a body can be neither white nor black, so it's a case of 'mani' al-jam' faqat' – they cannot both be true, but can both be false). From 'it is white', conclude 'it is not black'; but from 'it is not white', you cannot conclude 'it is black' (it could be red). For the third type (exclusive of disjunction only), the valid inference is the reverse: from negating one side, affirm the other; but affirming one side does not negate the other because both can be true. The commentary also clarifies that the demonstrative pronouns in the matn are used with different references in the two hemistichs to avoid redundancy. This lesson is crucial for understanding the distinct logical behaviors of different disjunctive relations.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Rules of Disjunctive Conditional Syllogisms
Author explains rules and examples of three types of disjunctive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a disjunctive syllogism is haqiqi, man'iat al-jam', or man'iat al-khuluw, and determine which moods (affirming or denying a disjunct) yield valid conclusions.

Core Idea
The type of disjunction determines which inferences are valid: only incompatibility, only exhaustiveness, or both.

In this passage, the author breaks down disjunctive conditional syllogisms into three types based on the relationship between the two alternatives. Understanding these types is crucial for valid reasoning in Islamic sciences, where dichotomies are common. We will learn to distinguish these types and apply the correct moods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction type
Determine whether the two alternatives are mutually exclusive (cannot both be true), exhaustive (cannot both be false), or both. This determines which moods are valid.
Haqiqi = both incompatible and exhaustive; man'iat al-jam' = only incompatible; man'iat al-khuluw = only exhaustive.
2
Determine the given mood
Check whether the premise affirms one alternative (positing) or denies one alternative (negating). This determines which operation you are trying to perform.
3
Apply the valid mood rule
For each type, apply the rule: For haqiqi, both affirmation-of-one-to-negation-of-other and negation-of-one-to-affirmation-of-other are valid. For man'iat al-jam', only affirmation-to-negation is valid. For man'iat al-khuluw, only negation-to-affirmation is valid.
Memorize the rule using the mnemonic: 'Jam' = just affirmation-to-negation; Khuluw = just negation-to-affirmation; Haqiqi = both.
4
Test with a concrete example
Apply the rule to a specific disjunction from Islamic sciences, such as 'The world is either created or uncreated' (haqiqi). If you affirm it is created, you can validly conclude it is not uncreated. If you deny it is created, you can validly conclude it is uncreated.

Chapter on the conditional. If it is disjunctive: then positing this yields negating that, and the converse is likewise. And that applies to the more specific. Then if it is a barrier to conjunction, then by positing this, the negation of that is understood, without converse. And if it is a barrier to negation, then it is the converse of that. The commentator said: Their conjunction is impossible. And know that there is no redundancy in this rhyme because of the difference in the meaning in which the demonstrative pronoun is used in the first hemistich and the second hemistich. His statement: 'and that applies to the more specific' means that all of that applies to the true conditional, which is more specific than others; based on what the author presented, that the true is more specific than both the barrier to conjunction and the barrier to emptiness. His statement: 'Then if it is a barrier to conjunction... etc.' It is possible that 'thumma' is for sequential mention, and it is possible that it is for sequential honor, because the true is more honorable than others. And his statement: 'then by positing this... etc.' means: by positing one of the two extremes, the negation of the other is understood, because their conjunction is impossible. So if you say, for example: 'This body is either white or black, but it is white,' it yields: 'It is not black'; or you say: 'But it is black,' it yields: 'It is not white.' And his statement: 'without converse' means: because their negation is not impossible, so negating one does not yield positing the other. So if you said in the mentioned example: 'But it is not white,' it would not yield: 'It is black'; and if you said: 'But it is not black,' it would not yield: 'It is white.' His statement: 'And if it is a barrier to negation...' It is not hidden that 'kāna' is postponed from an advance, and the original is: 'And if it is a barrier to negation.' And his statement: 'then it is the converse of that' means: the converse of the barrier to conjunction. So by negating one of the two extremes, the positing of the other is understood, without the converse. So if you say, for example: 'This: either it is not-white or it is not-black, but it is not not-white,' it yields: 'It is not-black'; or you say: 'But it is not not-black,' it yields: 'It is not-white.' Contrary to if you said: 'But it is not-white,' for it does not yield: 'It is not not-black'; and contrary to if you said: 'But it is not-black,' for it does not yield: 'It is not not-white.' It yields in the first case because their negation is impossible, and it does not yield in the second case because their conjunction is not impossible.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of disjunctive conditional syllogisms (qiyās istithnā'ī munfaṣil). It begins with the matn lines that state the general rule: for a disjunctive conditional, positing one disjunct yields negating the other, and the converse (negating one yields positing the other) is also true. However, this full converse applies only to the 'more specific' type, which is the haqiqi (true disjunction) that is both incompatible and exhaustive. Then the matn distinguishes two sub-types: man'iat al-jam' (incompatibility alone) and man'iat al-khuluw (exhaustiveness alone). For man'iat al-jam', positing one yields negating the other, but negating one does not yield positing the other (no converse). For man'iat al-khuluw, negating one yields positing the other, but positing one does not yield negating the other (the converse of man'iat al-jam'). The commentator al-Bajuri expands on each case with examples. First, for the haqiqi (both properties), he gives the example of number: either even or odd. If you affirm one, you can deny the other; if you deny one, you can affirm the other. This works because the two cannot both be true together (incompatibility) and cannot both be false together (exhaustiveness). Next, for man'iat al-jam', he gives the example of a body being white or black (assuming it cannot be both white and black at the same time). If you affirm it is white, you can deny it is black, but if you deny it is white, you cannot affirm it is black because it could be some other color. Finally, for man'iat al-khuluw, he gives an example using negated terms: either it is not-white or it is not-black. This means the two cannot be false together (i.e., it cannot be both white and black). In this case, if you deny one (e.g., say it is white), you cannot affirm the other (it is black) because both can be true? Actually the example is carefully constructed: the disjunction 'it is either not-white or not-black' means it cannot be both white and black (since that would make both components false). So if you affirm it is white (i.e., deny not-white), you cannot conclude it is black because it could be white and also black? Wait, careful: The example says: 'This: either it is not-white or it is not-black, but it is not not-white' yields 'it is not-black'. That means: if it is not the case that it is not-white (i.e., it is white), then it must be not-black (i.e., it is black). That seems to be the converse of man'iat al-jam'? Actually the commentator is explaining man'iat al-khuluw: negation of one yields positing the other. Here 'not-white' and 'not-black' are the disjuncts. If you deny one (say you deny 'not-white', meaning it is white), then you can affirm the other ('not-black', meaning it is black). This works because if it is white, it cannot be not-black? Wait think: The disjunction says either not-white or not-black. If it is white (so not-white is false), then for the disjunction to be true, not-black must be true, so it is black. So indeed negating one disjunct (making it false) forces the other to be true. Conversely, if you affirm one disjunct (say it is not-white, meaning it is not white), you cannot conclude the other is false (i.e., you cannot say it is black) because it could be not-white but also not-black (i.e., some other color). So in man'iat al-khuluw, only the mood of negating one yields positing the other is valid. The commentator emphasizes this by saying 'without converse' meaning that positing one does not yield negating the other. Thus the passage clearly lays out the three types: haqiqi (full converse), man'iat al-jam' (only position-to-negation valid), and man'iat al-khuluw (only negation-to-position valid).

Inference Rules for the Exhaustive Disjunction Syllogism
Commentator explains the inference rule for an exhaustive disjunctive syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid inference patterns in an exhaustive (mani' raf') disjunctive syllogism and apply them to Islamic legal examples.

Core Idea
Negating one disjunct yields the other; affirming one does not negate the other.

In this passage, the commentator explains the rule for a disjunctive syllogism where the disjunction is exhaustive (it cannot be that both are false) but not exclusive (both can be true). This is different from exclusive disjunction (mani' al-jam') where affirming one negates the other. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct reasoning in legal and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of disjunction
Determine whether the disjunction is exhaustive (mani' raf'), exclusive (mani' al-jam'), or both. Here the passage explicitly states it is the 'inhibitor of raising' (mani' raf'), meaning exhaustive.
Exhaustive disjunction: both disjuncts cannot be false together.
2
Understand 'raf'' as negation
In this context, 'raf'' means raising or negating one of the disjuncts. The inference rule turns on negating one disjunct to get the other.
Do not confuse with affirmation; 'raf'' here means negation.
3
Apply the rule: negate one to get the other
Because both cannot be false, if you negate one disjunct, the other must be true. For example, negating 'not-white' gives 'not-black'.
This is valid only if the disjunction is truly exhaustive.
4
Apply the rule: affirming one does not negate the other
Since both can be true, affirming one disjunct does not force the other to be false. For example, affirming 'not-white' does not give 'not not-black' because the object might still be not-black.
This distinguishes exhaustive from exclusive disjunction.
5
Test with the provided example
Work through the four possible cases: negate first, negate second, affirm first, affirm second. Confirm which inferences are valid according to the rules.
Only the two negations are valid; the two affirmations are invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (وَإِذَا مَانِعَ رَفْعٍ كَانَ لَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ كَانَ مُؤخَّرَةٌ) مِنْ تَقْدِيمٍ، وَالأَصْلُ : وَإِذَا كَانَ مَانِعُ رَفْعِ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (فَهْوَ عَكْسُ ذَا أَيْ: عَكْسُ مَانِعِ الجَمْعِ ، فَبِرَفْعِ أَحَدٍ الطَّرَفَيْنِ زُكِنَ وَضْعُ الْآخَرِ دُونَ عَكْس؛ فَإِذَا قُلْتَ مَثَلاً : هَذَا : إِمَّا غَيْرُ أَبْيَضَ وَإِمَّا غَيْرُ أَسْوَدَ ، لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ غَيْرُ أَبْيَضَ أَنْتَجَ : «أَنَّهُ غَيْرُ أَسْوَدَ ، أَوْ قُلْتَ : لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ غَيْرُ أَسْوَدَ أَنْتَجَ : أَنَّهُ غَيْرُ أَبْيَضَ ، بِخِلَافِ مَا لَوْ قُلْتَ: «لَكِنَّهُ غَيْرُ أَبْيَضَ»، فَإِنَّهُ لَا يُنْتِجُ : «أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ غَيْرُ أَسْوَدَ ، وَبِخِلَافِ مَا لَوْ قُلْتَ: «لَكِنَّهُ غَيْرُ أَسْوَدَ فَإِنَّهُ لَا يُنْتِجُ : «أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ غَيْرُ أَبْيَضَ ) ، وَإِنَّمَا أَنْتَجَ فِي الشَّقِّ الأَوَّلِ ؛ لأَنَّهُ يَمْتَنِعُ ارْتِفَاعُهُمَا ، وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ يُنْتِجُ فِي الشَّقِّ الثَّاني ؛ لأَنَّهُ لَا يَمْتَنِعُ اجْتِمَاعُهُما .
His statement: 'And if the inhibitor of raising is, it is not hidden that the verb 'was' is deferred from fronting, and the original is: 'And if the inhibitor of raising is.' And his statement: 'So it is the opposite of that' meaning: the opposite of the inhibitor of collection. So by raising one of the two extremes, the placement of the other is known without conversion. So if you say for example: 'This: either not-white or not-black, but it is not not-white,' it produces 'that it is not-black.' Or if you say: 'But it is not not-black,' it produces 'that it is not-white.' Contrary to if you said: 'But it is not-white,' for it does not produce 'that it is not not-black,' and contrary to if you said: 'But it is not-black,' for it does not produce 'that it is not not-white.' It only produced in the first case because the raising of both is impossible, and it did not produce in the second case because the combination of both is not impossible.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage explains the rule for the disjunctive syllogism when the disjunction is exhaustive (مانع خلو) but not exclusive. The commentator clarifies that 'raising' here means negating one disjunct. When the disjunction is such that both cannot be false (exhaustive), then negating one yields the other. However, affirming one does not yield the negation of the other because both can be true. The example uses a disjunction of 'either not-white or not-black' which is exhaustive (anything is either not-white or not-black) but not exclusive (something can be both not-white and not-black, e.g., green). Thus, negating 'not-white' (i.e., it is white) gives 'not-black'; negating 'not-black' gives 'not-white'. But affirming 'not-white' does not give 'not not-black' (i.e., black), because it could still be not-black as well. This is a key point in logic: the inference rules depend on the type of disjunction.

رفع
عكس
مانع الجمع

B) Negate not-white, therefore not-black.
C) Affirm not-black, therefore not not-white.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
For an exhaustive disjunction, negating one disjunct is valid and yields the other; affirming one is invalid because both can be true.

The Syllogism

The Syllogism of the Opposite (Qiyas al-Khalf): Proof by Contradiction
Author introduces the four types of appendices to the syllogism, focusing on qiyas al-khalf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and validate a proof by contradiction (qiyas al-khalf) using the conditional reasoning structure taught in this passage.

Core Idea
The syllogism of the opposite proves a claim by showing its negation leads to an absurdity.

This passage introduces a powerful logical tool: proving a proposition by disproving its opposite. While the author of al-Sullam only lists three appendices, the commentator al-Anbabi draws attention to a fourth—qiyas al-khalf—and illustrates it with a classic kalam argument for God's eternity. By mastering this form, you will be able to construct rigorous proofs by contradiction in theological and legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition to be proved
Find the claim (matlub) that the arguer intends to establish. In the example, it is 'God is eternal' (qadim).
2
Assume the negation of the proposition
Take the contradictory of the claim as a temporary assumption. Here: 'If He were not eternal' (law lam yakun qadiman).
3
Derive consequences from the negation
From the negation, derive logical consequences until you reach an absurdity (muhal). The text says: 'If He were not eternal, He would be originated; if originated, an absurdity follows.'
4
Apply the rule that whatever leads to absurdity is false
The chain of conditionals shows the negation leads to an absurdity. By the rule 'whatever leads to an absurdity is false', the negation is false.
5
Conclude the original proposition is true
Since the negation of the proposition is false, the proposition itself must be true (by the law of excluded middle). Thus God's eternity is proven.
This step completes the proof by contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي لَوَاحِقِ القِيَاسِ الإِضَافَةُ جِنْسِيَّةٌ لَا اسْتِغْرَاقِيَّةٌ لأَنَّ المُصَنِّف لَمْ يَتَكَلَّمْ إِلَّا عَلَى ثَلَاثَةٍ مِنْهَا وَهِيَ: القِيَاسُ المُرَكَّبُ وَقِيَاسُ الاِسْتِقْرَاءِ وَقِيَاسُ التَّمْثِيلِ وَأَهْمَلَ رَابِعاً وَهُوَ: قِيَاسُ الخُلْفِ وَضَابِطُهُ: أَنْ يُسْتَدَلَّ عَلَى ثُبُوتِ المَطْلُوبِ بِإِبْطَالِ نَقِيضِهِ كَأَنْ تَقُولَ فِي مَقَام الاسْتِدْلَالِ عَلَى ثُبُوتِ قِدَمِهِ تَعَالَى: لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ قَدِيماً لَكَانَ حَادِثاً وَلَوْ كَانَ حَادِثًا لَزِمَ المُحَالُ وَمَا أَدَّى إِلَى المُحَالِ بَاطِلٌ وَإِذَا بَطَلَ ذَلِكَ بَطَلَ مَا أَدَّى إِلَيْهِ وَإِذَا بَطَلَ ذَلِكَ ثَبَتَ المَطْلُوبُ. قَالَ الْأَنْبَابِيُ: قَوْلُهُ: (وَهُوَ: قِيَاسُ الخُلْفِ) أَيِ الْبَاطِلُ، سُمِّيَ بِذَلِكَ لِكَوْنِهِ يُنْتِجُ الْبَاطِلَ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ عَدَمِ صِحَّةِ الْمَطْلُوبِ، لَا لِكَوْنِهِ بَاطِلًا. وَقِيلَ: لِأَنَّ الْمُسْتَدِلَّ بِهِ يَنْزِلُ حُجَّتَهُ خَلْفَ ظَهْرِهِ، وَيَقْصِدُ حُجَّةَ خَصْمِهِ لِيُبْطِلَهَا. وَقِيلَ: لِأَنَّهُ يَأْتِي الْمَطْلُوبَ مِنْ خَلْفِهِ أَيْ مِنْ وَرَائِهِ الَّذِي هُوَ نَقِيضُهُ. وَقِيلَ: لِأَنَّ نَتِيجَتَهُ مِمَّا يُنْبَذُ خَلْفَ الظَّهْرِ لِبُطْلَانِهَا، فَيَصِحُّ الْمَطْلُوبُ. اهـ
A chapter on the appendices of the syllogism. The addition is generic, not exhaustive, because the author only discussed three of them: compound syllogism, induction, and analogy, and omitted a fourth: the syllogism of the opposite. Its rule is: to infer the truth of the required proposition by disproving its opposite, as when you say in the context of proving the eternity of God Most High: 'If He were not eternal, He would be originated; and if He were originated, an absurdity would follow; and whatever leads to an absurdity is false; and when that is false, what leads to it is false; and when that is false, the required proposition is established.' Al-Anbabi said: His statement: 'It is the syllogism of the opposite' means the false one; it is so named because it produces falsehood on the assumption that the required proposition is not true, not because it itself is false. And it is said: because the arguer throws his argument behind his back and aims at the opponent's argument to refute it. And it is said: because it comes to the required proposition from behind, i.e., from its rear which is its opposite. And it is said: because its conclusion is thrown behind the back due to its falsehood, so the required proposition becomes correct. End.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage introduces the section on the appendices of the syllogism. The commentator notes that the author (of al-Sullam) only discusses three types: compound syllogism (قياس مركب), induction (استقراء), and analogy (تمثيل), omitting a fourth: the syllogism of the opposite (قياس الخلف). He then defines قياس الخلف as a proof by contradiction: you prove the truth of a proposition by showing that its negation leads to an absurdity. An example is given: to prove that God is eternal, assume He is not eternal; then He would be originated (since everything non-eternal is originated), and an originated thing requires a creator, leading to an infinite regress or other absurdity; thus the assumption is false, so He is eternal. This is a valid form of demonstration. The commentator by al-Anbabi then provides four etymological explanations for the name 'قياس الخلف': (1) It produces a falsehood under the assumption that the target is false; (2) The arguer discards his own argument and attacks the opponent's; (3) It approaches the target from behind (its opposite); (4) Its conclusion is thrown away as false, thereby establishing the intended truth. These explanations highlight its dialectical role.

Key Terms
قياس مركب
قياس الاستقراء
قياس التمثيل
قياس الخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Infer the truth of a proposition by disproving its opposite.
C) Infer the truth of a proposition by analogy with a similar case.
D) Infer the truth of a proposition by enumerating all its parts.

Key Takeaway
The syllogism of the opposite (qiyas al-khalf) is a valid demonstrative tool that proves a proposition by reducing its negation to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
