Understanding Qiyās al-Khalf (Proof by Contradiction)
Author defines the fourth appendage of the syllogism: qiyās al-khalf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical structure of qiyās al-khalf and construct a valid proof by contradiction using an Islamic theological example.

Core Idea
Qiyās al-khalf proves a claim by showing its opposite leads to impossibility.

In this passage, al-Bājūrī defines the fourth appendage of the syllogism: qiyās al-khalf (syllogism per impossibile). This method, also known as proof by contradiction, is a powerful tool in kalām and uṣūl al-fiqh. The author of al-Sullam omitted it, but al-Bājūrī restores it with a clear rule and a theological example proving God's eternity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logical structure
The passage gives a rule: prove a proposition by invalidating its contradictory. Recognize that this is a form of reductio ad absurdum. The argument does not directly prove the conclusion; instead, it shows that the opposite leads to an impossibility.
This method is also called proof by contradiction.
2
Examine the example
The example proves God's eternity (qidam). Break down the steps: Assume the opposite (God is not eternal). Derive that He would be originated (ḥādith). Derive that origination leads to an impossibility (muḥāl). Then, since whatever leads to impossibility is false, the assumption is false, so the original claim is true.
The impossibility is left implicit but is based on kalām principles.
3
Formalize the key premises
The argument uses a chain: If not eternal then originated. If originated then impossible. Therefore, if not eternal then impossible. The principle 'whatever leads to impossibility is false' gives: if not eternal is false, then eternal is true.
This shows the argument is valid if the premises are accepted.
4
Check the definition's completeness
The definition of qiyās al-khalf is given by its rule: inferring the truth by invalidating the contradictory. Check if this covers all instances: any argument that proves a claim by showing its negation leads to absurdity fits. It excludes other syllogisms because they don't use contradiction.
Al-Bājūrī's definition is a rasm (descriptive definition) rather than a hadd (essential definition).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي لَوَاحِقِ القِيَاسِ. الإِضَافَةُ جِنْسِيَّةٌ، لَا اسْتِغْرَاقِيَّةٌ لأَنَّ المُصَنِّفَ لَمْ يَتَكَلَّمْ إِلَّا عَلَى ثَلَاثَةٍ مِنْهَا؛ وَهِيَ: القِيَاسُ المُرَكَّبُ، وَقِيَاسُ الاِسْتِقْرَاءِ، وَقِيَاسُ التَّمْثِيلِ؛ وَأَهْمَلَ رَابِعاً وَهُوَ: قِيَاسُ الخُلْفِ، وَضَابِطُهُ: أَنْ يُسْتَدَلَّ عَلَى ثُبُوتِ المَطْلُوبِ بِإِبْطَالِ نَقِيضِهِ؛ كَأَنْ تَقُولَ فِي مَقَامِ الاسْتِدْلَالِ عَلَى ثُبُوتِ قِدَمِهِ تَعَالَى: لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ قَدِيماً لَكَانَ حَادِثاً، وَلَوْ كَانَ حَادِثًا لَزِمَ المُحَالُ، وَمَا أَدَّى إِلَى المُحَالِ بَاطِلٌ، وَإِذَا بَطَلَ ذَلِكَ بَطَلَ مَا أَدَّى إِلَيْهِ، وَإِذَا بَطَلَ ذَلِكَ ثَبَتَ المَطْلُوبُ.
A chapter on the appendices of the syllogism. The addition [of the word 'syllogism'] is generic, not comprehensive, because the author only discussed three of them: the composite syllogism, the inductive syllogism, and the analogical syllogism; and he omitted a fourth, which is the syllogism of the opposite (qiyās al-khalf). Its rule is that the truth of the desired conclusion is inferred by invalidating its contradictory; as when you say, in the context of proving His eternity, exalted is He: 'If He were not eternal, He would be originated; and if He were originated, an impossibility would follow; and whatever leads to impossibility is false; and when that is false, what leads to it is false; and when that is false, the desired conclusion is established.'
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth appendage of the syllogism, qiyās al-khalf (syllogism per impossibile), which the author of al-Sullam omitted. The commentator explains that the author only discussed three appendages: composite, inductive, and analogical. Qiyās al-khalf is defined by its rule: it proves a proposition by invalidating its opposite. The example given is from theology: proving God's eternity. One argues: if God were not eternal, He would be originated; if originated, an absurdity follows; anything leading to absurdity is false; therefore, the negation of the desired conclusion is false, so the conclusion (God's eternity) is true. This is a form of proof by contradiction.

D) An analogical argument transferring a ruling from one case to another

Key Takeaway
Qiyās al-khalf is a proof by contradiction that establishes a proposition by showing its negation leads to an impossibility, and it is a vital method in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

The Syllogism

Composite Syllogism: Definition and Construction
Definition and construction method of the composite syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a composite syllogism by linking simple syllogisms through the method of qalb al-natīja (flipping the conclusion).

Core Idea
A composite syllogism chains proofs: the conclusion of one becomes the premise of the next.

So far, we have studied simple syllogisms – single arguments with two premises and a conclusion. But many proofs in Islamic sciences require multiple steps: we need to chain syllogisms together. This lesson introduces the composite syllogism (al-qiyās al-murakkab), which does exactly that. The commentator explains how to identify it and, crucially, how to build one by converting the conclusion of one syllogism into a premise for another.

Guided Path
1
Define the composite syllogism
Locate the definition in the text: a composite syllogism is a syllogism composed of more than one 'proof' (ḥujja), where each proof is itself a simple syllogism. Note that the composition can be explicit or even potential (bi al-quwwa).
The term 'ḥujja' here is synonymous with syllogism.
2
Identify the components
A composite syllogism contains at least two simple syllogisms. Their conclusions are called 'separate conclusions' (mafṣūl al-natā'ij) when they are not yet linked. Recognise that the conclusions of the component syllogisms are the material that will be reused.
3
Apply the flipping operation (qalb al-natīja)
The verse instructs: 'flip a conclusion in it as its premise.' This means you take the conclusion of one simple syllogism and use it as a premise in the next. The word 'flip' (قلب) implies making the conclusion take the role of a premise. Practise this by writing out a simple syllogism and then using its conclusion as a premise in a second syllogism.
The flipping can be virtual (taqdīran) if the conclusion is already in the form of a premise.
4
Chain the syllogisms onward
After flipping, combine the new premise (the former conclusion) with another premise to derive a new conclusion. Repeat this process 'ilā halumma jarra' – continuously onward – until the desired final conclusion is reached. The expression indicates indefinite repetition.
The phrase 'halumma jarra' idiomatically means 'and so on'.

وَأَلْ في «القِيَاسِ لِلعَهْدِ، وَالمَعْهُودُ: القِيَاسُ البَسِيطُ، وَإِلَّا لَمْ يَصِحَ جَعْلُ القِيَاسِ المُرَكَّبِ مِنْ جُمْلَةِ اللَّوَاحِقِ. قَوْلُهُ: (وَمِنْهُ مَا يَدْعُونَهُ: «مُرَكَّبَا») أَيْ: وَمِنَ القِيَاسِ مِنْ حَيْثُ هُوَ مَا يُسَمُّونَهُ: «قِيَاساً مُرَكَّباً». وَقَوْلُهُ: (لِكَوْنِهِ مِنْ حُجَجٍ قَدْ رُكْبَا) أَيْ: وَلَوْ بِالقُوَّةِ؛ كَمَا فِي مَفْصُولِ النَّتَائِجِ، وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ الحُجَجَ جَمْعُ: «حُجَّةٍ) وَهِيَ: القِيَاسُ، لَكِنَّ المُرَادَ بِالجَمْعِ مَا فَوْقَ الوَاحِدِ. قَوْلُهُ: (فَرَكْبَتُهُ) جَوَابُ الشَّرْطِ الَّذِي بَعْدَهُ عَلَى مَذْهَبِ الكُوفِيِّينَ، وَدَلِيلُ جَوَابِهِ عَلَى مَذْهَبِ البَصْرِيِّينَ. قَوْلُهُ: (وَاقْلِبْ نَتِيْجَةٌ ... إلخ) أَيْ: وَلَوْ تَقْدِيراً؛ كَمَا فِي مَفْصُولِ النَّتَائِجِ. قَوْلُهُ: (بِهِ) أَيْ: فِيهِ، وَ البَاءُ بِمَعْنَى: فِي». قَوْلُهُ: (مُقَدِّمَهُ) مَعْمُولُ ثَانِ لِقَوْلِهِ: «اقْلِبْ»؛ لِتَضْمِينِهِ مَعْنَى: اجْعَلْ». قَوْلُهُ: ( بِأُخْرَى) أَيْ: مَعَ أُخْرَى، فَـ «البَاءُ» بِمَعْنَى: مَعَ». قَوْلُهُ: (إِلَى هَلُمَّ جَرَّا) اعْلَمْ أَنَّ هَلُمَّ فِي الأَصْلِ تُسْتَعْمَلُ لِطَلَبِ الإِقْبَالِ، ثُمَّ اسْتُعِيرَتْ لِطَلَبِ الاسْتِمْرَارِ، وَالجَرُّ) فِي الأَصْلِ مَصْدَرُ جَرَّهُ»: إِذَا سَحَبَهُ، وَالمُرَادُ مِنْهُ هُنَا: الاسْتِمْرَارُ، وَإِلَى فِي كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ مُقَدَّرَةُ الدُّخُولِ عَلَى مَحْذُوفٍ مَوْصُوفٍ بِقَوْلٍ مَحْذُوفٍ، فَكَأَنَّهُ قَالَ: «إِلَى أَمْرٍ يُقَالُ فِيهِ لِيَسْتَمِرَّ عَلَى ذَلِكَ اسْتِمْرَاراً».
And the [word] 'al-' in 'al-qiyās' is for the specific reference, and the usual referent is the simple syllogism; otherwise, it would not be valid to include the composite syllogism among the appendices. His saying: 'And from it is what they call composite' means: from the syllogism as such, what they name 'composite syllogism'. His saying: 'because it is composed of proofs' means: even potentially, as in the separate conclusions. It is obvious that 'proofs' is plural of 'proof' which is the syllogism, but the meaning of the plural is more than one. His saying: 'then you composed it' is the answer of the conditional that follows according to the Kufan school, and evidence for the answer according to the Basran school. His saying: 'and flip a conclusion... etc.' means: even virtually, as in the separate conclusions. His saying: 'in it' means: in it, and the 'bā'' is in the sense of 'in'. His saying: 'its premise' is the second object of 'flip' due to its implying the meaning of 'make'. His saying: 'with another' means: along with another, so the 'bā'' is in the sense of 'with'. His saying: 'onward continuously' know that 'halumma' originally is used for requesting approach, then it was borrowed for requesting continuation, and 'jarran' originally is the verbal noun of 'jarrahu' when he drags it, and what is meant here is continuation, and 'ilā' in the author's speech is implied entering upon an omitted thing described by an omitted saying, so it is as if he said: 'to a matter concerning which it is said: let it continue upon that continuously'.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage explains the composite syllogism (al-qiyās al-murakkab). The commentator first clarifies that the term 'al-qiyās' in the chapter heading refers specifically to the simple syllogism; otherwise, including composite syllogism among the appendices would be invalid. Then he parses the matn verse: 'And from it (i.e., from the syllogism) is what they call composite.' The reason it is called composite is that it is composed of multiple proofs (ḥujaj), even if only potentially, as when the conclusions are separate (mafṣūl al-natā'ij). The term 'proofs' (ḥujaj) is the plural of ḥujja, which here means syllogism, and the plural indicates more than one. The verse then instructs: 'Then you compose it if you want to know it, and flip a conclusion in it as its premise.' This means: to construct a composite syllogism, take the conclusion of one syllogism and make it a premise of another. This flipping can be done virtually, as in the case of separate conclusions. The word 'in it' (bihi) means 'in it' (fīhi). The phrase 'its premise' (muqaddimahu) is the second object of 'flip' because 'flip' implies 'make'. Then: 'From combining it with another, a conclusion follows, onward continuously.' This describes the chain: you take a conclusion and combine it with another premise to get another conclusion, and repeat. The expression 'ilā halumma jarran' is explained: 'halumma' originally means 'come' (request to approach), but here it is used metaphorically for 'continue'; 'jarran' is from 'jarra' (to drag), meaning continuation. So the phrase means 'continuously onward until the desired conclusion is reached.'

حجة
قلب النتيجة

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Mutassil and Mafṣūl al-Nata'ij in Compound Syllogisms
Author defines mutassil and mafṣūl al-nata'ij with examples and their equivalence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mutassil al-nata'ij and mafṣūl al-nata'ij in compound syllogisms and identify which type an argument chain belongs to.

Core Idea
The key difference is whether intermediate conclusions are stated or omitted.

In this lesson, we examine two types of compound syllogism (qiyas murakkab) defined by how the conclusions are presented. The author defines mutassil al-nata'ij (connected conclusions) where intermediate conclusions are written, and mafṣūl al-nata'ij (disconnected conclusions) where they are omitted. Both are shown to be equivalent in yielding the final result. Understanding this distinction helps in parsing complex argument chains in Islamic logic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus
Both mutassil and mafṣūl are types of qiyas murakkab (compound syllogism). Recognize that they belong to this broader category.
2
Extract the differentia
Mutassil explicitly states the intermediate conclusion; mafṣūl omits it. This is the sole distinguishing feature.
3
Examine the examples
For mutassil: 'Every human is an animal, every animal is sentient, so every human is sentient; and every human is sentient, every sentient is growing, so every human is growing.' For mafṣūl: 'Every human is an animal, every animal is sentient, every sentient is growing...' without writing the intermediate conclusions.
These are standard logical examples used in Islamic logic texts.
4
Note the subtlety about repetition
In mutassil, the intermediate conclusion appears both as a conclusion and as a premise for the next syllogism. Some scholars (e.g., Ibn Ya'qub) argue it is mentioned only once as a conclusion, but the author prefers the view that it is stated twice based on an earlier statement.
This does not affect logical equivalence.
5
Confirm equivalence
Both types yield the same final result. The matn states 'كل سوَى' (all are equal). The choice is merely stylistic.

مُتَّصِلَ النَّتَائِج الَّذِي حَوَى يَكُونُ، أَوْ مَفْصُولَهَا كُلٌّ سَوَا. قَوْلُهُ : (مُتَّصِلَ النَّتَائِحِ الَّذِي حَوَى ... إلخ) لَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ مُتَّصِلَ النَّتَائِحِ وَمَفْصُولَهَا، قِسْمَانِ مِنَ القِيَاسِ المُرَكَّبِ : (۱) وَالأَوَّلُ هُوَ : مَا ذُكِرَتْ فِيهِ نَتَائِجُهُ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ حَسَّاسٌ، فَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَسَّاسٌ، وَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَسَّاسٌ، وَكُلُّ حَسَّاسٍ نَامٍ، فَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَامٍ، ... وَهَكَذَا، وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَ بِذَلِكَ؛ لِوَصْلِ نَتَائِجِهِ بِمُقَدِّمَاتِهِ. (٢) وَالثَّانِي هُوَ : مَا لَمْ تُذْكَرْ فِيهِ نَتَائِجُهُ؛ نَحْوُ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ حَسَّاسٌ، وَكُلُّ حَسَّاسٍ نَامٍ، .. وَهَكَذَا، وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَ بِذَلِكَ؛ لِفَصْلِ نَتَائِجِهِ عَنْ مُقَدِّمَاتِهِ؛ كَذَا يُؤْخَذُ مِنْ كَلَامِ الشَّيْخِ المَلَّوِيِّ. وَمُقْتَضَاهُ : أَنَّ النَّتِيجَةَ تُذْكَرُ فِي القِسْمِ الأَوَّلِ مَرَّتَيْنِ : مَرَّةً نَتِيجَةً، وَمَرَّةً مُقَدِّمَةً، وَالَّذِي يُفِيدُهُ كَلَامُ ابْنِ يَعْقُوبَ أَنَّهَا تُذْكَرُ فِيهِ مَرَّةً وَاحِدَةً نَتِيجَةً؛ نَحْوُ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ حَسَّاسٌ، فَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَسَّاسٌ، وَكُلُّ حَسَّاسٍ نَامٍ، فَكُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ نَامٍ ... وَهَكَذَا. قَالَ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ : وَالإِنْصَافُ أَنَّ هَذَا أَوْجَهُ وَأَنْسَبُ بِجَعْلِ مُتَّصِلِ النَّتَائِجِ قِيَاساً وَاحِداً بِحَسَبِ الظَّاهِرِ. اهـ. لَكِنَّ الأَوَّلَ هُوَ الَّذِي يَقْتَضِيهِ قَوْلُ المُصَنِّفِ فِيمَا تَقَدَّمَ: وَاقْلُبْ نَتِيجَةً ... إلخ، فَلْيُتَنَبَّهْ. قَوْلُهُ : (كُلُّ سَوَا) أَيْ : كُلٌّ مِنْ مُتَّصِلِ النَّتَائِحِ وَمَفْصُولِهَا سَوَاءٌ فِي إِفَادَةِ المَطْلُوبِ.
Connected conclusions, that which includes, it is, or their disconnected ones all equal. His saying: (Connected conclusions that includes... etc.) It is not hidden that connected conclusions and their disconnected ones are two types of compound syllogism: (1) The first is: those whose conclusions are mentioned; e.g., Every human is an animal, and every animal is sentient, so every human is sentient, and every human is sentient, and every sentient is growing, so every human is growing, ... and so on. It is named thus for connecting its conclusions with its premises. (2) The second is: those whose conclusions are not mentioned; e.g., Every human is an animal, and every animal is sentient, and every sentient is growing, ... and so on. It is named thus for separating its conclusions from its premises. Thus it is taken from the words of Sheikh al-Mallawi. And its implication: that the conclusion is mentioned in the first type twice: once as conclusion and once as premise. What the statement of Ibn Ya'qub conveys is that it is mentioned once as conclusion; e.g., Every human is an animal, and every animal is sentient, so every human is sentient, and every sentient is growing, so every human is growing, ... and so on. Some investigators said: The truth is that this is more appropriate and more suitable for making connected conclusions a single syllogism in appearance. End. But the first is what is necessitated by the author's earlier statement: 'And invert the conclusion... etc.' so be alert. His saying: (all equal) meaning: each of connected conclusions and their disconnected ones is equal in yielding the sought result.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage defines two types of compound syllogism: mutassil al-nata'ij (connected conclusions) and mafṣūl al-nata'ij (disconnected conclusions). The author first cites the matn line 'Connected conclusions, that which includes, it is, or their disconnected ones all equal.' Then the commentary explains that these are two divisions of compound syllogism (qiyas murakkab). The first type (mutassil) is where the conclusion is explicitly stated after each pair of premises, and the same conclusion may serve as a premise for the next step. The example given uses a chain: human → animal → sentient → growing. The second type (mafṣūl) omits the intermediate conclusions, listing only the premises in sequence. The naming reflects whether conclusions are connected to (wusila) or separated from (fusila) the premises. The commentary then discusses a subtle point: in the mutassil definition, the conclusion is mentioned twice (first as conclusion, then as premise), but Ibn Ya'qub holds it is mentioned only once as conclusion, which some investigators consider more appropriate for treating the whole chain as a single syllogism. However, the author notes that the first view (repeating the conclusion) is implied by an earlier statement of the author (al-Sullam). Finally, both types are deemed equal in yielding the desired result.

Key Terms
مُتَّصِل النَّتَائِج
مَفْصُول النَّتَائِج
قِيَاس مُرَكَّب

Key Takeaway
Mutassil and mafṣūl are two types of compound syllogism differing only in whether intermediate conclusions are stated; both are logically equivalent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Divisions of Proof and Definition of Oratory
Bajuri explains the division of hujja into transmitted and rational, then defines the five rational proofs with focus on oratory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish transmitted proof from rational proof and identify the definition of oratory (khitaba) along with its two types of premises.

Core Idea
Proof defeats the opponent; it is either transmitted or rational; oratory persuades using accepted or probable premises.

In this passage, Bajuri explains the overarching division of proof (hujja) into transmitted (naqliyya) and rational ('aqliyya), and then focuses on the rational proofs, especially oratory (khitaba). Understanding how proofs are classified helps you evaluate arguments in Islamic sciences: some rely on revelation, others on reason, and oratory uses premises that are accepted or probable to persuade an audience.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main divisions of proof
Bajuri states that hujja divides into naqliyya (transmitted) and 'aqliyya (rational). Note that the total number of proof types is six: one transmitted, and five rational.
Transmitted proof depends on the Book, Sunnah, or consensus, even if the intellect perceives it.
2
Distinguish the source of premises for each division
Transmitted proof uses premises from revelation or consensus (explicitly or by derivation). Rational proof relies solely on the intellect. But demonstration (burhan) can sometimes use transmitted premises, though logicians usually restrict it to purely rational premises.
Bajuri answers an objection about demonstration being classified as rational despite possibly having transmitted premises.
3
Recognize oratory as one of the five rational proofs
The five rational proofs are well-known to logicians; oratory (khitaba) is one of them. It is defined by the type of premises it uses.
4
Analyze the definition of oratory
Oratory is composed of accepted premises (maqbula) or probable premises (maznuna). Accepted premises are taken from a credible speaker; probable premises are more likely true than false.
The name 'oratory' comes from its purpose: to persuade the audience, as orators do.
5
Examine the examples of oratory
First example: 'Righteous action necessitates success' (accepted premise) leads to 'Righteous action ought not to be neglected'. Second example: 'So-and-so wanders at night with weapons' (probable premise) leads to 'So-and-so is a thief'. Notice how the premises are not certain but serve to persuade.
Both examples are categorical syllogisms, but the focus here is on the nature of the premises, not the form.

أَقْسَامُ الحُجَّةِ. قَوْلُهُ : (أَقْسَامُ الحُجَّةِ وَجُمْلَتُهَا سِتَّةٌ) لأَنَّ المُصَنِّفَ سَيَذْكُرُ أَنَّهَا تَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى : نَقْلِيَّةٍ، وَعَقْلِيَّة، وَأَنَّ الثَّانِيَةَ تَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى خَمْسَةِ أَقْسَامٍ، وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتِ الحُجَّةُ بِذَلِكَ؛ لأَنَّ المُتَمَسِّكَ بِهَا يَحُجُ خَصْمَهُ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَحُجَّةٌ) مُبْتَدَاً ، وَالمُسَوِّعُ لِلابْتِدَاءِ بِهَا : قَصْدُهُ الجِنْسَ أَوِ التَّفْصِيلَ . وَقَوْلُهُ : (نَقْلِيةٌ) نِسْبَةٌ لِلنَّقْلِ ؛ لِاسْتِنَادِهَا إِلَيْهِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ العَقْلُ هُوَ المُدْرِكُ لَهَا، وَهِيَ : مَا كَانَ كُلُّ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَتَيْهَا أَوْ إِحْدَاهُمَا مِنَ الكِتَابِ أَوِ السُّنَّةِ، أو الإجماع؛ تَصْرِيحاً أَوِ اسْتِنْبَاطاً». فَإِنْ قِيلَ : سَيَجْعَلُ المُصَنِّفُ البُرْهَانَ مِنْ أَقْسَامِ العَقْلِيَّةِ، مَعَ أَنَّهُ قَدْ يَتَرَكَّبُ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ كِلْتَاهُمَا أَوْ إِحْدَاهُمَا نَقْلِيَّةٍ . أُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ جَعْلِ البُرْهَانِ مِنْ أَقْسَامِ العَقْلِيَّةِ أَنَّهُ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا عَقْلِيًّا، عَلَى أَنَّهُ قَدْ يُقَالُ بِاخْتِصَاصِ البُرْهَانِ عِنْدَ المَنَاطِقَةِ بِمَا مُقَدِّمَتَاهُ عَقْلِيَّتَانِ؛ لِأَنَّهُمْ إِنَّمَا يَبْحَثُونَ عَنِ العَقْلِيَّاتِ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (عَقْلِيَّه) نِسْبَةٌ لِلعَقْلِ ؛ لِاسْتِنَادِهَا إِلَيْهِ. قَوْلُهُ : (أَقْسَامُ هَذِي) أَي : العَقْلِيَّةِ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (خَمْسَةٌ جَلِيَّهُ) أَيْ : وَاضِحَةٌ عِنْدَ أَهْلِ المَنْطِقِ . قَوْلُهُ : (خَطَابَةٌ) هِيَ - بِفَتْحِ الخَاءِ : مَا رُكَّبَ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَاتٍ مَقْبُولَةٍ، أَوْ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَاتٍ مَظْنُونَةٍ. فَمِثَالُ الأَوَّلِ : أَنْ تَقُولَ: «العَمَلُ الصَّالِحُ يُوجِبُ الفَوْزَ، وَكُلُّ مَا كَانَ كَذَلِكَ لَا يَنْبَغِي إِهْمَالُهُ» يَنْتُجُ : «أَنَّ العَمَلَ الصَّالِحَ لَا يَنْبَغِي إِهْمَالُهُ». وَمِثَالُ الثَّانِي : أَنْ تَقُولَ : فُلَانٌ يَطُوفُ بِاللَّيْلِ بِالسِّلَاحِ، وَكُلُّ مَنْ كَانَ كَذَلِكَ مُتَلَصِّصٌ يَنْتُجُ : «أَنَّ فُلاناً مُتَلَصِّص». وَسُمِّيَتْ بِذَلِكَ ؛ لِأَنَّ القَصْدَ مِنْهَا تَرْغِيبُ المُخَاطَبِ فِيمَا يَفْعَلُهُ، كَمَا يَفْعَلُهُ الخُطَبَاءُ.
The sections of the proof. His statement: 'The sections of the proof, and their total is six' because the author will mention that they divide into: transmitted and rational, and that the second divides into five sections. And it is only called 'proof' because the one who adheres to it defeats his opponent. His statement: 'And a proof' is the subject, and what permits beginning with it is his intention of the genus or the detail. And his statement: 'transmitted' is a relation to transmission, because of its reliance upon it, even if the intellect is the perceiver of it. And it is: that which both of its premises or one of them are from the Book, the Sunnah, or consensus—explicitly or by derivation. If it is said: The author will make demonstration among the rational sections, even though it may be composed of two premises both or one of which are transmitted. The answer: It is not necessary from making demonstration among the rational sections that it is only rational; moreover it may be said that demonstration according to the logicians is specific to that whose two premises are rational, because they only investigate rational matters. His statement: 'rational' is a relation to the intellect, because of its reliance upon it. His statement: 'sections of these' i.e., the rational. His statement: 'five clear' i.e., clear to the people of logic. His statement: 'oratory'—with fatha on the kha—is that which is composed of accepted premises, or of probable premises. An example of the first: that you say: 'Righteous action necessitates success, and everything that is like that ought not to be neglected,' yielding: 'Righteous action ought not to be neglected.' An example of the second: that you say: 'So-and-so wanders at night with weapons, and everyone who is like that is a thief,' yielding: 'So-and-so is a thief.' And it is called thus because the intention from it is to encourage the addressee in what he does, as orators do.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage defines the divisions of proof (hujja) and then focuses on the definition of oratory (khitaba).

Bajuri begins by explaining that the total number of proof types is six, because the author (al-Akhdari in al-Sullam) will later state that proof divides into transmitted (naqliyya) and rational (aqliyya), and the rational divides into five. The term 'hujja' is used because the one who employs it defeats his opponent.

He then clarifies that the word 'hujja' in the matn is in the nominative as a subject, and the justification for beginning with it is that the author intends either the genus or detail.

Next, he defines transmitted proof: it is that in which both premises or one of them come from the Book, Sunnah, or consensus, whether explicitly or by derivation. He anticipates an objection: the author will list demonstration (burhan) among the rational proofs, yet demonstration can be composed of transmitted premises. The answer is that classifying demonstration as rational does not preclude it from sometimes using transmitted premises; however, according to logicians, demonstration is strictly reserved for arguments with both premises rational, because they primarily deal with rational matters.

He then defines rational proof as relying on the intellect. The 'five clear' sections of rational proof are well-known to logicians.

Finally, he defines oratory (khitaba) as that composed of accepted premises (maqbula) or probable premises (maznuna). He gives two examples: The first uses an accepted premise (that righteous action brings success) to conclude it should not be neglected; the second uses a probable premise (that someone wandering at night armed is likely a thief) to conclude that person is a thief. The name 'oratory' comes from its purpose: to persuade the audience, as orators do.

حجة
نقلية
عقلية
خطابة
مقبولة
مظنونة

Key Takeaway

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Limmi and Inni Demonstration: Cause and Effect
Author divides demonstration into limmi and inni based on causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a limmi and inni demonstration by analyzing the causal direction of the middle term in a categorical syllogism from Islamic sources.

Core Idea
Demonstration's power lies in the middle term: is it the real cause or just the epistemic clue?

In this passage, the author unpacks the core division of demonstration (burhan) into two types based on the nature of the middle term's causality. A limmi demonstration moves from cause to effect, while inni moves from effect to cause. Mastering this distinction sharpens your ability to evaluate whether a given proof in kalam or fiqh establishes why something is true or merely that it is true.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term in each example
In the two syllogisms (Zaid's humors and fever), locate the term that appears in both premises and is absent from the conclusion. This is the middle term (al-hadd al-awsaṭ).
In the first example, 'corrupted humors' is the middle term; in the second, 'fever' is the middle term.
2
Determine the causal direction
Ask: Is the middle term the real cause of the conclusion in the external world, or is it merely the epistemic reason we know the conclusion? The author states that in limmi, the middle term is both a real and epistemic cause; in inni, it is only epistemic.
Corrupted humors cause fever (real cause). Fever does not cause corrupted humors; rather, it is an effect.
3
Classify the demonstration type
Based on the causal direction, label the syllogism. If the middle term is a real cause of the conclusion, it is limmi (answers 'why'). If it is only an epistemic cause (inference from effect to cause), it is inni (answers 'that').
First example: limmi. Second example: inni.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Construct a similar pair of syllogisms using an Islamic ruling and its cause, e.g., the prohibition of wine due to intoxication. Identify which is limmi and which is inni.
Example: 'Nabidh is intoxicating; every intoxicant is forbidden; therefore nabidh is forbidden' is limmi. 'Nabidh is forbidden; every forbidden intoxicant is intoxicating? Not necessarily; inference from effect to cause is weaker.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مِنْ أَوَّلِيَّاتٍ، مُشَاهَدَاتِ مُجَرَّبَاتٍ ، مُتَوَاتِرَاتِ
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ البُرْهَانَ قِسْمَانِ : لِمِّيُّ ، وَإِنِّي ، وَذَلِكَ لأَنَّ الحَدَّ الوَسَطَ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ عِلَّةٌ لِلمَظْلُوبِ ذِهْناً، وَإِلَّا لَمْ يَصِحَ الاِسْتِدْلَالُ، ثُمَّ لَا يَخْلُو :
فَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ عِلَّةٌ فِي الخَارِجِ أَيضاً ؛ بِمَعْنَى : أَنَّهُ سَبَبٌ فِيهِ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : زَيْدٌ مُتَعَفِّنُ الأَخْلَاطِ ، وَكُلُّ مُتَعَفِّنِ الأَخْلَاطِ مَحْمُومٌ يَنتُجُ : زَيْدٌ مَحْمُومٌ ، فَإِنَّ تَعَفُّنَ الأَخْلَاطِ» - بِمَعْنَى : خُرُوجِ الطَّبَائِعِ عَنِ الِاسْتِقَامَةِ - عِلَّةٌ لِتُبُوتِ الحُمَى فِي الخَارِج ، كَمَا هُوَ عِلَّةٌ لَهُ فِي الذِّهْنِ، وَيُسَمَّى البُرْهَانُ حِينَئِذٍ : الِمِّيَّا» ؛ لِإِفَادَتِهِ اللمِّيَّةِ الَّتِي هِي العِلَّهُ، وَسُمِّيَتْ بِذَلِكَ ؛ لأَنَّهُ يُقَالَ فِي السُّؤَالِ عَنْهَا : «لِمَ». - وَإِمَّا أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ كَذَلِكَ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : زَيْدٌ مَحْمُومٌ ، وَكُلُّ مَحْمُومٍ مُتَعَفِّنُ الأَخْلَاطِ، يَنْتُجُ : زَيْدُ مُتَعَفِّنُ الأَخْلَاطِ، فَإِنَّ الحُمَى لَيْسَتْ عِلَّةٌ لِتُبُوتِ تَعَفُّنِ الأَخْلَاطِ فِي الخَارِجِ، بَلِ الوَاقِعُ العَكْسُ ، وَيُسَمَّى البُرْهَانُ حِينَئِذٍ : «إِنِّيَّا» ؛ لِإِفَادَتِهِ إِنْيَّةِ الحُكْم ؛ أَي تُبُوتِهِ، وَسُمِّيَ بِذَلِكَ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يُقَالُ فِيهِ : إِنْ كَذَا .
وَالحَاصِلُ : أَنَّهُ مَتَى اسْتُدِلَ بِالعِلَّةِ عَلَى المَعْلُولِ كَانَ البُرْهَانُ لِميًّا، وَمَتَى اسْتُدِلَّ بِالمَعْلُولِ عَلَى العِلَّةِ كَانَ البُرْهَانُ إِنَّا ؛ أَفَادَهُ المَلوِيُّ مَعَ زِيَادَةٍ .
قَوْلُهُ : (مِنْ) أَوَّلِيَّاتٍ ... إلخ) بَدَلٌ مِنْ قَوْلِهِ : مِنْ مُقَدِّمات ... إلخ».
From first principles, observations, experiments, recurrent reports.
Know that demonstration is two types: limmi and inni. That is because the middle term must be a cause of the sought [conclusion] in the mind; otherwise, the inference would not be valid. Then it is not free from:
- Either it is also a cause in reality, meaning it is a cause for it [the conclusion] externally; as in your saying: 'Zaid has corrupted humors, and everyone with corrupted humors has a fever,' yielding 'Zaid has a fever.' For 'corrupted humors'—meaning departure of the temperaments from balance—is a cause for the existence of fever externally, just as it is a cause for it in the mind. The demonstration is then called 'limmi' because it gives the 'limmiyya' which is the cause; and it is so named because one asks about it: 'Why?'
- Or it is not like that; as in your saying: 'Zaid has a fever, and everyone with a fever has corrupted humors,' yielding 'Zaid has corrupted humors.' For fever is not a cause for the existence of corrupted humors externally; rather, the opposite is the case. The demonstration is then called 'inni' because it gives the 'inniyya' of the judgment, i.e., its existence. It is so named because one says: 'Indeed it is so.'
The upshot: whenever one infers from the cause to the effect, the demonstration is limmi; whenever one infers from the effect to the cause, the demonstration is inni. Al-Mallawi transmitted this with addition.
His saying: 'from first principles... etc.' is a substitution for his saying: 'from premises... etc.'
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the definition of demonstration (burhan). It begins with a list of six types of certain premises from which demonstration is composed: first principles (awwaliyat), observations (mushahadat), experiments (mujarrabat), recurrent reports (mutawatirat), and by implication two more (hadsiyat and qata'iyat) mentioned elsewhere. The commentator then states that demonstration is of two types: limmi (a priori) and inni (a posteriori). The key criterion is the role of the middle term (al-hadd al-awsaṭ) in the syllogism. In any valid demonstration, the middle term must be a cause of the sought conclusion in the mind (i.e., it must be the epistemic reason for affirming the conclusion). If, in addition, the middle term is also a real cause of the conclusion in the external world, then the demonstration is called limmi (from 'lima' = why). The example given: 'Zaid has corrupted humors; everyone with corrupted humors has a fever; therefore Zaid has a fever.' Here, the corrupted humors (middle term) are both the epistemic cause (we know fever through them) and the real, physical cause of the fever. In contrast, if the middle term is only an epistemic cause but not a real cause, the demonstration is called inni (from 'inna' = indeed). The example: 'Zaid has a fever; everyone with fever has corrupted humors; therefore Zaid has corrupted humors.' Here, fever is the epistemic cause (we know the corrupted humors through it), but in reality, the corrupted humors cause the fever, not the other way around. The inferential direction is reversed: from effect to cause. The commentator summarises: inference from cause to effect = limmi; from effect to cause = inni. He attributes this explanation to al-Mallawi. Finally, he notes that the verse's listing of premise types is a substitution (badal) for an earlier phrase about premises.

برهان إني
حد وسط
علة
تعفن الأخلاط

Limmi and Inni: Cause vs. Effect in Demonstration
Al-Bajuri defines the two types of demonstration with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given demonstration as limmi or inni by identifying whether the middle term is the cause or the effect of the conclusion.

Core Idea
Limmi answers 'why' (cause→effect); Inni answers 'that' (effect→cause).

This passage introduces the two types of demonstration (burhan) in logic: limmi (a priori) and inni (a posteriori). The key distinction is the causal direction between the middle term and the conclusion. Understanding this helps you evaluate whether a proof provides the reason for a fact or merely establishes the fact itself. Let's walk through the logical structure of each type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
Locate the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. In the examples, 'corrupted humors' and 'fever' serve as middle terms.
2
Determine external causality
Ask: Is the middle term the real-world cause of the conclusion? In the first example, corrupted humors cause fever; in the second, fever does not cause corrupted humors.
External cause means the middle term is the reason the conclusion is true in reality.
3
Classify as limmi or inni
If the middle term is an external cause, the demonstration is limmi. If it is an effect (or not a cause), it is inni.
Limmi answers 'lima' (why); inni answers 'inna' (that it is).
4
Verify inference direction
Check the direction: inference from cause to effect is limmi; from effect to cause is inni.
In both cases the middle term is a mental cause of the conclusion, but only in limmi is it also an external cause.

وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ البُرْهَانَ قِسْمَانِ : لِمِّيُّ ، وَإِنِّي (۱) ، وَذَلِكَ لأَنَّ الحَدَّ الوَسَطَ لَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ عِلَّةٌ لِلمَظْلُوبِ ذِهْنا (٢)، وَإِلَّا لَمْ يَصِحَ الاِسْتِدْلَالُ، ثُمَّ لَا يَخْلُو : فَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ عِلَّةٌ فِي الخَارِجِ أَيضاً ؛ بِمَعْنَى : أَنَّهُ سَبَبٌ فِيهِ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : زَيْدٌ مُتَعَفِّنُ الأَخْلَاطِ (۳) ، وَكُلُّ مُتَعَفِّنِ الأَخْلَاطِ مَحْمُومٌ يَنتُجُ : زَيْدٌ مَحْمُومٌ ، فَإِنَّ تَعَفُّنَ الأَخْلَاطِ» - بِمَعْنَى : خُرُوجِ الطَّبَائِعِ عَنِ الِاسْتِقَامَةِ - عِلَّةٌ لِتُبُوتِ الحُمَى فِي الخَارج ، كَمَا هُوَ عِلَّةٌ لَهُ فِي الذِّهْنِ، وَيُسَمَّى البُرْهَانُ حِينَئِذٍ : الِمِّيَّا» ؛ لِإِفَادَتِهِ اللمِّيَّةِ الَّتِي هِي العِلَّهُ، وَسُمِّيَتْ بِذَلِكَ ؛ لأَنَّهُ يُقَالَ فِي السُّؤَالِ عَنْهَا : «لِمَ». - وَإِمَّا أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ كَذَلِكَ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : زَيْدٌ مَحْمُومٌ ، وَكُلُّ مَحْمُومٍ مُتَعَفِّنُ الأَخْلَاطِ، يَنْتُجُ : زَيْدُ مُتَعَفِّنُ الأَخْلَاطِ، فَإِنَّ الحُمَى لَيْسَتْ عِلَّةٌ لِتُبُوتِ تَعَفُّنِ الأَخْلَاطِ فِي الخَارِجِ، بَلِ الوَاقِعُ العَكْسُ ، وَيُسَمَّى البُرْهَانُ حِينَئِذٍ : «إِنِّيَّا» ؛ لِإِفَادَتِهِ إِنْيَّةِ الحُكْم ؛ أَي تُبُوتِهِ، وَسُمِّيَ بِذَلِكَ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يُقَالُ فِيهِ : إِنْ كَذَا (٤). وَالحَاصِلُ : أَنَّهُ مَتَى اسْتُدِلَ بِالعِلَّةِ عَلَى المَعْلُولِ كَانَ البُرْهَانُ لِميًّا، وَمَتَى اسْتُدِلَّ بِالمَعْلُولِ عَلَى العِلَّةِ كَانَ البُرْهَانُ إِنَّا ؛ أَفَادَهُ المَلوِيُّ مَعَ زِيَادَةٍ ) .
Know that demonstration is of two types: limmi and inni. That is because the middle term must be a cause for the desired [conclusion] mentally, otherwise inference would not be valid. Then it is either also a cause externally, meaning that it is a cause in reality—as in your statement: 'Zayd has corrupted humors, and every one with corrupted humors is feverish,' yielding 'Zayd is feverish'; indeed, corruption of humors (meaning deviation of natures from balance) is a cause for the existence of fever externally, just as it is a cause mentally. In this case the demonstration is called 'limmi' because it gives the 'limmiyya' which is the cause, and it is so named because in asking about it one says 'why?'—or it is not so, as in your statement: 'Zayd is feverish, and every feverish person has corrupted humors,' yielding 'Zayd has corrupted humors'; indeed, fever is not a cause for the existence of corruption of humors externally; rather, the opposite is true. In this case the demonstration is called 'inni' because it gives the 'inniyya' of the judgment, i.e., its existence, and it is so named because in it one says 'that it is so.' The upshot: whenever one infers from the cause to the effect, the demonstration is limmi; and whenever one infers from the effect to the cause, the demonstration is inni. Al-Mallawi stated this with an addition.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of demonstration (burhan) in logic: limmi (a priori) and inni (a posteriori). The author begins by stating that the middle term must be a mental cause of the conclusion; otherwise the inference is invalid. Then he explains that the middle term may or may not also be an external cause. The first case is limmi: the middle term is a cause both mentally and externally, as in the example: 'Zayd has corrupted humors; everyone with corrupted humors is feverish; therefore Zayd is feverish.' Here, the corruption of humors is the external cause of fever. The demonstration is called limmi because it answers the question 'why?' (lima). The second case is inni: the middle term is not the external cause but rather the effect, as in: 'Zayd is feverish; everyone feverish has corrupted humors; therefore Zayd has corrupted humors.' Here, fever is the effect, not the cause. This demonstration is called inni because it affirms the existence (inniyya) of the judgment. The summary: inference from cause to effect is limmi; from effect to cause is inni.

Key Terms
البرهان
اللِّمِّي
الإِنِّي
العلة

Checkpoint
In the demonstration 'Zayd has corrupted humors; everyone with corrupted humors is feverish; therefore Zayd is feverish', the middle term is 'corrupted humors'. Is this demonstration limmi or inni?
A) Limmi, because corrupted humors are the external cause of fever.
B) Inni, because the middle term is an effect of fever.
C) Limmi, because the middle term is a mental cause only.
D) Inni, because the inference goes from effect to cause.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between limmi and inni hinges on whether the middle term is the real cause of the conclusion (limmi) or an effect (inni).

The Rational View: Premises Necessarily Imply the Conclusion
Al-Bajuri defines and defends the rational view of implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rational view of implication and defend it against the objection that it restricts divine power.

Core Idea
Rational implication is logical necessity that does not limit God's omnipotence.

In this lesson, we examine the first of four views on how premises indicate the conclusion: the rational connection. Al-Bajuri defines it, distinguishes it from Mu'tazilite and philosophical views, and responds to a key objection about divine omnipotence. Understanding this view is crucial for grasping the foundations of logical demonstration in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four views listed in the matn
The base text (matn) states four views: rational, customary, generated, obligatory. Al-Bajuri confirms this and focuses on the rational view as supported.
2
Understand al-Bajuri's definition of the rational view
He says the connection is 'aqlī (rational) without generation or causation. It means the conclusion is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the premises.
3
Distinguish it from Mu'tazilite and philosophical views
The Mu'tazilites claim generation (tawallud) but actually imply rational necessity; philosophers openly hold it is rational. Al-Bajuri criticizes the Mu'tazilites for disguising their position.
4
Examine the objection and al-Bajuri's answer
Objection: If the connection is necessary, then even God cannot prevent the conclusion from following, which conflicts with His free will. Answer: The non-creation of the necessary concomitant with the entailing is impossible, and impossibility does not fall under divine power.
This mirrors kalām discussions on substance and accident.
5
Apply the principle to other rational concomitants
Al-Bajuri notes that the same logic applies to all rationally necessary pairs, such as substance and accident. If the objection were valid, no rational entailment would exist in creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۲۰) وَفِي دَلَالَةِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ عَلَى النَّتِيجَةِ خِلَافٌ آتِ: عَقْلِيٌّ، أَوْ عَادِيٌّ، أَوْ تَوَلُّدُ، أَوْ وَاجِبٌ؛ وَالأَوَّلُ المُؤَيَّدُ. قَالَ الباجوري: قَوْلُهُ : خِلَافٌ آتِ أَيْ: عَلَى أَرْبَعَةِ أَقْوَالٍ، كَمَا فَصَّلَهُ المُصَنِّفُ بَعْدُ. قَوْلُهُ : (عَقْلِيٌّ) خَبَرٌ لِمُبْتَدَأَ مَحْذُوفٍ ؛ وَالتَّقْدِيرُ : وَهَذَا الاِرْتِبَاطُ عَقْلِيٌّ . وَالمُرَادُ : عَقْلِيٌّ بِلَا تَوَلُّدٍ وَلَا تَعْلِيلٍ : لِيُغَايِرَ قَوْلَ المُعْتَزِلَةِ بِالتَّوَلُدِ، فَإِنَّهُ يَسْتَلْزِمُ أَنَّهُ عَقْلِيٌّ وَإِنْ كَانُوا يَدَّعُونَ أَنَّهُ عَادِيٌّ، وَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّهُمْ أَخَذُوا قَوْلَهُمْ بِالتَّوَلُدِ فِي هَذِهِ المَسْأَلَةِ وَفِي غَيْرِهَا مِنْ مَذْهَبِ الفَلَاسِفَةِ فِي الأَسْبَابِ الطَّبِيعِيَّةِ، وَهُوَ أَنَّهَا تُؤَثِّرُ فِي مُسَبِّبَاتِهَا بِطَبْعِهَا عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ العَقْلِيِّ عِنْدَ وُجُودِ الشَّرْطِ وَانْتِفَاءِ المَانِعِ ؛ غَايَةُ الأَمْرِ أَنَّهُمْ تَسَتَّرُوا بِتَغْيِيرِ العِبَارَةِ. وَلِيُغَايِرَ قَوْلَ الفَلَاسِفَةِ، فَإِنَّهُمْ لَا يُنْكِرُونَ أَنَّهُ عَقْلِيٌّ . وَاعْتُرِضَ هَذَا القَوْلُ : بِأَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ عَلَيْهِ أَنَّهُ لَا يُمْكِنُ تَخَلَّفُ النَّتِيجَةِ عَنِ الدَّلِيلِ، مَعَ أَنَّ ذَلِكَ فِعْلُ القَادِرِ المُخْتَارِ؛ الَّذِي إِنْ شَاءَ فَعَلَ وَإِنْ شَاءَ تَرَكَ. وَأُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ عَدَمَ خَلْقِ اللَّازِمِ مَعَ خَلْقِ المَلْزُومِ مُحَالٌ، فَلَا تَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ القُدْرَةُ، وَحِينَئِذٍ فَلَا يُنَافِي أَنَّهُ فِعْلُ القَادِرِ المُخْتَارِ ، وَهَكَذَا يُقَالُ فِي كُلِّ مُتَلَازِمَيْنِ عَقْلاً كَـ : «الجَوْهَرِ، وَالعَرَضِ»، وَلَوْ تَوَجَّهَ هَذَا الاِعْتِرَاضُ لَمْ يَثْبُتْ لَازِمٌ عَقْلِيٌّ فِي الكَائِنَاتِ . قَوْلُهُ : (أَوْ عَادِيٌّ) «أَوْ» فِي ذَلِكَ وَفِيمَا بَعْدَهُ لِتَنْوِيعِ الخِلَافِ. وَالمُرَادُ : عَادِيٌّ بِلَا تَوَلُّدٍ ؛ لِيُغَايِرَ قَوْلَ المُعْتَزِلَةِ بِالتَّوَلُدِ، فَإِنَّهُمْ يَزْعُمُونَ أَنَّهُ عَادِيٌّ، وَإِنْ كَانَ يَلْزَمُهُمْ أَنَّهُ عَقْلِيٌّ كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ. وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّهُ يُمْكِنُ عَلَى هَذَا القَوْلِ تَخَلَّفُ النَّتِيجَةِ عَنِ الدَّلِيلِ، وَصَوَّرَهُ بَعْضُهُمْ بِمَا إِذَا لَمْ يَتَفَطَّنِ الشَّخْصُ لِانْدِرَاجِ الأَصْغَرِ تَحْتَ الأَوْسَطِ، فَإِنَّهُ حِينَئِذٍ تَتَخَلَّفُ النَّتِيجَةُ عَنِ الدَّلِيلِ.
(120) And concerning the indication of the premises to the conclusion, there is a difference to come: rational, or customary, or generated, or obligatory; and the first is supported. Al-Bajuri said: His statement “difference to come” i.e., based on four statements, as the author later details. His statement: “rational” is a predicate of a deleted subject, i.e., “this connection is rational.” It is meant: rational without generation and without causation, to differentiate it from the Mu’tazilite view of generation, for it necessarily implies that it is rational even though they claim it is customary; that is because they took their view of generation in this matter and other from the philosophers’ doctrine concerning natural causes, namely that they affect their effects by their nature in the manner of rational necessity given the presence of condition and absence of impediment; the point is that they concealed themselves by changing the expression. And to differentiate it from the philosophers’ view, for they do not deny that it is rational. And this view was objected to: that it entails that the conclusion cannot fail to follow from the evidence, despite that being an act of the free agent who does as He wills and leaves as He wills. And it was answered: that the non-creation of the necessary concomitant with the creation of the entailed is impossible, so power does not attach to it; therefore it does not negate that it is the act of the free agent. And thus it is said concerning every pair that are rationally necessary concomitants, like substance and accident. And if this objection were valid, no rational necessary concomitant would be established in contingent things. His statement: “or customary” – “or” in that and in what follows is for diversification of the difference. It is meant: customary without generation, to differentiate it from the Mu’tazilite view of generation, for they claim it is customary, even though it necessarily implies [for them] that it is rational as before. And it is obvious that on this view the conclusion can fail to follow from the evidence; some illustrated it as when a person does not realize that the minor premise is included under the middle, for then the conclusion fails to follow from the evidence.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by al-Bajuri on the matn line that states there is a disagreement about how premises indicate the conclusion, listing four views: rational, customary, generated, and obligatory, with the rational view being supported. Al-Bajuri begins by confirming that the difference consists of four positions. He then explains the rational view: the connection between premises and conclusion is rational, meaning it is a necessary implication (luzūm ‘aqlī) without generation or causation. This differentiates it from the Mu’tazilite view of generation (tawallud), which, despite claiming to be customary, actually implies rational necessity because they adopted the philosophers’ doctrine of natural causation—that causes produce effects by their nature with a rational necessity, though they disguised it by changing the wording. It also differs from the philosophers, who admit it is rational. An objection is raised: if the connection is rational, the conclusion must always follow from the premises, but that conflicts with divine power—God could will otherwise. The answer: it is impossible for the necessary concomitant (lāzim) to be absent while the entailing (malzūm) is present; divine power does not extend to the impossible, so it does not conflict with free will. This is analogous to any rational necessary concomitants like substance and accident; if the objection held, no rational entailment would exist in creation. Then al-Bajuri discusses the customary view: the connection is based on habit (‘āda), not rational necessity. He clarifies that it is customary without generation, to differentiate from the Mu’tazilites who claim it is customary but actually imply rationality. On this view, it is possible for the conclusion not to follow, e.g., when a person fails to recognize that the minor term is included under the middle term in a syllogism. This lesson thus presents the first two of the four views on the nature of the logical consequence, defending the rational view and explaining the implications of the customary view.

Key Terms
عَقْلِيٌّ
دَلَالَةُ الْمُقَدِّمَاتِ عَلَى النَّتِيجَةِ
تَوَلُّدُ
لُزُوم عَقْلِي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bajuri, how does the rational view of implication respond to the objection that it restricts God's power?
A) It admits that God can break the entailment if He wills.
B) It argues that the impossibility of the conclusion not following from premises is a logical necessity that does not limit divine power.
C) It claims that the connection is only customary, not necessary.
D) It says that the Mu'tazilite view of generation solves the problem.

Key Takeaway
The rational view holds that premises necessarily imply the conclusion as a logical necessity, and this necessity does not conflict with divine omnipotence because logical impossibilities are not objects of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a categorical syllogism from a fiqh ruling (e.g., all intoxicants are forbidden; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden) and explain why al-Bajuri would say the conclusion follows necessarily on the rational view.

The Customary View: Implication as Divine Habit
Al-Bajuri explains the customary view of implication and its consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the customary view of implication and explain how it allows for the possibility of the conclusion not following.

Core Idea
Customary implication is based on God's habit, not necessity, so non-implication is possible.

In this lesson, we examine the second view on implication: the customary connection. Al-Bajuri clarifies that it is 'ādī without generation, distinguishing it from the Mu'tazilite claim. He also notes that on this view, the conclusion may fail to follow if the reasoner fails to perceive the inclusion of the minor under the middle. This view aligns with Ash'ari occasionalism and has implications for the certainty of logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of customary connection
Al-Bajuri defines the customary view as 'ādī without generation. The conclusion follows due to God's habit, not rational necessity. It is the standard Ash'ari position on causation.
2
Differentiate it from the Mu'tazilite view
The Mu'tazilites claim their view is customary but actually it is tawallud, which implies rational necessity. Al-Bajuri insists the two are distinct: the customary view is truly without necessity.
3
Examine the possibility of non-implication
If the connection is customary, it is possible for the conclusion not to follow. Al-Bajuri gives an example: when a person does not realize that the minor term is included under the middle term, the conclusion may not be reached even though the premises are true.
This highlights that the implication depends on human perception in the customary view.
4
Contrast with the rational view
Unlike the rational view, where non-implication is impossible, the customary view allows for exceptions. This has implications for the certainty of syllogistic reasoning in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۲۰) وَفِي دَلَالَةِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ عَلَى النَّتِيجَةِ خِلَافٌ آتِ: عَقْلِيٌّ، أَوْ عَادِيٌّ، أَوْ تَوَلُّدُ، أَوْ وَاجِبٌ؛ وَالأَوَّلُ المُؤَيَّدُ. قَالَ الباجوري: قَوْلُهُ : خِلَافٌ آتِ أَيْ: عَلَى أَرْبَعَةِ أَقْوَالٍ، كَمَا فَصَّلَهُ المُصَنِّفُ بَعْدُ. قَوْلُهُ : (عَقْلِيٌّ) خَبَرٌ لِمُبْتَدَأَ مَحْذُوفٍ ؛ وَالتَّقْدِيرُ : وَهَذَا الاِرْتِبَاطُ عَقْلِيٌّ . وَالمُرَادُ : عَقْلِيٌّ بِلَا تَوَلُّدٍ وَلَا تَعْلِيلٍ : لِيُغَايِرَ قَوْلَ المُعْتَزِلَةِ بِالتَّوَلُدِ، فَإِنَّهُ يَسْتَلْزِمُ أَنَّهُ عَقْلِيٌّ وَإِنْ كَانُوا يَدَّعُونَ أَنَّهُ عَادِيٌّ، وَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّهُمْ أَخَذُوا قَوْلَهُمْ بِالتَّوَلُدِ فِي هَذِهِ المَسْأَلَةِ وَفِي غَيْرِهَا مِنْ مَذْهَبِ الفَلَاسِفَةِ فِي الأَسْبَابِ الطَّبِيعِيَّةِ، وَهُوَ أَنَّهَا تُؤَثِّرُ فِي مُسَبِّبَاتِهَا بِطَبْعِهَا عَلَى وَجْهِ اللُّزُومِ العَقْلِيِّ عِنْدَ وُجُودِ الشَّرْطِ وَانْتِفَاءِ المَانِعِ ؛ غَايَةُ الأَمْرِ أَنَّهُمْ تَسَتَّرُوا بِتَغْيِيرِ العِبَارَةِ. وَلِيُغَايِرَ قَوْلَ الفَلَاسِفَةِ، فَإِنَّهُمْ لَا يُنْكِرُونَ أَنَّهُ عَقْلِيٌّ . وَاعْتُرِضَ هَذَا القَوْلُ : بِأَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ عَلَيْهِ أَنَّهُ لَا يُمْكِنُ تَخَلَّفُ النَّتِيجَةِ عَنِ الدَّلِيلِ، مَعَ أَنَّ ذَلِكَ فِعْلُ القَادِرِ المُخْتَارِ؛ الَّذِي إِنْ شَاءَ فَعَلَ وَإِنْ شَاءَ تَرَكَ. وَأُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ عَدَمَ خَلْقِ اللَّازِمِ مَعَ خَلْقِ المَلْزُومِ مُحَالٌ، فَلَا تَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ القُدْرَةُ، وَحِينَئِذٍ فَلَا يُنَافِي أَنَّهُ فِعْلُ القَادِرِ المُخْتَارِ ، وَهَكَذَا يُقَالُ فِي كُلِّ مُتَلَازِمَيْنِ عَقْلاً كَـ : «الجَوْهَرِ، وَالعَرَضِ»، وَلَوْ تَوَجَّهَ هَذَا الاِعْتِرَاضُ لَمْ يَثْبُتْ لَازِمٌ عَقْلِيٌّ فِي الكَائِنَاتِ . قَوْلُهُ : (أَوْ عَادِيٌّ) «أَوْ» فِي ذَلِكَ وَفِيمَا بَعْدَهُ لِتَنْوِيعِ الخِلَافِ. وَالمُرَادُ : عَادِيٌّ بِلَا تَوَلُّدٍ ؛ لِيُغَايِرَ قَوْلَ المُعْتَزِلَةِ بِالتَّوَلُدِ، فَإِنَّهُمْ يَزْعُمُونَ أَنَّهُ عَادِيٌّ، وَإِنْ كَانَ يَلْزَمُهُمْ أَنَّهُ عَقْلِيٌّ كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ. وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّهُ يُمْكِنُ عَلَى هَذَا القَوْلِ تَخَلَّفُ النَّتِيجَةِ عَنِ الدَّلِيلِ، وَصَوَّرَهُ بَعْضُهُمْ بِمَا إِذَا لَمْ يَتَفَطَّنِ الشَّخْصُ لِانْدِرَاجِ الأَصْغَرِ تَحْتَ الأَوْسَطِ، فَإِنَّهُ حِينَئِذٍ تَتَخَلَّفُ النَّتِيجَةُ عَنِ الدَّلِيلِ.
(120) And concerning the indication of the premises to the conclusion, there is a difference to come: rational, or customary, or generated, or obligatory; and the first is supported. Al-Bajuri said: His statement “difference to come” i.e., based on four statements, as the author later details. His statement: “rational” is a predicate of a deleted subject, i.e., “this connection is rational.” It is meant: rational without generation and without causation, to differentiate it from the Mu’tazilite view of generation, for it necessarily implies that it is rational even though they claim it is customary; that is because they took their view of generation in this matter and other from the philosophers’ doctrine concerning natural causes, namely that they affect their effects by their nature in the manner of rational necessity given the presence of condition and absence of impediment; the point is that they concealed themselves by changing the expression. And to differentiate it from the philosophers’ view, for they do not deny that it is rational. And this view was objected to: that it entails that the conclusion cannot fail to follow from the evidence, despite that being an act of the free agent who does as He wills and leaves as He wills. And it was answered: that the non-creation of the necessary concomitant with the creation of the entailed is impossible, so power does not attach to it; therefore it does not negate that it is the act of the free agent. And thus it is said concerning every pair that are rationally necessary concomitants, like substance and accident. And if this objection were valid, no rational necessary concomitant would be established in contingent things. His statement: “or customary” – “or” in that and in what follows is for diversification of the difference. It is meant: customary without generation, to differentiate it from the Mu’tazilite view of generation, for they claim it is customary, even though it necessarily implies [for them] that it is rational as before. And it is obvious that on this view the conclusion can fail to follow from the evidence; some illustrated it as when a person does not realize that the minor premise is included under the middle, for then the conclusion fails to follow from the evidence.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by al-Bajuri on the matn line that states there is a disagreement about how premises indicate the conclusion, listing four views: rational, customary, generated, and obligatory, with the rational view being supported. Al-Bajuri begins by confirming that the difference consists of four positions. He then explains the rational view: the connection between premises and conclusion is rational, meaning it is a necessary implication (luzūm ‘aqlī) without generation or causation. This differentiates it from the Mu’tazilite view of generation (tawallud), which, despite claiming to be customary, actually implies rational necessity because they adopted the philosophers’ doctrine of natural causation—that causes produce effects by their nature with a rational necessity, though they disguised it by changing the wording. It also differs from the philosophers, who admit it is rational. An objection is raised: if the connection is rational, the conclusion must always follow from the premises, but that conflicts with divine power—God could will otherwise. The answer: it is impossible for the necessary concomitant (lāzim) to be absent while the entailing (malzūm) is present; divine power does not extend to the impossible, so it does not conflict with free will. This is analogous to any rational necessary concomitants like substance and accident; if the objection held, no rational entailment would exist in creation. Then al-Bajuri discusses the customary view: the connection is based on habit (‘āda), not rational necessity. He clarifies that it is customary without generation, to differentiate from the Mu’tazilites who claim it is customary but actually imply rationality. On this view, it is possible for the conclusion not to follow, e.g., when a person fails to recognize that the minor term is included under the middle term in a syllogism. This lesson thus presents the first two of the four views on the nature of the logical consequence, defending the rational view and explaining the implications of the customary view.

Key Terms
عَادِيٌّ
تَوَلُّدُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bajuri, what is an example of how the conclusion can fail to follow on the customary view?
A) When the premises are false.
B) When God directly prevents the conclusion.
C) When a person does not realize that the minor term is included under the middle term.
D) When the syllogism is in a different figure.

Key Takeaway
The customary view of implication allows for the conclusion not to follow due to habit, consistent with Ash'ari occasionalism, and this accounts for human error in reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'The Quran is the word of God; the word of God is preserved; therefore the Quran is preserved.' According to the customary view, explain a scenario where the conclusion might not follow, using al-Bajuri's example of lack of perception.
?

The Syllogism

Four Opinions on Premise-Conclusion Connection
Bajuri enumerates and critiques four doctrines on the logical connection between premises and conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and contrast the four doctrines (ʿaqlī, ʿādī, tawallud, wājib) on the logical connection between premises and conclusion, and explain why the first (ʿaqlī) is the supported view in Ashʿarī logic.

Core Idea
The rational view is defended against the objection of limiting God’s power; the other three are rejected.

This passage by al-Bajuri presents four competing theories of how premises lead to conclusions. Understanding these views is crucial for evaluating the validity of logical proofs in kalām and usul al-fiqh. We will dissect each view, the objections raised, and the responses, culminating in the Ashʿarī preference for rational necessity without generation or causation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four views
Locate the four terms in the Arabic: عقلي (rational), عادي (customary), تولد (generation), واجب (necessary). Note that the first is marked as supported.
These are the four opinions on how premises indicate the conclusion.
2
Analyze the rational view (ʿaqlī)
Understand that ʿaqlī means the conclusion follows by rational necessity without generation (tawallud) or causal efficacy (taʿlīl). Note the objection: it seems to limit God's power. Study the response: the impossibility of the conclusion not following does not constrain God because the pair is a necessary concomitant, like substance and accident.
This is the Ashʿarī view, defended by al-Razi and al-Ghazali.
3
Analyze the customary view (ʿādī)
Notice that 'customary' means the conclusion follows habitually but can be broken. The objection: it could fail if the person doesn't notice the middle term. The refutation: the discussion assumes the proof fulfills all conditions, including attention. A stronger depiction: God might create knowledge of premises without the conclusion as a miracle.
This view is also from Ahl al-Haqq but is not preferred.
4
Analyze the generation view (tawallud)
Define tawallud: an act necessitates another act in the same agent, like finger movement causing ring movement. Applied: knowledge of premises generates knowledge of conclusion. Note that al-Bajuri condemns this as based on the Muʿtazilī doctrine that humans create their own acts.
This view is rejected as corrupt.
5
Analyze the necessary view (wājib)
Understand that 'necessary' means the conclusion is necessitated by causal efficacy (taʿlīl). This is the philosophers' view. Al-Bajuri states it is false by conclusive proofs, as it implies natural causation independent of God.
This view is also rejected.

وفي دلالة المقدمات على النتيجة خلاف آت (121) عقلي، أو عادي، أو تولد، أو واجب؛ والأول المؤيد. الباجوري: قوله: خلاف (آت) أي: على أربعة أقوال، كما فصله المصنف بعد. قوله: (عقلي) خبر لمبتدأ محذوف؛ والتقدير: وهذا الارتباط عقلي. والمراد: عقلي بلا تولد ولا تعليل - ليغاير قول المعتزلة بالتولد، فإنه يستلزم أنه عقلي وإن كانوا يدعون أنه عادي، وذلك لأنهم أخذوا قولهم بالتولد في هذه المسألة وفي غيرها من مذهب الفلاسفة في الأسباب الطبيعية، وهو أنها تؤثر في مسبباتها بطبعها على وجه اللزوم العقلي عند وجود الشرط وانتفاء المانع؛ غاية الأمر أنهم تسكروا بتغيير العبارة. - وليغاير قول الفلاسفة، فإنهم لا ينكرون أنه عقلي. واعترض هذا القول: بأنه يلزم عليه أنه لا يمكن تخلف النتيجة عن الدليل، مع أن ذلك فعل القادر المختار؛ الذي إن شاء فعل وإن شاء ترك. وأجيب: بأن عدم خلق اللازم مع خلق الملزوم محال، فلا تتعلق به القدرة، وحينئذ فلا ينافي أنه فعل القادر المختار، وهكذا يقال في كل متلازمين عقلا كـ: «الجوهر، والعرض»، ولو توجه هذا الاعتراض لم يثبت لازم عقلي في الكائنات. قوله: او عادي) «أو في ذلك وفيما بعده لتنويع الخلاف. والمراد: عادي بلا تولد؛ ليغاير قول المعتزلة بالتولد، فإنهم يزعمون أنه عادي، وإن كان يلزمهم أنه عقلي كما تقدم. ولا يخفى أنه يمكن على هذا القول تخلف النتيجة عن الدليل، وصوره بعضهم بما إذا لم يتفطن الشخص لاندراج الأصغر تحت الأوسط، فإنه حينئذ تتخلف النتيجة عن الدليل. ورد: بأن الكلام في الدليل المستجمع للشروط، ومنها التفطن لذلك، وهو مفقود حينئذ. والجواب بـ: «أنه يمكن أن صاحب هذا القول لا يشترط هذا الشرط» لا يخفى بعده، فالأولى تصويره بما إذا خلق الله تعالى العلم بالدليل دون العلم بالنتيجة؛ خرقاً للعادة. قوله: (او تولد) أي: ذو تولد، أو أن التولد بمعنى: المتولد، ويؤيد الثاني قوله بعد: أو واجب». وضابط التولد عند القائلين به وهم المعتزلة - قبحهم الله تعالى -: أن يوجب الفعل لفاعله فعلاً آخر؛ كما في: حركة الإصبع مع حركة الخاتم. وعلى هذا فالعلم بالدليل مخلوق للشخص، ويتولد عنه العلم بالنتيجة، وهذا كما ترى مبني على مذهبهم الفاسد، وهو: أن العبد يخلق أفعال نفسه الاختيارية. قوله: أو واجب أي: على طريق التعليل، فهذا القول مبني على القول بتأثير العلة في معلولها، وهو باطل بالأدلة القاطعة. قوله: (والأول المؤيد) أي: المقوى، حيث اختاره الإمام الرازي، وشهره حجة الإسلام وغيره. والحاصل: أن الأقوال أربعة؛ قولان منها لأهل الحق لكن الأول هو المختار المشهور، وقولان منها لأهل الزيغ والضلال.
And concerning the indication of premises to the conclusion, there is a difference to come: (121) rational, or customary, or generated, or necessary; and the first is supported. Al-Bajuri: His saying: 'difference to come' i.e., upon four sayings, as the author detailed later. His saying: 'rational' is a predicate of an omitted subject; the estimation is: and this connection is rational. What is meant: rational without generation and without causation - to differ from the Mu'tazila's saying of generation, for it entails that it is rational even though they claim it is customary, and that is because they took their saying of generation in this issue and in others from the philosophers' doctrine concerning natural causes, which is that they affect their effects by their nature in the manner of rational necessity upon the existence of the condition and the absence of the impediment; the utmost of the matter is that they became confused by changing the expression. - And to differ from the philosophers' saying, for they do not deny that it is rational. And this saying was objected to: that it entails that the conclusion cannot fail to follow from the proof, while that is the act of the free agent, who if He wills does and if He wills leaves. And it was answered: that the non-creation of the necessary consequent with the creation of the antecedent is impossible, so power does not attach to it, and then it does not contradict that it is the act of the free agent, and thus it is said concerning every pair of rational concomitants like 'substance and accident', and if this objection were valid, no rational concomitant would be established in existents. His saying: 'or customary' - 'or' in that and in what follows is for diversifying the difference. What is meant: customary without generation; to differ from the Mu'tazila's saying of generation, for they claim that it is customary, even though it entails for them that it is rational as previously. And it is not hidden that upon this saying, the conclusion can fail to follow from the proof, and some depicted it as when a person does not notice the subsumption of the minor under the middle, for then the conclusion fails to follow from the proof. And it was refuted: that the discussion is about the proof that fulfills the conditions, and among them is noticing that, and it is absent then. And the answer: 'It is possible that the proponent of this saying does not stipulate this condition' - its remoteness is not hidden, so better is to depict it as when Allah, exalted, creates knowledge of the proof without knowledge of the conclusion, as a breach of custom. His saying: 'or generated' i.e., possessing generation, or that generation means the generated thing, and the second is supported by his later saying: 'or necessary'. And the definition of generation according to those who hold it, and they are the Mu'tazila - may Allah disfigure them - is: that an act necessitates for its agent another act; as in the movement of the finger with the movement of the ring. And upon this, knowledge of the proof is created by the person, and knowledge of the conclusion is generated from it, and this, as you see, is based upon their corrupt doctrine, which is that the servant creates his own voluntary acts. His saying: 'or necessary' i.e., upon the way of causation, so this saying is based upon the saying of the effect of the cause upon its effect, and it is false by conclusive proofs. His saying: 'and the first is supported' i.e., strengthened, as Imam al-Razi chose it, and Hujjat al-Islam and others made it well-known. And the summary: the sayings are four; two of them belong to the people of truth but the first is the chosen and well-known, and two of them belong to the people of deviation and misguidance.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is an expansion by al-Bajuri on the disagreement regarding how premises (muqaddimāt) indicate (dalāla) the conclusion (natīja). It is a definitional passage that classifies and critiques four possible views, defending the first. The author first states that there is a disagreement (khilāf) that will be detailed, listing four positions: ʿaqlī (rational), ʿādī (customary), tawallud (generation), and wājib (necessary). He immediately notes that the first is the supported (muʾayyad) view. Al-Bajuri then explains each. For the rational view, he clarifies that it means the connection is rationally necessary (ʿaqlī) without involving generation (tawallud) or causal efficacy (taʿlīl). This distinguishes it from the Muʿtazila, who claim generation but actually imply rational necessity, and from the philosophers, who also affirm rational necessity. An objection is raised: rational necessity would imply that God cannot withhold the conclusion, contradicting His free agency. The response is that the necessity is not a constraint on God; rather, it is impossible for a necessary concomitant to be absent when its antecedent exists, so it is not subject to divine power in that sense—just as it is impossible for a substance to exist without an accident. If the objection were valid, no rational concomitance (luzūm ʿaqlī) would be established. For the customary (ʿādī) view, the connection is based on habit, not intellectual necessity, so the conclusion can occasionally fail, e.g., if the person lacks attention to the middle term. This is refuted: the discussion assumes the proof fulfills conditions including attention. A stronger depiction: God might create knowledge of premises but not of the conclusion as a miracle (kharq al-ʿāda). The generation (tawallud) view is defined: an act necessitates another act for the agent, like finger movement causing ring movement. Applied: knowledge of premises generates knowledge of conclusion. Al-Bajuri condemns this as based on the Muʿtazilī belief that humans create their own acts. The necessary (wājib) view holds that the conclusion is necessitated by the effect of a cause, which is rejected as false. The passage concludes by reaffirming the first opinion as chosen and well-known, while the last two belong to deviant groups. Throughout, al-Bajuri uses Islamic examples: the pairs of substance/accident, and the critique of Muʿtazila and philosophers, all within the Islamic theological context.

دلالة
عقلي
عادي
تولد
واجب

Key Takeaway

The Four Types of Proof in Islamic Theology
Author enumerates four types of proof and defends the first as the strongest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and define the four types of proof (al-ḥujja) as enumerated in the matn, and identify which type the commentator affirms as the strongest.

Core Idea
Proof in theology is fourfold, but only rational proof carries certainty.

In this passage, the commentator expands on a matn line that divides proof into four categories. Understanding this classification is essential for evaluating arguments in kalām, as it helps distinguish valid proofs from those based on flawed theological premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types
Locate the four terms in the Arabic: عَقْلِيُّ (rational), عَادِيٌّ (habitual), تَوَلَّدُ (generated), وَاجِبٌ (obligatory). These are the categories of proof enumerated in the matn.
2
Define each type
Read the commentator's explanation: rational proof is based on necessary intellectual causation; habitual proof on customary occurrence; generated proof as per Muʿtazila where one action necessitates another (like finger movement causing ring movement); obligatory proof based on causal efficacy.
The last two are based on rejected doctrines, as the commentator explains.
3
Identify the commentator's preference
The commentator says 'the first is the supported' (وَالأَوَّلُ المُؤَيَّدُ) and later states that the first is the chosen and well-known view of Ahl al-Haqq. This indicates rational proof is the strongest and most sound.

أقسام الحجة (۱۲۱) عَقْلِيُّ، أَوْ عَادِيٌّ ، أَوْ تَوَلَّدُ، أَوْ وَاجِبٌ وَالأَوَّلُ المُؤَيَّدُ الباجوري وَرُدَّ : بِأَنَّ الكَلَامَ فِي الدَّلِيلِ المُسْتَجْمِعِ لِلشُّرُوطِ، وَمِنْهَا التَّفَصُّنُ لِذَلِكَ، وَهُوَ مَفْقُودٌ حِينَئِذٍ . وَالجَوَابُ بِـ : «أَنَّهُ يُمْكِنُ أَنَّ صَاحِبَ هَذَا القَوْلِ لَا يَشْتَرِطُ هَذَا الشَّرْطَ لَا يَخْفَى بُعْدَهُ، فَالْأَوْلَى تَصْوِيرُهُ بِمَا إِذَا خَلَقَ اللهُ تَعَالَى العِلْمَ بِالدَّلِيلِ دُونَ العِلْمِ بِالنَّتِيجَةِ ؛ خَرْقاً لِلعَادَةِ. قَوْلُهُ : (اَوْ تَوَلُّهُ) أَيْ: ذُو تَوَلُّدٍ، أَوْ أَنَّ التَّوَلُّدَ بِمَعْنَى : المُتَوَلدِ، وَيُؤيِّدُ الثَّانِي قَوْلُهُ بَعْدُ: أَوْ وَاجِبٌ». وَضَابِطُ التَّوَلُدِ عِنْدَ القَائِلِينَ بِهِ وَهُمُ المُعْتَزِلَةُ - فَبَّحَهُمُ اللهُ تَعَالَى -: أَنْ يُوجِبَ الفِعْلُ لِفَاعِلِهِ فِعْلاً آخَرَ؛ كَمَا فِي : حَرَكَةِ الإِصْبَعِ مَعَ حَرَكَةِ الخَاتَمِ . وَعَلَى هَذَا فَالعِلْمُ بِالدَّلِيلِ مَخْلُوقٌ لِلشَّخْصِ ، وَيَتَوَلَّهُ عَنْهُ العِلْمُ بِالنَّتِيجَةِ، وَهَذَا كَمَا تَرَى مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى مَذْهَبِهِمُ الفَاسِدِ، وَهُوَ : أَنَّ العَبْدَ يَخْلُقُ أَفْعَالَ نَفْسِهِ الاخْتِيَارِيَّةِ. قَوْلُهُ: أَوْ وَاجِبٌ أَيْ : عَلَى طَرِيقِ التَّعْلِيلِ، فَهَذَا القَوْلُ مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى القَوْلِ بِتَأْثِيرِ العِلَّةِ فِي مَعْلُولِهَا، وَهُوَ بَاطِلٌ بِالأَدِلَّةِ القَاطِعَةِ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَالأَوَّلُ المُؤَيَّدُ) أي : المُقَوَّى، حَيْثُ اخْتَارَهُ الإِمَامُ الرَّازِيُّ، وَشَهَّرَهُ حُجَّةُ الإِسْلَامِ وغيره . وَالحَاصِلُ : أَنَّ الأَقْوَالَ أَرْبَعَةٌ ؛ قَوْلَانِ مِنْهَا لِأَهْلِ الحَقِّ لَكِنَّ الأَوَّلَ هُوَ المُخْتَارُ المَشْهُورُ، وَقَوْلَانِ مِنْهَا لِأَهْلِ الزَّيْعِ وَالضَّلَالِ .
Divisions of the proof. (121) Rational, or habitual, or generated, or obligatory, and the first is the supported. al-Bajuri. And it was rebutted: that the discussion is about the proof that fulfills the conditions, among them is the [؟] for that, and it is lacking at that time. And the answer with: 'Indeed it is possible that the holder of this statement does not require this condition' — its distance is not hidden. So the best is to conceive it as when Allah creates knowledge of the evidence without knowledge of the result, as a breach of habit. His statement: 'or generated' meaning: having generation, or that generation is in the sense of the generated, and the second is supported by his statement afterwards: 'or obligatory'. And the criterion of generation according to those who affirm it — and they are the Mu'tazila, may Allah disfigure them — is that the action necessitates for its agent another action; like the movement of the finger with the movement of the ring. And upon this, knowledge of the evidence is created for the person, and from it knowledge of the result is generated. And this, as you see, is based on their corrupt doctrine, which is that the servant creates his own voluntary actions. His statement: 'or obligatory' meaning: by way of causal efficacy. So this statement is based on the doctrine of the effect of the cause upon its effect, and it is false by decisive proofs. His statement: 'and the first is the supported' meaning: the strengthened, because al-Imam al-Razi chose it, and Hujjat al-Islam and others made it well-known. And the conclusion: that the views are four; two of them are from the people of truth, but the first is the chosen and well-known, and two of them are from the people of deviation and misguidance.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn line that enumerates four types of proof: rational (ʿaqli), habitual (ʿādi), generated (mutawallid), and obligatory (wājib). The commentator first explains that the first type is the strongest. He then rebuts an objection regarding the condition of proof, and gives an answer. He then defines 'generated' proof as understood by the Muʿtazila, who hold that an action can necessitate another action, like finger movement causing ring movement. He refutes this by noting it depends on the false Muʿtazili doctrine that humans create their own actions. He then defines 'obligatory' proof as based on causal efficacy, which he also rejects as false. Finally, he affirms that the first type (rational proof) is the preferred view, chosen by al-Razi and al-Ghazali. The passage concludes by summarizing that two of the four views belong to Ahl al-Haqq and are sound, but the first is the most well-known, while the other two belong to the people of deviation.
