Theological Rejection of Generated and Obligatory Proof
Author rejects generated and obligatory proofs based on theological principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the theological grounds on which the commentator rejects the generated (mutawallid) and obligatory (wājib) proofs.

Core Idea
Generated proof depends on Muʿtazilite human agency; obligatory proof on causal efficacy—both are rejected by Ashʿarīs.

After enumerating the four types of proof, the commentator explicitly dismisses the generated and obligatory proofs as based on corrupt doctrines. This lesson examines the specific theological reasons for these rejections, which are central to understanding Ashʿarī epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Locate the rejection of generated proof
Find where the commentator says 'this is based on their corrupt doctrine, which is that the servant creates his own voluntary actions.' This rejection is directed at the Muʿtazilite concept of generated proof.
2
Understand the generated proof mechanism
The Muʿtazila hold that an action can necessitate another action by generation (tawallud), like finger movement causing ring movement. In proof, knowledge of the evidence generates knowledge of the result. The commentator rejects this because it implies humans create their own actions.
3
Locate the rejection of obligatory proof
Find where the commentator says 'this statement is based on the doctrine of the effect of the cause upon its effect, and it is false by decisive proofs.' This refers to the obligatory proof based on causal efficacy.
4
Understand the obligatory proof mechanism
Obligatory proof means that the cause necessarily produces its effect by its own power. The commentator dismisses this as false because it contradicts the Ashʿarī occasionalist view that Allah directly creates all effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الحجة (۱۲۱) عَقْلِيُّ، أَوْ عَادِيٌّ ، أَوْ تَوَلَّدُ، أَوْ وَاجِبٌ وَالأَوَّلُ المُؤَيَّدُ الباجوري وَرُدَّ : بِأَنَّ الكَلَامَ فِي الدَّلِيلِ المُسْتَجْمِعِ لِلشُّرُوطِ، وَمِنْهَا التَّفَصُّنُ لِذَلِكَ، وَهُوَ مَفْقُودٌ حِينَئِذٍ . وَالجَوَابُ بِـ : «أَنَّهُ يُمْكِنُ أَنَّ صَاحِبَ هَذَا القَوْلِ لَا يَشْتَرِطُ هَذَا الشَّرْطَ لَا يَخْفَى بُعْدَهُ، فَالْأَوْلَى تَصْوِيرُهُ بِمَا إِذَا خَلَقَ اللهُ تَعَالَى العِلْمَ بِالدَّلِيلِ دُونَ العِلْمِ بِالنَّتِيجَةِ ؛ خَرْقاً لِلعَادَةِ. قَوْلُهُ : (اَوْ تَوَلُّهُ) أَيْ: ذُو تَوَلُّدٍ، أَوْ أَنَّ التَّوَلُّدَ بِمَعْنَى : المُتَوَلدِ، وَيُؤيِّدُ الثَّانِي قَوْلُهُ بَعْدُ: أَوْ وَاجِبٌ». وَضَابِطُ التَّوَلُدِ عِنْدَ القَائِلِينَ بِهِ وَهُمُ المُعْتَزِلَةُ - فَبَّحَهُمُ اللهُ تَعَالَى -: أَنْ يُوجِبَ الفِعْلُ لِفَاعِلِهِ فِعْلاً آخَرَ؛ كَمَا فِي : حَرَكَةِ الإِصْبَعِ مَعَ حَرَكَةِ الخَاتَمِ . وَعَلَى هَذَا فَالعِلْمُ بِالدَّلِيلِ مَخْلُوقٌ لِلشَّخْصِ ، وَيَتَوَلَّهُ عَنْهُ العِلْمُ بِالنَّتِيجَةِ، وَهَذَا كَمَا تَرَى مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى مَذْهَبِهِمُ الفَاسِدِ، وَهُوَ : أَنَّ العَبْدَ يَخْلُقُ أَفْعَالَ نَفْسِهِ الاخْتِيَارِيَّةِ. قَوْلُهُ: أَوْ وَاجِبٌ أَيْ : عَلَى طَرِيقِ التَّعْلِيلِ، فَهَذَا القَوْلُ مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى القَوْلِ بِتَأْثِيرِ العِلَّةِ فِي مَعْلُولِهَا، وَهُوَ بَاطِلٌ بِالأَدِلَّةِ القَاطِعَةِ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَالأَوَّلُ المُؤَيَّدُ) أي : المُقَوَّى، حَيْثُ اخْتَارَهُ الإِمَامُ الرَّازِيُّ، وَشَهَّرَهُ حُجَّةُ الإِسْلَامِ وغيره . وَالحَاصِلُ : أَنَّ الأَقْوَالَ أَرْبَعَةٌ ؛ قَوْلَانِ مِنْهَا لِأَهْلِ الحَقِّ لَكِنَّ الأَوَّلَ هُوَ المُخْتَارُ المَشْهُورُ، وَقَوْلَانِ مِنْهَا لِأَهْلِ الزَّيْعِ وَالضَّلَالِ .

Understanding the 'Khatima' in Islamic Scholarly Works
Explanation of the term 'khatima' and its application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the linguistic and technical definitions of 'khatima' and explain its role in structuring Islamic texts.

Core Idea
The khatima is not just a conclusion; it is a formally defined structural component of Islamic learning.

In this passage, the commentator begins by clarifying the term 'khatima' (conclusion), which is used to seal a book, chapter, or section. Understanding this definition helps us appreciate how Islamic scholars structure their works to ensure the reader grasps the culminating points.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The commentator defines 'khatima'. Find the Arabic term and note that it is given both a linguistic and a technical definition.
The linguistic definition is general; the technical definition specifies its use in texts.
2
Extract the linguistic definition
Linguistically, 'khatima' means 'that with which a thing is sealed'. Understand this as a seal that completes something.
3
Extract the technical definition
Technically, it refers to specific words indicating specific meanings that seal a book, chapter, section, or the like. Note the qualifiers: 'specific words' and 'specific meanings'.
This distinguishes it from informal conclusions.
4
Apply the definition to the current text
The commentator states that here, 'khatima' refers to the phrase 'And error of the demonstration...'. See how the definition matches: it seals the section on demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خَاتِمَةٌ عَلَى السُّلَّمِ الْمُرَوَّنَقِ (۱۲۲) وَخَطَأُ الْبُرْهَانِ حَيْثُ وُجِدَا فِي مَادَةٍ، أَوْ صُورَةٍ؛ فَالْمُبْتَدَا الْبَاجُورِي خَاتِمَةٌ [ قَوْلُهُ : ] (خَاتِمَةٌ) هِيَ لُغَةٌ : «مَا يُخْتَمُ بِهِ الشَّيْءُ» وَاصْطِلَاحاً : أَلْفَاظٌ مَخْصُوصَةٌ دَالَّةٌ عَلَى مَعَانٍ مَخْصُوصَةٍ قَدْ خُتِمَ بِهَا كِتَابٌ، أَوْ بَابٌ، أَوْ فَصْلٌ، أَوْ نَحْوُ ذَلِكَ، فَهِيَ هُنَا عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ قَوْلِهِ : وَخَطَأُ الْبُرْهَانِ... إلخ»، فَتَفَطَنْ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَخَطَأُ الْبُرْهَانِ ... إلخ) اعْتَرَضَهُ سَيِّدِي سَعِيد : بِأَنَّهُ كَانَ الْأَوْلَى أَنْ يَقُولَ: «وَخَطَا الْقِيَاسِ) ؛ لِأَنَّ الْخَطَأَ كَمَا يَكُونُ فِي الْبُرْهَانِ يَكُونُ فِي غَيْرِهِ، وَهُوَ مَحْذُورٌ فِي الْقِيَاسِ كُلِّهِ وَأَجَابَ الشَّيْخُ الْمَلْوِيُّ: بِأَنَّهُ اقْتَصَرَ عَلَى الْبُرْهَانِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُشْتَرَطُ نَفْيُ جَمِيعِ مَا سَيَذْكُرُهُ إِلَّا فِيهِ، قَالَ : وَلَوْ سُلِّمَ أَنَّهُ يُشْتَرَطُ نَفْيُ جَمِيعِ مَا سَيَذْكُرُهُ فِي غَيْرِهِ، فَتَخْصِيصُهُ الْبُرْهَانَ بِالذِّكْرِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ الْمَقْصُودُ الْأَهَمُّ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ هُوَ الَّذِي يُفِيدُ الْيَقِينَ - قَوْلُهُ : حَيْثُ وُجِدَا) أَيْ : فِي أَيِّ تَرْكِيبٍ وُجِدَ، فَالْحِيثِيَّةُ لِلْإِطْلَاقِ . قَوْلُهُ : (فِي مَادَةٍ، أَوْ صُورَةٍ ... إلخ) مُحَصَّلُهُ : أَنَّهُ قَسَّمَ الْخَطَأَ إِلَى قِسْمَيْنِ : خَطَأَ فِي الْمَادَّةِ، وَخَطَأَ فِي الصُّورَةِ، ثُمَّ قَسَمَ الْقِسْمَ الْأَوَّلَ إِلَى قِسْمَيْنِ : خَطَأ فِي اللَّفْظِ، وَخَطَأَ فِي الْمَعْنَى
Conclusion on al-Sullam al-Munawraq. (122) And error of the demonstration, wherever it is found, in matter or in form; so the beginner... al-Bajuri. Conclusion: [His statement:] (Conclusion) lexically: 'that with which a thing is sealed'. And technically: specific words indicating specific meanings by which a book, or chapter, or section, or the like is sealed. So here it is an expression for his statement: 'And error of the demonstration... etc.', so pay attention. His statement: (And error of the demonstration... etc.) was objected to by my master Sa'id: that he should have said: 'And error of the syllogism', because error occurs in demonstration as well as in other [syllogisms], and it is a defect in all syllogisms. And al-Shaykh al-Mallawi answered: that he limited it to demonstration because the negation of all that he will mention is only required in it. He said: Even if it were conceded that the negation of all that he will mention is required in other [syllogisms], then his specification of demonstration is because it is the most important objective; for it is the one that yields certainty. His statement: 'wherever they are found' meaning: in any composition they are found, so the 'where-ness' is for absoluteness. His statement: (in matter or form... etc.) Its summary: that he divided error into two divisions: error in matter, and error in form. Then he divided the first division into two divisions: error in wording, and error in meaning.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the concluding section (khatima) of the text. The commentator first defines 'khatima' linguistically and technically. Then he addresses an objection from a scholar named Sa'id, who argues that the matn should have said 'error of the syllogism' rather than 'error of the demonstration', since errors occur in all syllogisms, not just demonstration. The commentator reports al-Mallawi's response: the author limited the discussion to demonstration because only in demonstration is it required to avoid all the errors listed; in other types of syllogism, some errors are permissible. Even if it were required, demonstration is the most important because it yields certainty. Next, the commentator explains the phrase 'wherever it is found' as indicating absolute location. Finally, he summarizes the classification: error is divided into material (in the premises) and formal (in the structure). Material error is further divided into verbal error and conceptual error.

Key Terms
خَاتِمَة
لُغَة
اصْطِلَاحاً

Why Focus on Demonstration? Defending the Scope of Error Discussion
Objection and response regarding the title

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Mallawi's response to the objection that the matn should discuss error in all syllogisms, not just demonstration.

Core Idea
Demonstration is prioritized because it demands all errors be avoided and it alone yields certainty.

An objector named Sa'id argues that the matn should have said 'error of the syllogism' instead of 'error of the demonstration', since errors occur in all syllogisms. The commentator reports al-Mallawi's defense, which reveals why demonstration gets special treatment.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
Sa'id objects that the matn should have used 'syllogism' instead of 'demonstration' because error affects all types of syllogisms, not just demonstration.
This is a logical criticism of the author's choice of terms.
2
Identify al-Mallawi's first response
Al-Mallawi argues that the author limited it to demonstration because the requirement to avoid all the errors listed applies only to demonstration; other syllogistic arts (dialectical, rhetorical, etc.) do not require avoiding every error.
This is a technical distinction based on the conditions of each art.
3
Identify his second response (even if the first is conceded)
Even if other arts also required avoiding all errors, demonstration is still the most important objective because it alone yields certainty (yaqin). Hence the focus is justified.
This shows the prioritization based on epistemic value.
4
Evaluate the strength of the response
Consider whether the response adequately addresses the objection. The first response is a principled distinction; the second is a pragmatic justification.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خَاتِمَةٌ عَلَى السُّلَّمِ الْمُرَوَّنَقِ (۱۲۲) وَخَطَأُ الْبُرْهَانِ حَيْثُ وُجِدَا فِي مَادَةٍ، أَوْ صُورَةٍ؛ فَالْمُبْتَدَا الْبَاجُورِي خَاتِمَةٌ [ قَوْلُهُ : ] (خَاتِمَةٌ) هِيَ لُغَةٌ : «مَا يُخْتَمُ بِهِ الشَّيْءُ» وَاصْطِلَاحاً : أَلْفَاظٌ مَخْصُوصَةٌ دَالَّةٌ عَلَى مَعَانٍ مَخْصُوصَةٍ قَدْ خُتِمَ بِهَا كِتَابٌ، أَوْ بَابٌ، أَوْ فَصْلٌ، أَوْ نَحْوُ ذَلِكَ، فَهِيَ هُنَا عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ قَوْلِهِ : وَخَطَأُ الْبُرْهَانِ... إلخ»، فَتَفَطَنْ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَخَطَأُ الْبُرْهَانِ ... إلخ) اعْتَرَضَهُ سَيِّدِي سَعِيد : بِأَنَّهُ كَانَ الْأَوْلَى أَنْ يَقُولَ: «وَخَطَا الْقِيَاسِ) ؛ لِأَنَّ الْخَطَأَ كَمَا يَكُونُ فِي الْبُرْهَانِ يَكُونُ فِي غَيْرِهِ، وَهُوَ مَحْذُورٌ فِي الْقِيَاسِ كُلِّهِ وَأَجَابَ الشَّيْخُ الْمَلْوِيُّ: بِأَنَّهُ اقْتَصَرَ عَلَى الْبُرْهَانِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُشْتَرَطُ نَفْيُ جَمِيعِ مَا سَيَذْكُرُهُ إِلَّا فِيهِ، قَالَ : وَلَوْ سُلِّمَ أَنَّهُ يُشْتَرَطُ نَفْيُ جَمِيعِ مَا سَيَذْكُرُهُ فِي غَيْرِهِ، فَتَخْصِيصُهُ الْبُرْهَانَ بِالذِّكْرِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ الْمَقْصُودُ الْأَهَمُّ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ هُوَ الَّذِي يُفِيدُ الْيَقِينَ - قَوْلُهُ : حَيْثُ وُجِدَا) أَيْ : فِي أَيِّ تَرْكِيبٍ وُجِدَ، فَالْحِيثِيَّةُ لِلْإِطْلَاقِ . قَوْلُهُ : (فِي مَادَةٍ، أَوْ صُورَةٍ ... إلخ) مُحَصَّلُهُ : أَنَّهُ قَسَّمَ الْخَطَأَ إِلَى قِسْمَيْنِ : خَطَأَ فِي الْمَادَّةِ، وَخَطَأَ فِي الصُّورَةِ، ثُمَّ قَسَمَ الْقِسْمَ الْأَوَّلَ إِلَى قِسْمَيْنِ : خَطَأ فِي اللَّفْظِ، وَخَطَأَ فِي الْمَعْنَى
Conclusion on al-Sullam al-Munawraq. (122) And error of the demonstration, wherever it is found, in matter or in form; so the beginner... al-Bajuri. Conclusion: [His statement:] (Conclusion) lexically: 'that with which a thing is sealed'. And technically: specific words indicating specific meanings by which a book, or chapter, or section, or the like is sealed. So here it is an expression for his statement: 'And error of the demonstration... etc.', so pay attention. His statement: (And error of the demonstration... etc.) was objected to by my master Sa'id: that he should have said: 'And error of the syllogism', because error occurs in demonstration as well as in other [syllogisms], and it is a defect in all syllogisms. And al-Shaykh al-Mallawi answered: that he limited it to demonstration because the negation of all that he will mention is only required in it. He said: Even if it were conceded that the negation of all that he will mention is required in other [syllogisms], then his specification of demonstration is because it is the most important objective; for it is the one that yields certainty. His statement: 'wherever they are found' meaning: in any composition they are found, so the 'where-ness' is for absoluteness. His statement: (in matter or form... etc.) Its summary: that he divided error into two divisions: error in matter, and error in form. Then he divided the first division into two divisions: error in wording, and error in meaning.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the concluding section (khatima) of the text. The commentator first defines 'khatima' linguistically and technically. Then he addresses an objection from a scholar named Sa'id, who argues that the matn should have said 'error of the syllogism' rather than 'error of the demonstration', since errors occur in all syllogisms, not just demonstration. The commentator reports al-Mallawi's response: the author limited the discussion to demonstration because only in demonstration is it required to avoid all the errors listed; in other types of syllogism, some errors are permissible. Even if it were required, demonstration is the most important because it yields certainty. Next, the commentator explains the phrase 'wherever it is found' as indicating absolute location. Finally, he summarizes the classification: error is divided into material (in the premises) and formal (in the structure). Material error is further divided into verbal error and conceptual error.

Classifying Errors in Demonstration: Material and Formal
Classification of error into matter and form, and matter into wording and meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between material error (verbal vs. conceptual) and formal error in demonstrative syllogisms.

Core Idea
Error in demonstration is either in the premises (matter) or in the logical structure (form); material error is further subdivided into verbal and conceptual.

The commentator summarizes the classification of errors in demonstration: error can be in the matter (the content of the premises) or in the form (the logical structure). Material error is then divided into error in wording (lafz) and error in meaning (ma'na). This classification is foundational for diagnosing why a demonstrative argument fails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories of error
The commentator states that error is divided into two types: error in matter (madda) and error in form (sura). Matter refers to the premises; form refers to the arrangement.
This is a standard distinction in logic.
2
Subdivide material error
Material error is further divided into error in wording (lafz) and error in meaning (ma'na). Error in wording relates to the expression of premises; error in meaning relates to the actual content or truth of premises.
This subdivision will be explored in the following lines of the text.
3
Map examples to each category
Think of an example: a syllogism with an ambiguous term (error in wording) vs. a syllogism with a false premise (error in meaning). Both are material errors. A syllogism that has correct premises but is invalid in form (e.g., second figure with two affirmative premises) is a formal error.
4
Apply the classification to a demonstrative argument
Take a demonstrative argument from kalam, such as 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' Check for material error (is the premise 'everything temporally originated has a cause' true? Is there any ambiguity?) and formal error (is the figure and mood valid?).

Distinguishing Material and Formal Error in Syllogisms
The commentator defines material and formal error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify an error in a syllogism as either material or formal based on the definitions in the passage.

Core Idea
Error in a syllogism originates either in the premises (material) or in their arrangement (form).

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a crucial distinction from the matn: the difference between material and formal error. Understanding this classification will help you diagnose why a logical argument fails – whether because of what the premises say or how they are structured.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories
The passage divides error into 'error in material' and 'error in form'. Note that material error is further divided into verbal and conceptual.
2
Define 'material' (mādda)
Material refers to the two premises of a syllogism considered either in wording or meaning. This is the source of material error.
3
Define 'form' (ṣūra)
Form refers to the arrangement (naẓm) and structure (hay'a) of the syllogism. Formal error arises from an invalid figure or mood.
4
Apply the classification
Given a faulty syllogism, decide whether the fault lies in the premises (e.g., false or ambiguous statement) or in the logical structure (e.g., invalid figure).
Example: A syllogism with an ambiguous middle term is a material error, even if it also appears invalid in form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخطأ البرهان حيث وجدا في مادة أو صورة فالمبتدا في اللفظ كـ اشتراك أو كجعل ذا تباين مثل الرديف مأخذًا قوله: (في مادة أو صورة... إلخ) محصله: أنه قسم الخطأ إلى قسمين: خطأ في المادة، وخطأ في الصورة، ثم قسم القسم الأول إلى قسمين: خطأ في اللفظ، وخطأ في المعنى. والمُراد بالمادة: مجموع المقدمتين باعتبار اللفظ أو المعنى؛ ليأتَّى التقسيم الذي ذكره خطأ المادة، والمُراد بالصورة: النظم والهيئة. قوله: (فالمبتدا) أي: الأول الذي هو الخطأ في المادة، وسيذكر مقابله في قوله: والثاني... إلخ. قوله: (في اللفظ) أي: خطأ في اللفظ، وسيذكر مقابله في قوله: وفي المعاني... إلخ، فتفطن. قوله: (اشتراك) مثال لسبب الخطأ، لا للخطأ نفسه، وتكلف بعضهم جعله على حذف مضاف، والتقدير: كخطأ اشتراك. ومثال ذلك: أن تقول مشيراً إلى الحيض: هذا قرء، وكل قرء لا يحرم الوطء فيه - وتريد الظهر - ينتج: «هذا لا يحرم الوطء فيه»، فإن الخطأ في ذلك في مادته بسبب الاشتراك. فإن قيل: الخطأ في هذا البرهان في صورته؛ لأنه لم يتكرر فيه الحد الوسط معنى، فكيف جعلوه من الخطأ في المادة؟ أجيب: بأنه لما كان عدم تكرار الحد الوسط معنى ناشئاً من المشترك الذي هو جزء من أجزاء المادة، جعلوا ذلك الخطأ من الخطأ في المادة، وإن كان يصح جعله من الخطأ في الصورة باعتبار عدم تكرار الحد الوسط معنى. انظر كلام الملوي في كبير. قوله: أو كجعل ذا تباين... إلخ) لا يخفى أن ذا بمعنى: صاحب، فكان حقها الجر بالياء، لكنها جاءت هنا بالألف على لغة القصر في الأسماء الستة؛ كذا قال المصنف في شرحه، لكن اعترضه سيدي سعيد: بأن لغة القصر إنما هي في: أب، وأخ، وحم، لا في: ذي، و قم بلا ميم، فإنهما إنما يعربان بالأحرف؛ كما نبه عليه المرادي، فكان الأولى أن يبدل ذلك البيت؛ بأن يقول مثلاً: ...
And the error of demonstration, wherever it is found, is in material or form. So the first [is] in wording, like equivocation, or like making a possessor of disparity like a synonym as a basis. His statement: (in material or form...) Summary: that he divided error into two divisions: error in material and error in form. Then he divided the first division into two: error in wording and error in meaning. And by 'material' is meant the two premises together in respect of wording or meaning, so that the division he mentioned of material error is possible. And by 'form' is meant the arrangement and structure. His statement: (so the first) i.e., the first which is error in material, and he will mention its counterpart in his statement: and the second... His statement: (in wording) i.e., error in wording, and he will mention its counterpart in his statement: and in meanings... so understand. His statement: (equivocation) is an example of the cause of error, not of error itself, and some strained to make it on the assumption of a deleted genitive, the estimation being: like error of equivocation. An example of that: that you say pointing to menstruation: 'This is a qur', and every qur' does not make intercourse unlawful' - meaning the purity - producing: 'This does not make intercourse unlawful.' So the error in that is in its material because of equivocation. If it is said: the error in this demonstration is in its form because the middle term is not repeated in meaning, so how did they make it from material error? Answer: because the lack of repetition of the middle term in meaning arises from the equivocal term which is a part of the material, they made that error from material error, although it is valid to make it from formal error considering the lack of repetition of the middle term in meaning. See al-Malawi's words in his large commentary. His statement: (or making 'dha' differing...) It is not hidden that 'dha' means 'owner', so it should have genitive with ya, but it came here with alif according to the language of shortening in the six nouns; thus said the author in his commentary. But Sayyid Sa'id objected that the language of shortening is only in 'ab, akh, ham', not in 'dhi' and 'qam' without mim, because they are both declined only with letters, as al-Muradi pointed out. So it would have been better for him to replace that verse; for example saying: ...

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of errors in syllogisms based on the matn of al-Sullam. The commentator first reproduces the matn line: 'وخطأ البرهان حيث وجدا في مادة أو صورة فالمبتدا في اللفظ كـ اشتراك أو كجعل ذا تباين مثل الرديف مأخذًا'. Then he explains that the matn divides error into two main types: material (in the premises) and formal (in the structure). Material error is further divided into verbal error (lafẓ) and conceptual error (ma'nā). He defines 'material' as the two premises considered either in wording or meaning, and 'form' as the arrangement (naẓm) and shape (hay'a). He then clarifies that 'the first' (al-mubtada') in the matn refers to the first type of error, i.e., material error. The second type (formal) will be mentioned later. He then focuses on the example of verbal error due to equivocation (ishtirāk): the word 'qur'' in Arabic can mean both 'menstruation' and 'purity' (the period between menses). The example syllogism is: 'This is a qur'. Every qur' does not make intercourse unlawful. Therefore, this does not make intercourse unlawful.' If one intends 'purity' for the second premise but means 'menstruation' in the first, the middle term 'qur'' is not repeated in meaning, leading to a fallacy. The commentator addresses a potential objection: this might seem like a formal error (middle term not repeated in meaning), but it is classified as material because the equivocal term is part of the material (the premises). He notes that it could also be considered formal, and refers to al-Malawi's larger commentary. Finally, he discusses a grammatical issue: the word 'dha' (ذَا) in the matn, meaning 'possessor', should have genitive case with 'ya' according to standard grammar, but here it appears with 'alif' (دَا) due to a dialectal shortening (qaṣr). A scholar named Sayyid Sa'id objects that such shortening is only allowed for 'ab', 'akh', and 'ḥam', not for 'dhi' and 'qam', and suggests replacing the verse. The passage ends mid-sentence.

حد وسط

A) The arrangement and structure of the syllogism.
B) The two premises together, considered in wording or meaning.
C) The conclusion of the syllogism.
D) The middle term only.

Key Takeaway
Material error stems from the premises (wording or meaning), while formal error stems from the syllogism's structure.

Equivocation and the Classification of Error: The 'Qur'' Example
The commentator discusses the equivocation example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the equivocation fallacy in the 'qur'' example is classified as material rather than formal, and identify the role of the middle term.

Core Idea
Equivocation can cause the middle term to lack a single meaning, but the error is rooted in the premises – making it material, not formal.

In this lesson, we examine a classic example of a logical error in a fiqh context: the fallacy of equivocation involving the word 'qur''. The commentator addresses a subtle objection: why is this error considered material when it seems like a formal error? By working through the example, you'll strengthen your understanding of the distinction between material and formal error.

Guided Path
1
Read the example
The example: 'This is a qur'. Every qur' does not make intercourse unlawful. Therefore, this does not make intercourse unlawful.' The word 'qur'' can mean menstruation or purity.
2
Identify the ambiguity
In the first premise, 'this is a qur'' likely means menstruation. In the second premise, 'every qur' does not make intercourse unlawful' is true only if 'qur'' means purity (the period between menses). Thus the middle term is used in two different senses.
3
Recognize the formal objection
One might object: the error is formal because the middle term is not repeated in meaning. The syllogism is invalid due to equivocation.
4
Understand the answer
The commentator says the error is material because the equivocal term (qur') is part of the premises (the material). The lack of repetition in meaning arises from that ambiguous term. Hence it is classified as material error.
This shows that the classification depends on the source of the error: if the fault originates in the premise wording, it is material.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخطأ البرهان حيث وجدا في مادة أو صورة فالمبتدا في اللفظ كـ اشتراك أو كجعل ذا تباين مثل الرديف مأخذًا قوله: (في مادة أو صورة... إلخ) محصله: أنه قسم الخطأ إلى قسمين: خطأ في المادة، وخطأ في الصورة، ثم قسم القسم الأول إلى قسمين: خطأ في اللفظ، وخطأ في المعنى. والمُراد بالمادة: مجموع المقدمتين باعتبار اللفظ أو المعنى؛ ليأتَّى التقسيم الذي ذكره خطأ المادة، والمُراد بالصورة: النظم والهيئة. قوله: (فالمبتدا) أي: الأول الذي هو الخطأ في المادة، وسيذكر مقابله في قوله: والثاني... إلخ. قوله: (في اللفظ) أي: خطأ في اللفظ، وسيذكر مقابله في قوله: وفي المعاني... إلخ، فتفطن. قوله: (اشتراك) مثال لسبب الخطأ، لا للخطأ نفسه، وتكلف بعضهم جعله على حذف مضاف، والتقدير: كخطأ اشتراك. ومثال ذلك: أن تقول مشيراً إلى الحيض: هذا قرء، وكل قرء لا يحرم الوطء فيه - وتريد الظهر - ينتج: «هذا لا يحرم الوطء فيه»، فإن الخطأ في ذلك في مادته بسبب الاشتراك. فإن قيل: الخطأ في هذا البرهان في صورته؛ لأنه لم يتكرر فيه الحد الوسط معنى، فكيف جعلوه من الخطأ في المادة؟ أجيب: بأنه لما كان عدم تكرار الحد الوسط معنى ناشئاً من المشترك الذي هو جزء من أجزاء المادة، جعلوا ذلك الخطأ من الخطأ في المادة، وإن كان يصح جعله من الخطأ في الصورة باعتبار عدم تكرار الحد الوسط معنى. انظر كلام الملوي في كبير. قوله: أو كجعل ذا تباين... إلخ) لا يخفى أن ذا بمعنى: صاحب، فكان حقها الجر بالياء، لكنها جاءت هنا بالألف على لغة القصر في الأسماء الستة؛ كذا قال المصنف في شرحه، لكن اعترضه سيدي سعيد: بأن لغة القصر إنما هي في: أب، وأخ، وحم، لا في: ذي، و قم بلا ميم، فإنهما إنما يعربان بالأحرف؛ كما نبه عليه المرادي، فكان الأولى أن يبدل ذلك البيت؛ بأن يقول مثلاً: ...
And the error of demonstration, wherever it is found, is in material or form. So the first [is] in wording, like equivocation, or like making a possessor of disparity like a synonym as a basis. His statement: (in material or form...) Summary: that he divided error into two divisions: error in material and error in form. Then he divided the first division into two: error in wording and error in meaning. And by 'material' is meant the two premises together in respect of wording or meaning, so that the division he mentioned of material error is possible. And by 'form' is meant the arrangement and structure. His statement: (so the first) i.e., the first which is error in material, and he will mention its counterpart in his statement: and the second... His statement: (in wording) i.e., error in wording, and he will mention its counterpart in his statement: and in meanings... so understand. His statement: (equivocation) is an example of the cause of error, not of error itself, and some strained to make it on the assumption of a deleted genitive, the estimation being: like error of equivocation. An example of that: that you say pointing to menstruation: 'This is a qur', and every qur' does not make intercourse unlawful' - meaning the purity - producing: 'This does not make intercourse unlawful.' So the error in that is in its material because of equivocation. If it is said: the error in this demonstration is in its form because the middle term is not repeated in meaning, so how did they make it from material error? Answer: because the lack of repetition of the middle term in meaning arises from the equivocal term which is a part of the material, they made that error from material error, although it is valid to make it from formal error considering the lack of repetition of the middle term in meaning. But Sayyid Sa'id objected that the language of shortening is only in 'ab, akh, ham', not in 'dhi' and 'qam' without mim, because they are both declined only with letters, as al-Muradi pointed out. Classification of Errors in Demonstration
Author defines general categories of error in demonstration: matter vs form, and within matter, wording vs meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the primary divisions of error in a demonstrative syllogism (matter vs. form) and further classify errors in matter as either linguistic (lafz) or conceptual (ma'na).

Core Idea
All errors in demonstration are either in the matter (premises) or the form (structure), and matter errors are either wording or meaning.

In this lesson, we examine the opening classification of errors in demonstration from the logical tradition. The author distinguishes between material and formal errors, and within material errors, further between linguistic and conceptual errors. This taxonomy is essential for diagnosing why an argument fails. We will break down the categories and see how they apply to real arguments from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the primary division
The passage states that error in demonstration occurs either in matter (mādda) or in form (ṣūra). Identify these two branches in the Arabic text.
Matter refers to the content of the premises; form refers to their logical structure (nazm wa hay'a).
2
Identify the subdivision of matter error
Within matter error, the author further divides it into error in wording (lafẓ) and error in meaning (maʿnā). Notice the examples given: homonymy (ishtirāk) and treating distinct things as synonyms.
This subdivision is made possible by defining matter as the aggregate of premises with respect to both wording and meaning.
3
Recognize the examples of wording errors
Two examples: (1) ishtirāk – using the same word with different meanings; (2) making dissimilar terms appear synonymous (jaʿlu dhī tabāyun mithla al-radīf). Understand why these cause error.
These are errors in the linguistic expression of the premises, not in their conceptual content.
4
Connect to the concept of logical form
Form error is defined as a defect in the arrangement and structure of the syllogism. Contrast this with matter error: even if premises are true, faulty form invalidates the argument.
For example, an invalid syllogistic figure is a form error, while a false premise or ambiguous term is a matter error.
5
Apply to a real argument
Consider the argument: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Every nabidh is intoxicating. Therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Check for errors in matter (e.g., is 'intoxicating' used unambiguously?) and form (is the syllogism in a valid figure?).
If the term 'intoxicant' is ambiguous (homonymy), that is a matter error in wording. If the syllogism is valid, then no form error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَخَطَأُ البُرْهَانِ حَيْثُ وُجِدَا فِي مَادَةٍ، أَوْ صُورَةٍ فَالمُبْتَدَا فِي «اللَّفْظِ كَـ : اشْتِرَاكِ، أَوْ كَ: جَعَلِ ذَا تَبَايُن مِثلَ الرَّدِيفِ مَأخَذَا. وَالمُرَادُ بِـ المَادَّةِ : مَجْمُوعُ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ بِاعْتِبَارِ اللَّفْظِ أَوِ المَعْنَى؛ لِيَتَاتَّى التَّقْسِيمُ الَّذِي ذَكَرَهُ خَطَأَ المَادَّةِ، وَالمُرَادُ بـ «الصُّورَةِ : النَّظْمُ وَالهَيْئَةُ . قَوْلُهُ : (فَالْمُبْتَدَا) أَي : الأَوَّلُ الَّذِي هُوَ الخَطا فِي المَادَّةِ، وَسَيَذْكُرُ مَقَابِلَهُ فِي قَوْلِهِ : وَالثَّانِ... إلخ». قَوْلُهُ : (فِي اللَّفْظِ) أَيْ: خَطَأ فِي اللَّفْظِ ، وَسَيَذْكُرُ مُقَابِلَهُ فِي قَوْلِهِ : وَفِي المَعَانِي ... إلخ»، فَتَفَطَّنْ .
And the error of the demonstration, wherever it is found, is either in matter or in form, so the first [is error in matter]. In wording, like: [error due to] homonymy, or like making a mutually exclusive thing like a synonym in the premises. And the intended by 'matter' is: the aggregate of the two premises with respect to wording or meaning; to make possible the division that he mentioned of error of matter. And the intended by 'form' is: the arrangement and structure. His statement: 'so the first' means: the first which is the error in matter, and he will mention its counterpart in his statement: 'and the second... etc.' His statement: 'in wording' means: an error in wording, and he will mention its counterpart in his statement: 'and in meanings... etc.', so be attentive.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage begins the discussion of errors in demonstration (khata' al-burhan). The commentator Bajuri defines the general categories: error can be in the matter (madda), which refers to the content of the premises, or in the form (sura), which is the logical structure. Within error in matter, there is a further division: error due to wording (lafz) and error due to meaning (ma'na). He then gives two examples of error in wording: homonymy (ishtirak) and making something different (mubayin) as if it were a synonym (radif) in the premises. The commentator clarifies that 'matter' includes both the wording and the meaning of the premises, to allow for this subdivision. He also notes that the text will later discuss the counterpart errors. This is a typical classificatory move in Islamic logic texts.

D) Two: wording and meaning.

Key Takeaway
The first step in analyzing a demonstrative argument is to determine whether the error lies in its matter (premises) or form (structure), and if in matter, whether it is a linguistic or conceptual mistake.

Error in Wording: Homonymy (Ishtirak) Example
Author gives an example of error due to homonymy using 'qur'' and addresses an objection about the middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between material error (due to homonymy) and formal error in a categorical syllogism, using the example of 'qur'.

Core Idea
A single homonymous word can break the syllogism, but the root lies in the matter.

This passage clarifies a subtle point in the classification of syllogistic errors. The author provides an example using the well-known homonym 'qur'' to show that an error can be traced to the matter even when it seems to be a formal defect. As you work through it, you will learn how to trace the origin of an equivocation fallacy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the homonymous term
Find the word 'qur'' in the passage and note its two distinct meanings: menstruation (hayd) and purity between menses (tuhr).
This word is used in Fiqh for the waiting period ('iddah).
2
Map the premises
Write out the two premises and the conclusion: Minor: 'This is a qur'' (pointing to menstruation). Major: 'Every qur'' (meaning purity) is not forbidden. Conclusion: 'This is not forbidden to have intercourse in it.'
Pay attention to which meaning is intended in each premise.
3
Assess middle term repetition
Check if the middle term 'qur'' is repeated in meaning across the two premises. It is not; it shifts from menstruation to purity.
A valid syllogism requires the middle term to be identical in meaning.
4
Consider the objection
The objector claims the error is purely formal because the middle term is not repeated in meaning. Why might they say that? Because form requires a unified middle term.
5
Understand the response
The author answers that the lack of repetition arises from the homonymous word itself, which is part of the matter (the wording). Therefore, the error is classified under matter, though a formal perspective is also possible.
This shows the nuanced classification in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ : أَنْ تَقُولَ مُشِيراً إِلَى الحَيْضِ : هَذَا قُرْءٌ، وَكُلُّ قُرْءٌ لَا يَحْرُمُ الوَطْءُ فِيهِ - وَتُرِيدُ الظُّهْرَ - يَنْتُجُ : «هَذَا لَا يَحْرُمُ الوَطْءُ فِيهِ»، فَإِنَّ الخَطَأ فِي ذَلِكَ فِي مَادَّتِهِ بِسَبَبِ الِاشْتِرَاكِ . فَإِنْ قِيلَ : الخَطَأ فِي هَذَا البُرْهَانِ فِي صُورَتِهِ ؛ لأَنَّهُ لَمْ يَتَكَرَّرْ فِيهِ الحَدُّ الوَسَطُ مَعْنًى ، فَكَيْفَ جَعَلُوهُ مِنَ الخَطَأَ فِي المَادَّةِ؟ أجِيبَ : بِأَنَّهُ لَمَّا كَانَ عَدَمُ تَكْرَارِ الحَدّ الوَسَطِ مَعْنًى نَاشِئاً مِنَ المُشْتَرَكِ الَّذِي هُوَ جُزْءٌ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ المَادَّةِ ، جَعَلُوا ذَلِكَ الخَطَأ مِنَ الخَطَأ فِي المَادَّةِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ يَصِحُ جَعْلُهُ مِنَ الخَطَأ فِي الصُّورَةِ بِاعْتِبَارِ عَدَمِ تَكْرَارِ الحَدِّ الوَسَطِ مَعْنَى انْظُرْ كَلَامَ المَلْوِيِّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ.
And an example of that (error in wording due to homonymy) is: that you say, pointing to menstruation: 'This is a qur'', and 'Every qur'' is not forbidden to have intercourse in it' — and you intend (by 'qur'') 'purity (tuhr)' — it yields: 'This is not forbidden to have intercourse in it.' So the error in that is in its matter because of homonymy. If it is said: 'The error in this demonstration is in its form, because the middle term is not repeated in meaning, so how did they make it from the error in matter?' It is answered: Because the lack of repetition of the middle term in meaning arises from the homonymous term which is a part of the parts of the matter, they made that error from the error in matter, even if it is correct to make it from the error in form with respect to the lack of repetition of the middle term in meaning. Look at the words of al-Malawi in his 'Kabir'.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage provides a concrete example of error in wording caused by homonymy (ishtirak). The example uses the Arabic word 'qur'' which is homonymous, meaning both menstruation (hayd) and the period of purity (tuhr) between menses. The speaker points to a menstruation and says 'This is a qur'' intending the meaning of menstruation, then states 'Every qur'' is not forbidden to have intercourse in it' intending the meaning of purity (tuhr). The conclusion incorrectly states that intercourse is not forbidden during this time. The error lies in the matter (premises) because the middle term 'qur'' is used with different meanings in the two premises, though it is the same word. An objection is raised: since the middle term is not repeated in meaning, the error could be considered an error in form (because the syllogism lacks a unified middle term). The answer refines this: the lack of repetition itself stems from the homonymy, which is a part of the matter (the wording of the premise). Therefore, the error is classified under matter, although one could argue it is also an error in form. This shows the nuance in the classification. The commentator refers to al-Malawi's larger commentary for further discussion.

Key Terms
الاشْتِرَاك
الحَدُّ الوَسَط
المَادَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of 'qur'', why is the error classified as a material error rather than a formal error?
A) Because the form is valid, but the premises are false.
B) Because the lack of repetition of the middle term arises from the homonymous word, which is part of the matter.
C) Because the conclusion is false regardless of form.
D) Because the syllogism is in the correct figure.

Key Takeaway
An error in a syllogism is considered material when its root is the homonymous wording of the premises, even if the lack of repetition in meaning also constitutes a formal defect.

Error in Wording: Treating Distinct Terms as Synonyms
Author gives example of error by making distinct terms synonymous in premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of treating non-synonymous terms as synonyms in a syllogism and explain why it invalidates the argument.

Core Idea
Two terms can be related as general and specific, not synonyms; treating them as interchangeable destroys syllogistic validity.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary on al-Bannani exposes a subtle error in syllogistic reasoning: using two terms as if they were synonyms when they have a generality-specificity relationship. The example of sayf (sword) and sarim (cutter) shows how a conclusion can be false even if the premises seem plausible. This lesson teaches you to spot such material fallacies in definition and argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms in the premises
Find the two terms that are being treated as equivalent. In the example, they are 'sayf' and 'sarim'.
2
Determine the relationship between the terms
Check if they are true synonyms (interchangeable in all contexts) or if one is more general or specific. Here, 'sayf' includes non-cutting swords, while 'sarim' only refers to cutters, so 'sarim' is a subset of 'sayf'.
This is partial mutual exclusivity (al-tabayun al-juz'i).
3
Analyze the syllogism as if the terms were synonymous
The argument: 'This is a sayf. Every sayf is a sarim. Therefore this is a sarim.' Formally, this is valid (Barbara), but the major premise is false because not all sayf are sarim.
The error is in the matter (maddah), not the form.
4
Explain why the argument fails
Because 'sayf' and 'sarim' are not coextensive, the major premise is false. Treating them as synonyms leads to a false conclusion. The error is called jahl dhi tabayun mithl al-radif.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (أَوْ كَ: جَعَلِ ذَا تَبَايُنِ مِثلَ الرَّدِيفِ مَأخَذَا) لَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ ذَا بِمَعْنَى : صَاحِبٍ، فَكَانَ حَقُهَا الجِرَّ بِاليَاءِ، لَكِنَّهَا جَاءَتْ هُنَا بِالأَلِفِ عَلَى لُغَةِ القَصْرِ فِي الأَسْمَاءِ السِّتَّةِ؛ كَذَا قَالَ المُصَنِّفُ فِي شَرْحِهِ، لَكِنِ اعْتَرَضَهُ سَيِّدِي سَعِيد : بِأَنَّ لُغَةَ القَصْرِ إِنَّمَا هِيَ فِي: «أَبِ، وَ : أَخ، وَ حَمِ، لَا فِي: «ذِي، و : قم بِلَا مِيمٍ، فَإِنَّهُمَا إِنَّمَا يُعْرَبَانِ بِالأَحْرُفِ؛ كَمَا نَبَّهَ عَلَيْهِ المُرَادِيُّ، فَكَانَ الْأَوْلَى أَنْ يُبْدِلَ ذَلِكَ البَيْتَ؛ بِأَنْ يَقُولَ مَثَلاً: فِي اللَّفْظِ كَاشْتِرَاكِ أَوْ كَجَعْـلِ ذِي تباين مُرَادِفاً في الماخذ كَمَا نَقَلَهُ المَلوِيُّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ. وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ: «هَذَا سَيْفٌ) - مُشِيراً إِلَى غَيْرِ القَاطِعِ ، وَكُلُّ سَيْفٍ صَادِمٌ» - وَتُرِيدُ القَاطِعَ - يَنْتُجُ : هَذَا صَارِمٌ ، فَإِنَّ الخَطَأَ فِي ذَلِكَ فِي مَادَّتِهِ بِسَبَبِ جَعْلِ المُبَاينِ مِثْلَ الرَّدِيفِ فِي أَخْذِهِ فِي المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : زَيْدٌ إِنْسَانُ، وَكُلُّ بَشَرٍ حَيَوَانٌ». وَإِنَّمَا كَانَ الصَّارِمُ مُبَايِناً لِلسَّيْفِ؛ لأَنَّ السَّيْفَ اسْمٌ لِمَا كَانَ عَلَى الهَيْئَةِ المَعْلُومَةِ وَلَوْ غَيْرَ قَاطِع، وَالصَّارِمُ اسْمٌ لِذَلِكَ بِقَيْدِ أَنْ يَكُونَ قَاطِعاً، فَبَيْنَهُمَا العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ بِإِطْلَاقٍ، فَبَيْنَهُمَا التَّبَايُنُ الجزئي. قَوْلُهُ : (مِثْلَ الرَّدِيْفِ مَأْخَذَا) أَيْ : مِثْلَهُ فِي الْأَخْذِ فِي المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ.
His statement: 'or like making a possessor of mutual exclusivity like a synonym taken' — it is not hidden that 'dha' means 'possessor', so its right is to be in the genitive with the ya, but it came here with alif according to the language of shortening in the six nouns; thus said the author in his commentary. But Sayyid Sa'id objected to him: that the language of shortening is only in 'ab, akh, ham', not in 'dhi' and 'qam' without a mim, for they are only declined with the letters; as al-Muradi pointed out. So it would have been better to replace that line; by saying e.g.: 'fi al-lafz k-ishtirak aw ka-ja'li dhi tabayun muradifan fi al-makhidh' as al-Malawi transmitted in his 'Kabir'. And an example of that is that you say: 'This is a sword (sayf)' — pointing to a non-cutting [object] — and 'Every sword (sayf) is a cutter (sarim)' — but you intend the cutter — it yields: 'This is a cutter (sarim).' So the error in that is in its matter because of making the mutually exclusive like a synonym in taking it in the two premises; as in your saying: 'Zayd is a human (insan), and every human (bashar) is an animal (hayawan).' And the reason why 'sarim' is mutually exclusive of 'sayf' is that 'sayf' is a name for what is in the known shape even if it is not cutting, and 'sarim' is a name for that with the condition that it be cutting; so between them is absolute generality and specificity, so between them is partial mutual exclusivity. His statement: 'like the synonym taken' means: like it in the taking in the two premises.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage first engages in a grammatical critique of the matn line. The word 'dha' (possessor) in the phrase 'dha tabayun' is in the nominative with alif, but according to Sa'id's objection, it should be 'dhi' (genitive) because of the preposition 'ka'. The commentator suggests an alternative line from al-Malawi. Then the main logical point is presented: an example of error in wording caused by treating two terms that are not synonymous as if they were synonyms. The example uses 'sayf' (sword) and 'sarim' (cutter). The speaker points to an object that has the shape of a sword but is not cutting, calls it 'sayf', and states 'Every sayf is a sarim' (intending that all swords are cutters), but the intended conclusion is that this object is a sarim (cutter). The error is that 'sayf' and 'sarim' are mutually exclusive (mutabayin) because 'sayf' is broader (includes non-cutting swords) while 'sarim' is more specific (only cutting swords). Treating them as synonyms (radif) in the premises leads to a false conclusion. The commentator explains the relationship as generality and specificity, making them partially distinct. This is a material error because it is the wording that causes the confusion.

Key Terms
جَعَلِ ذَا تَبَايُنِ مِثلَ الرَّدِيفِ
المُبَايِن
التَّبَايُنُ الجزئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are 'sayf' and 'sarim' considered mutually exclusive (mubayin) in the example?
A) Because they are completely different in meaning.
B) Because 'sayf' has a broader meaning than 'sarim', and they are not fully synonymous.
C) Because 'sarim' is a type of 'sayf' but the example uses them as synonyms.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a syllogism depends not only on its form but also on the correct understanding of its terms; treating partially distinct terms as synonyms corrupts the material truth of the premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the argument: 'Every intoxicant (musqir) is forbidden; this beverage is an intoxicant (musqir); therefore it is forbidden.' If the first 'musqir' means only grape-based intoxicants, but the second means any intoxicant, identify the fallacy and explain using the concepts from this lesson.
?
Provide an original example from fiqh or usul al-fiqh where two terms are mistakenly treated as synonyms in a syllogistic argument. Describe the terms and explain the error.

Error in Meaning: Distinguishing False from True Propositions
Author defines error in meaning and addresses an objection based on homonymy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an error in syllogistic reasoning originates from meaning or wording, using the criteria of false propositions that resemble true ones.

Core Idea
A false proposition that looks true can cause error in both meaning and wording.

In classical Islamic logic, errors in syllogisms are classified into errors in wording (لفظ) and errors in meaning (معنى). This passage defines error in meaning as the confusion of a false proposition with a true one. However, an important objection shows that this same confusion can arise from homonymy, which is an error in wording. Understanding this nuance helps you pinpoint the source of fallacious reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of error in meaning
The passage states error in meaning occurs when a false proposition (قضية كاذبة) resembles a true proposition (قضية ذات صدق) and is mistaken for it.
This is the author's initial claim.
2
Recognize the objection
An objector argues that this confusion can also be a cause of error in wording. The example given shows a syllogism where a false major premise (Every eye is a flowing spring) resembles a true one due to homonymy (the term 'eye' has two meanings).
The error is in wording because the ambiguity of 'eye' leads to the false premise looking true.
3
Analyze the example syllogism
The example: 'This is an eye' (pointing to the sight organ) and 'Every eye is a flowing spring' (intending the sight organ) yields 'This is a flowing spring.' The major premise is false but looks true if 'eye' were taken as a spring.
The error is not in the form but in the matter (مادة) – the falsehood of the premise.
4
Distinguish wording vs. meaning error
The commentator notes that some scholars classify such errors as either wording or meaning depending on perspective. The confusion of false with true is the underlying mechanism, but the cause (homonymy vs. conceptual confusion) determines the classification.
This refines the definition: error in meaning is not exclusive; the same confusion can arise from wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (وَفِي المَعَانِي مُقَابِلُ لِقَوْلِهِ : فِي اللَّفْظِ»، كَمَا مَرَّ . قَوْلُهُ : (لِالْتِبَاسِ الكَاذِبَهُ . . بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ أَيْ : لِاشْتِبَاءِ القَضِيَّةِ الكَاذِبَةِ بِقَضِيَّةٍ ذَاتِ صِدْقٍ؛ بِأَنْ كَانَتْ تِلْكَ القَضِيَّةُ مِنَ القَضَايَا الشَّبيهَةِ بِالحَقِّ وَلَيْسَتْ بِهِ. وَاعْتُرِضَ عَلَى المُصَنِّفِ فِي جَعْلِهِ ذَلِكَ عِلَّةٌ لِلخَطَأَ فِي المَعْنَى ؛ بـ : أَنَّهُ قَدْ يَكُونُ عِلَّةٌ لِلخَطأ فِي اللَّفْظِ بِسَبَبِ الاشْتِرَاكِ؛ كَمَا إِذَا قُلْتَ : هَذِهِ عَيْنٌ» - مُثِيراً لِلبَاصِرَةِ - وَكُلُّ عَيْنٍ جَارِيَةٌ» - وَتُرِيدُ البَاصِرَةَ - يَنْتُجُ : هَذِهِ جَارِيَةٌ، فَإِنَّ الخَطأ فِي ذَلِكَ فِي مَادَّتِهِ مِنْ جِهَةِ اللَّفْظِ لِاليَبَاسِ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ؛ إِذِ الكُبْرَى كَاذِبَةٌ شَبِيهَةٌ بِالصَّادِقَةِ، وَقَدْ نَصَّ بَعْضُهُمْ عَلَى أَنَّ ذَلِكَ إِمَّا مِنْ جِهَةِ اللَّفْظِ وَإِمَّا مِنْ جهَةِ المَعْنَى.
His statement: 'and in the meanings' is the counterpart of his statement: 'in the wording', as previously. His statement: 'because of the confusion of the false one with a possessor of truth' means: because of the resemblance of the false proposition to a proposition possessing truth; such that that proposition is among the propositions resembling the truth and is not it. And an objection was raised against the author in his making that a cause for error in meaning: that it may be a cause for error in wording due to homonymy; as when you say: 'This is an eye' — pointing to the [organ of] sight — and 'Every eye is a flowing spring' — and you intend the [organ of] sight — it yields: 'This is a flowing spring.' So the error in that is in its matter from the aspect of wording due to the confusion of the false with the possessor of truth; for the major premise is false resembling the true one, and some have stated that that is either from the aspect of wording or from the aspect of meaning.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage defines error in meaning (khata' fi al-ma'ani). The commentator explains the phrase 'confusion of the false with a possessor of truth' as a false proposition that resembles a true one. The author of the matn (al-Akhdari?) is criticized for making this the exclusive cause of error in meaning, because an example of homonymy (eye/spring) also involves a false major premise that resembles a true one. In the example, 'This is an eye' (pointing to the eye) and 'Every eye is a flowing spring' (intending the eye) yields a false conclusion. The major premise 'Every eye is a flowing spring' is false (since many eyes are not springs) but resembles a true statement if 'eye' were taken in its other meaning. Thus, this confusion can occur in both wording (due to homonymy) and meaning. The commentator notes that some scholars say such errors can be classified either way depending on perspective.

Key Terms
الْتَبَاسُ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ (iltibas al-kadhiba bi dhat sidq): Confusion of a false proposition with a true one; a false premise that looks like a true premise.
قَضِيَّةٌ شَبِيهَةٌ بِالحَقِّ (qadiyya shabiha bi al-haqq): A proposition that resembles the truth but is not true; a false proposition that is easily mistaken for true.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the objection in the passage, what does the example of 'eye' demonstrate?
A) Error in meaning is impossible.
B) The confusion of false with true can also cause error in wording due to homonymy.
C) Error in wording is always caused by false premises.
D) The author's definition is strictly correct.

Key Takeaway
Confusion of a false proposition with a true one can cause both error in meaning and error in wording; the classification depends on whether the source is conceptual or linguistic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. This drink is an intoxicant. Therefore, this drink is forbidden.' If the minor premise is false because the drink is not actually an intoxicant but resembles one, is the error in wording or meaning? Explain using the passage's criteria.
?
Find an example of a false proposition that resembles a true one due to homonymy in a Fiqh context (e.g., the word 'qadi' can mean judge or prayer). Construct a syllogism with that ambiguity and classify the error.

Treating Accidental as Essential
Bajuri explains the fallacy of treating accidental as essential with definition and example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of treating an accidental attribute as essential in a categorical syllogism and explain why it leads to a material error.

Core Idea
Confusing an accidental property with an essential one makes a premise false while appearing true.

This lesson explores the material fallacy of treating an accidental attribute as essential. Bajuri defines accidental as what is established through an intermediary, and essential as without intermediary. Using the example of movement on a ship versus self-movement, we see how a seemingly valid syllogism yields a false conclusion due to equivocation in the middle term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the accidental and essential senses
Determine which predicate is used in the premise: 'moving' can mean by ship's motion (accidental) or by itself (essential).
Bajuri defines 'araḍī and dhātī explicitly.
2
Locate the middle term
The middle term is 'mover' (mutaharrik). It appears in both premises but with different meanings.
The major premise uses 'mover' in the essential sense; the minor uses it accidentally.
3
Check the conclusion
The conclusion follows formally but is false in reality because the premises were not both true in the same sense.
The error is in the matter, not the form.
4
Classify the fallacy
This is a material fallacy due to confusion of the false with the true, as the accidental premise is false when interpreted essentially.
Bajuri calls it 'iltibās al-kādhiba bi-dhāti ṣidq'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة
(١٢٤) وَفِي المَعَانِي»: لالْتِبَاسِ الكَاذِبَهُ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ، فَافْهَمِ المُخَاطَبَهُ (١٢٥) كـ: مِثْلِ جَعَلِ العَرَضِيْ كَالذَّاتِي أَوْ نَاتِجِ إحْدَى المُقَدِّمَـاتِ
الباجوري
وَأُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ جَعْلَهُ ذَلِكَ عِلَّةٌ لِلخَطَأَ فِي المَعْنَى لَا يُنَافِي أَنَّهُ قَدْ يَكُونُ عِلَّةٌ لِلخَطَأَ فِي اللَّفْظِ ؛ عَلَى أَنَّهُ قَدْ يُقَالُ : أَنَّ قَوْلَهُ : الِالْتِبَاسِ ... إلخ» رَاجِعُ لِلأَمْرَيْنِ (۱) ؛ أَعْنِي : الخَطَأَ فِي اللَّفْظِ وَالخَطَأ فِي المَعْنَى ؛ أَفَادَهُ المَلَّوِيُّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ» (۲)
قَوْلُهُ : (فَافْهَمِ المُخَاطَبَهُ) أَي : الكَلَامَ المُخَاطَبَ بِهِ ، فَالمَصْدَرُ بِمَعْنَى اسْمِ المَفْعُول . قَوْلُهُ : كَمِثْلِ جَعْلِ العَرَضِيْ كَالذَّاتِي (۳) «الكَافُ : زَائِدَةُ ، أَوْ أَنَّ مِثْلِ» لِتَأْكِيدِ مَعْنَى «الكَافِ»؛
كَمَا قِيلَ بِذَلِكَ فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى : لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْ) [الشورى: ١١].
وَالمُرَادُ بِـ «العَرَضِيّ» هُنَا : مَا ثَبَتَ لِلشَّيْء بِوَاسِطَةِ غَيْرِهِ؛ كَمَا فِي : المُتَحَرِّكِ بِحَرَكَةِ السَّفِينَةِ،
وَبِـ (الذَّاتِيِّ : مَا ثَبَتَ لِلشَّيْءٍ مِنْ غَيْرِ وَاسِطَةٍ ؛ كَمَا فِي : المُتَحَرِّكِ بِذَاتِهِ . وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ: «الجَالِسُ فِي السَّفِينَةِ مُتَحَرِّكُ - وَتُرِيدُ مُتَحَرِّكُ بِالتَّحَرُّكِ العَرَضِي - وَكُلُّ مُتَحَرِّكِ لَا يَثْبُتُ فِي مَوْضِعٍ» - وَتُرِيدُ مَا ذُكِرَ - يَنْتُجُ : الجَالِسُ فِي السَّفِينَةِ لَا يَثْبُتُ فِي مَوْضِعٍ، فَإِنَّ الخَطَأ فِي ذَلِكَ فِي مَادَّتِهِ مِنْ حَيْثُ المَعْنَى لِالْتِبَاسِ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ بِسَبَبِ جَعْلِ العَرَضِيِّ وَهُوَ المُتَحَرِّكُ بِحَرَكَةِ السَّفِينَةِ كَالذَّاتِي وَهُوَ التَّحَرُّكُ بِالذَّاتِ.
قَوْلُهُ : (أَوْ نَاتِجِ إِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَاتِ) المُرَادُ بِـ «النَّاتِحِ) : النَّتيجَةُ .
وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ : هَذِهِ نُقْلَةٌ، وَكُلُّ نُقْلَةٍ حَرَكَةٌ يَنْتُجُ : هَذِهِ حَرَكَةٌ»، وَهِيَ عَيْنُ إِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، وَمَحَلُّ ذَلِكَ : إِذَا لَمْ يُرِدِ الإِخْبَارَ (٤) بِأَنَّ النُّقْلَةَ تُسَمَّى : «حَرَكَةٌ»، وَإِلَّا حَصَلَتِ المُغَايَرَةُ بِاعْتِبَارِ مُلَاحَظَةِ التَّسْمِيَةِ .
(۱) الشرشيمي : قوله : (رَاجِعُ لِلأَمْرَيْنِ ... إلخ) هذا الجواب لا يناسب ؛ لأنَّه يُفيد أنَّ الخطأ في المادَّة من جهة اللفظ ، والتباس الكاذبة بالصَّادقة دائماً، مع أنَّه ليس كذلك ؛ بدليل هَذِهِ عَيْنٌ ... إلخ»، فإنَّهما صادقتان مع وجود الخطأ في المادَّة من جهة اللفظ، فالمتعين الجواب الأول.
وَقَدْ بَحَثَ سَيِّدِي سَعِيدٌ فِي كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ : بِأَنَّ الخَطَأ فِيمَا جُعِلَتْ فِيهِ النَّتِيجَةُ إِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَاتِ لَيْسَ فِي المَعْنَى لِاِلْتِبَاسِ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ ؛ لِصِدْقِ كُلِّ مِنَ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، بَلْ لَيْسَ مِنْ جِهَةِ المَادَّةِ أَصْلاً ، وَإِنَّمَا هُوَ مِنْ جِهَةِ أَنَّ النَّتِيجَةَ لَيْسَتْ مُغَايرَةٌ لِلمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، وَالوَاجِبُ أَنْ تَكُونَ مُغَايِرَةٌ لَهُمَا ، كَمَا عُرِفَ فِي حَدِّ القِيَاسِ . وَأَجَابَ الشَّيْخُ المَلَّوِيُّ فِي شَرْحِهِ الكَبِيرِ حَيْثُ قَالَ : وَإِذَا دَقَقْتَ النَّظَرَ وَجَدْتَ إِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ كَاذِبَةٌ ؛ لِأَنَّ فِيهَا حَمْلُ الشَّيْءِ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ ، وَالحَمْلُ يَقْتَضِي المُغَايرَةَ، وَمُغَايرَةُ الشَّيْءِ لِنَفْسِهِ مُخَالِفَةٌ لِلوَاقِعِ ، فَتَكُونُ كَاذِبَةٌ شَبِيهَةٌ بِالصَّادِقَةِ، فَصَحَّ جَعْلُ ذَلِكَ مِنْ أَنْوَاعِ الْتِبَاسِ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ . اهـ وَلَا يَخْفَى مَا فِيهِ مِنَ التَّكَلُّفِ
Conclusion. (124) And in the material [fallacies] it is due to confusing the false with the true, so understand the addressed speech. (125) Like treating the accidental as essential, or making the conclusion one of the premises. Al-Bajuri: And it was answered: That making that a cause for error in meaning does not negate that it may be a cause for error in wording; moreover, it may be said that his saying 'the confusion...etc.' refers to both matters: the error in wording and the error in meaning; al-Mallawi conveyed this in his big [commentary]. His saying: 'so understand the addressed speech' means the speech addressed, the verbal noun in the sense of the passive participle. His saying: 'like treating the accidental as essential' -- the kaf is extra, or 'like' is for emphasis of the meaning of kaf, as is said in His saying: 'There is nothing like Him' (42:11). And by 'accidental' here is meant what is established for a thing through an intermediary, like the mover by the movement of a ship; and by 'essential' what is established for a thing without intermediary, like the mover by itself. An example of that is to say: 'The one sitting in the ship is moving' -- meaning accidentally -- and 'Every mover does not remain in a place' -- meaning what was mentioned -- yields: 'The one sitting in the ship does not remain in a place.' The error in that is in its matter from the aspect of meaning, due to confusing the false with the true, because of treating the accidental (the mover by the ship's movement) as essential (motion by itself). His saying: 'or making the conclusion one of the premises' -- by 'product' is meant the conclusion. An example of that is to say: 'This is a nuqla (a certain motion), and every nuqla is a movement' yields 'This is a movement', and it is identical to one of the two premises. The place of that is when one does not intend to inform that nuqla is called 'movement'; otherwise, difference occurs by consideration of naming. (1) Al-Shirshimi: His saying: 'refers to both matters...etc.' this answer is not appropriate, because it conveys that the error in the matter is from the aspect of wording, and the confusion of the false with the true always, but that is not the case; evidence is 'this is a movement...etc.' because both are true while error in the matter from wording exists, so the first answer is the one to adopt. And Sayyid Sa'id investigated in the author's words: that the error in what the conclusion is made one of the premises is not in meaning due to confusing the false with the true, because both premises are true. Rather, it is not from the aspect of matter at all; it is from the aspect that the conclusion is not different from the premises, and it must be different from them, as known in the definition of syllogism. Al-Mallawi responded in his big commentary saying: And if you examine closely, you will find that one of the premises is false, because it includes predicating a thing of itself, and predication entails difference, and difference of a thing from itself is contrary to reality, so it is false resembling the true. So it is correct to make that a type of confusion of the false with the true. End. It is not hidden what is in it of artificiality.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage from Bajuri's super-commentary addresses two types of material fallacies in syllogisms. The first is treating an accidental attribute as an essential one. Bajuri defines 'accidental' as what belongs to a thing through an intermediary, like motion from a ship's movement, and 'essential' as what belongs without intermediary, like self-motion. The fallacy occurs when a proposition uses an accidental attribute as if it were essential, leading to a false conclusion that appears true. The example: 'The one sitting in the ship is moving' (using accidental motion) and 'Every mover does not remain in a place' (using essential motion) yields 'The one sitting in the ship does not remain in a place', which is false. The error is in the matter (content) due to confusing the false premise (accidental motion taken as essential) with a true one. The second fallacy is making the conclusion identical to one of the premises. The example: 'This is a nuqla, and every nuqla is a movement' yields 'This is a movement', which is exactly the major premise. This is fallacious because the conclusion should be distinct. A discussion follows: Sayyid Sa'id objects that both premises are true, so the error is not from matter but from failure to meet the condition of difference between premises and conclusion. Al-Mallawi defends that the premise 'every nuqla is a movement' is actually false if it is a tautology? He says it involves predication of a thing of itself, which is false because predication requires difference. Bajuri notes the artificiality of this response.

Key Terms
عرضي
ذاتي
التباس الكاذبة بذات صدق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example given by Bajuri, why is the conclusion 'The one sitting in the ship does not remain in a place' false?
A) Because the major premise is false.
B) Because the minor premise uses 'moving' in an accidental sense while the major uses it essentially.
C) Because both premises are false.
D) Because the syllogism is formally invalid.

Key Takeaway
Treating an accidental attribute as essential creates a material fallacy by equivocating the middle term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the accidental and essential senses of a predicate in a fiqh example: e.g., 'This sale is valid' where 'valid' might be accidental (due to consent) or essential (by definition). Explain how confusion would lead to a fallacy.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Fallacy of Making Conclusion One of the Premises
Bajuri explains the fallacy of making the conclusion identical to a premise, with commentary debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of a syllogism whose conclusion is identical to one of its premises and evaluate the scholarly debate about its nature.

Core Idea
A conclusion must be distinct from the premises; identity violates the definition of syllogism.

This lesson examines the second material fallacy: when the conclusion is exactly one of the premises. The example 'This is a nuqla; every nuqla is a movement' yields 'This is a movement', which is the major premise. This seems formally valid but is problematic. Scholars like Sayyid Sa'id and al-Mallawi debate whether this is a material or formal fallacy, and whether the premise is actually false due to tautology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Minor: 'This is a nuqla'; Major: 'Every nuqla is a movement'; Conclusion: 'This is a movement'.
Notice conclusion equals major premise.
2
Check against syllogism definition
The standard definition requires the conclusion to be different from the premises.
Bajuri references this condition.
3
Analyze the debate
Sayyid Sa'id says both premises are true so error is not material; Mallawi says one premise is false because predication of a thing of itself is false.
Bajuri finds Mallawi's response 'artificial'.
4
Synthesize
The fallacy is either formal (failing the distinctness condition) or material (if the premise is actually false due to tautology).
Most later logicians consider it a formal condition.

خاتمة
(١٢٤) وَفِي المَعَانِي»: لالْتِبَاسِ الكَاذِبَهُ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ، فَافْهَمِ المُخَاطَبَهُ (١٢٥) كـ: مِثْلِ جَعَلِ العَرَضِيْ كَالذَّاتِي أَوْ نَاتِجِ إحْدَى المُقَدِّمَـاتِ
الباجوري
وَأُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ جَعْلَهُ ذَلِكَ عِلَّةٌ لِلخَطَأَ فِي المَعْنَى لَا يُنَافِي أَنَّهُ قَدْ يَكُونُ عِلَّةٌ لِلخَطَأَ فِي اللَّفْظِ ؛ عَلَى أَنَّهُ قَدْ يُقَالُ : أَنَّ قَوْلَهُ : الِالْتِبَاسِ ... إلخ» رَاجِعُ لِلأَمْرَيْنِ (۱) ؛ أَعْنِي : الخَطَأَ فِي اللَّفْظِ وَالخَطَأ فِي المَعْنَى ؛ أَفَادَهُ المَلَّوِيُّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ» (۲)
قَوْلُهُ : (فَافْهَمِ المُخَاطَبَهُ) أَي : الكَلَامَ المُخَاطَبَ بِهِ ، فَالمَصْدَرُ بِمَعْنَى اسْمِ المَفْعُول . قَوْلُهُ : كَمِثْلِ جَعْلِ العَرَضِيْ كَالذَّاتِي (۳) «الكَافُ : زَائِدَةُ ، أَوْ أَنَّ مِثْلِ» لِتَأْكِيدِ مَعْنَى «الكَافِ»؛
كَمَا قِيلَ بِذَلِكَ فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى : لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْ) [الشورى: ١١].
وَالمُرَادُ بِـ «العَرَضِيّ» هُنَا : مَا ثَبَتَ لِلشَّيْء بِوَاسِطَةِ غَيْرِهِ؛ كَمَا فِي : المُتَحَرِّكِ بِحَرَكَةِ السَّفِينَةِ،
وَبِـ (الذَّاتِيِّ : مَا ثَبَتَ لِلشَّيْءٍ مِنْ غَيْرِ وَاسِطَةٍ ؛ كَمَا فِي : المُتَحَرِّكِ بِذَاتِهِ . وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ: «الجَالِسُ فِي السَّفِينَةِ مُتَحَرِّكُ - وَتُرِيدُ مُتَحَرِّكُ بِالتَّحَرُّكِ العَرَضِي - وَكُلُّ مُتَحَرِّكِ لَا يَثْبُتُ فِي مَوْضِعٍ» - وَتُرِيدُ مَا ذُكِرَ - يَنْتُجُ : الجَالِسُ فِي السَّفِينَةِ لَا يَثْبُتُ فِي مَوْضِعٍ، فَإِنَّ الخَطَأ فِي ذَلِكَ فِي مَادَّتِهِ مِنْ حَيْثُ المَعْنَى لِالْتِبَاسِ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ بِسَبَبِ جَعْلِ العَرَضِيِّ وَهُوَ المُتَحَرِّكُ بِحَرَكَةِ السَّفِينَةِ كَالذَّاتِي وَهُوَ التَّحَرُّكُ بِالذَّاتِ.
قَوْلُهُ : (أَوْ نَاتِجِ إِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَاتِ) المُرَادُ بِـ «النَّاتِحِ) : النَّتيجَةُ .
وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ : هَذِهِ نُقْلَةٌ، وَكُلُّ نُقْلَةٍ حَرَكَةٌ يَنْتُجُ : هَذِهِ حَرَكَةٌ»، وَهِيَ عَيْنُ إِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، وَمَحَلُّ ذَلِكَ : إِذَا لَمْ يُرِدِ الإِخْبَارَ (٤) بِأَنَّ النُّقْلَةَ تُسَمَّى : «حَرَكَةٌ»، وَإِلَّا حَصَلَتِ المُغَايَرَةُ بِاعْتِبَارِ مُلَاحَظَةِ التَّسْمِيَةِ .
(۱) الشرشيمي : قوله : (رَاجِعُ لِلأَمْرَيْنِ ... إلخ) هذا الجواب لا يناسب ؛ لأنَّه يُفيد أنَّ الخطأ في المادَّة من جهة اللفظ ، والتباس الكاذبة بالصَّادقة دائماً، مع أنَّه ليس كذلك ؛ بدليل هَذِهِ عَيْنٌ ... إلخ»، فإنَّهما صادقتان مع وجود الخطأ في المادَّة من جهة اللفظ، فالمتعين الجواب الأول.
وَقَدْ بَحَثَ سَيِّدِي سَعِيدٌ فِي كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ : بِأَنَّ الخَطَأ فِيمَا جُعِلَتْ فِيهِ النَّتِيجَةُ إِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَاتِ لَيْسَ فِي المَعْنَى لِاِلْتِبَاسِ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ ؛ لِصِدْقِ كُلِّ مِنَ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، بَلْ لَيْسَ مِنْ جِهَةِ المَادَّةِ أَصْلاً ، وَإِنَّمَا هُوَ مِنْ جِهَةِ أَنَّ النَّتِيجَةَ لَيْسَتْ مُغَايرَةٌ لِلمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، وَالوَاجِبُ أَنْ تَكُونَ مُغَايِرَةٌ لَهُمَا ، كَمَا عُرِفَ فِي حَدِّ القِيَاسِ . وَأَجَابَ الشَّيْخُ المَلَّوِيُّ فِي شَرْحِهِ الكَبِيرِ حَيْثُ قَالَ : وَإِذَا دَقَقْتَ النَّظَرَ وَجَدْتَ إِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ كَاذِبَةٌ ؛ لِأَنَّ فِيهَا حَمْلُ الشَّيْءِ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ ، وَالحَمْلُ يَقْتَضِي المُغَايرَةَ، وَمُغَايرَةُ الشَّيْءِ لِنَفْسِهِ مُخَالِفَةٌ لِلوَاقِعِ ، فَتَكُونُ كَاذِبَةٌ شَبِيهَةٌ بِالصَّادِقَةِ، فَصَحَّ جَعْلُ ذَلِكَ مِنْ أَنْوَاعِ الْتِبَاسِ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ . اهـ وَلَا يَخْفَى مَا فِيهِ مِنَ التَّكَلُّفِ
Conclusion. (124) And in the material [fallacies] it is due to confusing the false with the true, so understand the addressed speech. (125) Like treating the accidental as essential, or making the conclusion one of the premises. Al-Bajuri: And it was answered: That making that a cause for error in meaning does not negate that it may be a cause for error in wording; moreover, it may be said that his saying 'the confusion...etc.' refers to both matters: the error in wording and the error in meaning; al-Mallawi conveyed this in his big [commentary]. His saying: 'so understand the addressed speech' means the speech addressed, the verbal noun in the sense of the passive participle. His saying: 'like treating the accidental as essential' -- the kaf is extra, or 'like' is for emphasis of the meaning of kaf, as is said in His saying: 'There is nothing like Him' (42:11). And by 'accidental' here is meant what is established for a thing through an intermediary, like the mover by the movement of a ship; and by 'essential' what is established for a thing without intermediary, like the mover by itself. An example of that is to say: 'The one sitting in the ship is moving' -- meaning accidentally -- and 'Every mover does not remain in a place' -- meaning what was mentioned -- yields: 'The one sitting in the ship does not remain in a place.' The error in that is in its matter from the aspect of meaning, due to confusing the false with the true, because of treating the accidental (the mover by the ship's movement) as essential (motion by itself). His saying: 'or making the conclusion one of the premises' -- by 'product' is meant the conclusion. An example of that is to say: 'This is a nuqla (a certain motion), and every nuqla is a movement' yields 'This is a movement', and it is identical to one of the two premises. The place of that is when one does not intend to inform that nuqla is called 'movement'; otherwise, difference occurs by consideration of naming. (1) Al-Shirshimi: His saying: 'refers to both matters...etc.' this answer is not appropriate, because it conveys that the error in the matter is from the aspect of wording, and the confusion of the false with the true always, but that is not the case; evidence is 'this is a movement...etc.' because both are true while error in the matter from wording exists, so the first answer is the one to adopt. And Sayyid Sa'id investigated in the author's words: that the error in what the conclusion is made one of the premises is not in meaning due to confusing the false with the true, because both premises are true. Rather, it is not from the aspect of matter at all; it is from the aspect that the conclusion is not different from the premises, and it must be different from them, as known in the definition of syllogism. Al-Mallawi responded in his big commentary saying: And if you examine closely, you will find that one of the premises is false, because it includes predicating a thing of itself, and predication entails difference, and difference of a thing from itself is contrary to reality, so it is false resembling the true. So it is correct to make that a type of confusion of the false with the true. End. It is not hidden what is in it of artificiality.

Commentary
This passage from Bajuri's super-commentary addresses two types of material fallacies in syllogisms. The first is treating an accidental attribute as an essential one. Bajuri defines 'accidental' as what belongs to a thing through an intermediary, like motion from a ship's movement, and 'essential' as what belongs without intermediary, like self-motion. The fallacy occurs when a proposition uses an accidental attribute as if it were essential, leading to a false conclusion that appears true. The example: 'The one sitting in the ship is moving' (using accidental motion) and 'Every mover does not remain in a place' (using essential motion) yields 'The one sitting in the ship does not remain in a place', which is false. The error is in the matter (content) due to confusing the false premise (accidental motion taken as essential) with a true one. The second fallacy is making the conclusion identical to one of the premises. The example: 'This is a nuqla, and every nuqla is a movement' yields 'This is a movement', which is exactly the major premise. This is fallacious because the conclusion should be distinct. A discussion follows: Sayyid Sa'id objects that both premises are true, so the error is not from matter but from failure to meet the condition of difference between premises and conclusion. Al-Mallawi defends that the premise 'every nuqla is a movement' is actually false if it is a tautology? He says it involves predication of a thing of itself, which is false because predication requires difference. Bajuri notes the artificiality of this response.

مقدمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Sayyid Sa'id, why is the fallacy of making the conclusion one of the premises not a material fallacy?
A) Because both premises are false.
B) Because both premises are true, so there is no confusion of false with true.
C) Because the conclusion is false.
D) Because the syllogism is invalid.

Key Takeaway
A proper syllogism must yield a conclusion distinct from its premises; identity indicates either a formal defect or a tautological premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a categorical syllogism from a fiqh text where the conclusion is not new. Explain why it is fallacious either as a formal or material error.
