Fallacy: Judging Genus by Species' Property
Al-Bajuri explains the fallacy of judging genus by species' property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of judging a genus by a property of its species and evaluate whether it constitutes a confusion of false with true.

Core Idea
A false premise that does not resemble truth is not a confusion fallacy.

This lesson examines the material fallacy of applying a species-specific property to a genus. Al-Bajuri provides an example and a subsequent scholarly debate questions whether this fallacy belongs to the category of 'confusion of false with true'. You will learn to recognise this fallacy and understand the nuances in classifying logical errors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fallacy's structure
Examine the example: 'This is an animal; every animal is rational; therefore this is rational.' Notice that rationality is a property of humans, not all animals. The genus 'animal' is judged by the property of the species 'human'.
This pattern is the core of the fallacy.
2
Distinguish form from matter
The syllogism is formally valid (first figure, Barbara), but the matter is false because the major premise is false. The error lies in the content, not the form.
3
Analyse the objection
Al-Sabban argues that this fallacy does not involve confusion of false with true because the false premise is obviously false and does not resemble a true statement. Therefore, it does not mislead by resemblance.
This is a critical refinement of the classification of fallacies.
4
Receive the response
The rejoinder is that confusion of false with true is not the cause of all error, only for a subset. This fallacy is error, but not necessarily that specific type.
Understand that the author acknowledges the objection but retains the fallacy under a broader category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١٢٦) وَالحُكْمِ لِلْجِنْسِ بِحُكْمِ النَّوْعِ وَجَعْلِ كَالقَطْعِيِّ غَيْرِ القَطْعِي
الباجوري
قَوْلُهُ : (وَالحُكْمِ لِلْجِنْسِ بِحُكْمِ النَّوْعِ) «اللَّامُ فِيهِ بِمَعْنَى : عَلَى».
وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ : هَذَا حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ نَاطِقُ يَنْتُجُ : «هَذَا نَاطِقٌ»، فَإِنَّ الخَطَأ فِي ذَلِكَ فِي مَادَّتِهِ مِنْ حَيْثُ المَعْنَى بِسَبَبِ الحُكْمِ عَلَى الجِنْسِ بِحُكْمِ النَّوْعِ .
وَبَحَثَ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ فِي كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ (٥) : بِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ فِي ذَلِكَ التِبَاسُ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ؛ لأَنَّ المُقَدِّمَةَ الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا عَلَى الجِنْسِ بِحُكْمِ النَّوْعِ لَيْسَتْ شَبيهَةٌ بِالصَّادِقَةِ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ كَاذِبَةٌ، وَحِينَئِذٍ فَلَا يَصِحُ جَعْلُ ذَلِكَ مِنْ أَنْوَاعِ الْتِبَاسِ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ ؛ قَالَ : وَيُمْكِنُ أَنْ يُقَالَ : الْتِبَاسُ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ لَيْسَ عِلَّةٌ لِجَمِيعِ الخَطَأ، بَلْ لِمَجْمُوعِهَا ، فَافْهَمْ
قَوْلُهُ : (وَجَعْلِ كَالقَطْعِيِّ غَيْرِ القَطْعِيْ) بِجَرٌ غَيْرِ) بِإِضَافَةِ جَعْلِ» إِلَيْهِ، مِنْ إِضَافَةِ المَصْدَرِ لِمَفْعُولِهِ الأَوَّلِ، وَفَصَلَ بَيْنَ المُضَافِ وَالمُضَافِ إِلَيْهِ بِالمَفْعُولِ الثَّانِي وَهُوَ قَوْلُهُ: «كَالقَطْعِي»
(٥) هو الصبان، انظر: «حاشية الصَّبَّان على الشَّرح الصَّغير للملوي» (ص : ٥٧٨).
(126) And judging the genus by the property of the species, and treating the non-certain as certain. Al-Bajuri: His saying: 'and judging the genus by the property of the species' -- the lam in it means 'upon'. An example of that is to say: 'This is an animal, and every animal is rational' yields 'This is rational'. The error in that is in its matter from the aspect of meaning, because of judging the genus by the property of the species. And one of the investigators (5) examined the author's words: that there is no confusion of the false with the true in that, because the premise that judged the genus by the property of the species is not resembling the true, even if it is false. Therefore, it is not valid to make that a type of confusion of the false with the true. He said: It can be said: confusion of the false with the true is not a cause for all error, but for a collection of them. So understand. His saying: 'and treating the non-certain as certain' -- with jar on 'non-certain' due to the idafa of 'treating' to it, from the idafa of the verbal noun to its first object, and between the mudaf and mudaf ilayhi is separated by the second object, which is his saying 'like the certain'. (5) He is al-Sabban; see: Hashiyat al-Sabban 'ala al-Sharh al-Saghir li-l-Mallawi (p. 578).
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage explains two additional material fallacies: judging the genus by the property of the species, and treating a non-certain proposition as certain. The first fallacy occurs when one attributes a property specific to a species to the entire genus. For example, the syllogism 'This is an animal; every animal is rational' is invalid because rationality is a property of humans, not all animals. The conclusion 'This is rational' is false. The error is in the matter (content) due to misapplying the species' property to the genus. A researcher (identified in the margin as al-Sabban) objects that this type of error does not involve confusion of false with true because the false premise ('every animal is rational') does not resemble a true proposition; it is blatantly false. Therefore, it does not fit under the umbrella of 'confusion of false with true'. However, the response is that such confusion is not the exclusive cause of all error but only of a subset. The second fallacy is treating a non-certain proposition as certain, which involves presenting a probable or conjectural statement as if it were indubitable. The grammatical analysis: 'wa-ja`l' is a verbal noun governing two objects; the first object is 'ghayr al-qat`i' (non-certain) and the second object is 'ka-l-qat`i' (like certain), with the prepositional phrase 'ka-l-qat`i' intervening between the verbal noun and its first object. This illustrates the structure of the phrase.

Key Terms
الحكم للجنس بحكم النوع
مادّة
التباس الكاذبة بذات صدق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of the fallacy of judging the genus by the property of the species?
A) Every human is mortal; Socrates is human; therefore Socrates is mortal.
B) This is a liquid; every liquid is intoxicating; therefore this is intoxicating.
C) This is a plant; every plant is green; therefore this is green.
D) This is an action; every action is obligatory; therefore this is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Judging a genus by a species-specific property is a material fallacy that does not necessarily involve confusing false with true when the false premise is blatant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct an example of the fallacy of judging a genus by a species' property using an Islamic legal ruling (e.g., from prayer or fasting).
?
Explain whether your example counts as a 'confusion of false with true' according to al-Sabban's objection.

Fallacy: Treating Non-Certain as Certain
Al-Bajuri explains the fallacy of treating non-certain as certain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of treating a non-certain proposition as certain in a syllogistic argument.

Core Idea
Presenting a probable claim as indubitable undermines certainty of the conclusion.

The second fallacy discussed by al-Bajuri is treating a non-certain (zanni) proposition as if it were certain (qat'i). This often occurs when a theologian or jurist asserts a premise that is only probable with absolute conviction. You will learn to recognise this fallacy and understand its grammatical expression in Arabic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the fallacy
The fallacy involves using a premise that is not certain (only probable or conjectural) but presenting it as if it were certain. This can lead to a false conclusion if the premise is false, or at least a conclusion that lacks certainty.
2
Analyse the Arabic structure
Al-Bajuri explains the idafa: 'ja`l' (treating) governs two objects: the first is 'ghayr al-qat`i' (non-certain) and the second is 'ka-l-qat`i' (like certain). The prepositional phrase intervenes. This grammatical analysis clarifies the meaning of the phrase.
The structure: verbal noun + first object + second object (intervening).
3
Recognise the material error
The error is not in the form of the argument but in the matter: a non-certain premise is treated as certain, which can mislead the reasoner into accepting a conclusion with false confidence.
4
Apply to Islamic sciences
In fiqh and kalam, many rulings are based on zanni evidence (e.g., ahadith). Treating them as qat'i can lead to flawed arguments and overconfident conclusions.
For example, in debates on qiyas, some jurists elevate a zanni 'illah to certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الباجوري
قَوْلُهُ : (وَالحُكْمِ لِلْجِنْسِ بِحُكْمِ النَّوْعِ) «اللَّامُ فِيهِ بِمَعْنَى : عَلَى».
وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ : هَذَا حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ نَاطِقُ يَنْتُجُ : «هَذَا نَاطِقٌ»، فَإِنَّ الخَطَأ فِي ذَلِكَ فِي مَادَّتِهِ مِنْ حَيْثُ المَعْنَى بِسَبَبِ الحُكْمِ عَلَى الجِنْسِ بِحُكْمِ النَّوْعِ .

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Two Types of Material Error in Syllogisms
Commentator defines two material error types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the two types of material error in syllogisms: judging the genus by the property of the species, and treating a non-definitive premise as if it were definitive.

Core Idea
Material errors in syllogisms arise from false premises that appear true, not from faulty form.

In this lesson, we examine two specific types of material error that make a syllogism invalid even if its form is correct. The commentator defines these errors and gives examples. Understanding these errors will help you detect flawed arguments in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types from the matn
Find the two phrases that name the types: 'الحكم للجنس بحكم النوع' and 'جعل غير القطعي كالقطعي'.
These are the two categories discussed.
2
Understand the first type with example
Example: 'This is an animal, every animal is rational, therefore this is rational.' The error is attributing a property of a species to the entire genus.
Rationality belongs to humans, not all animals.
3
Understand the second type with example
Example: 'This is dead, every dead thing is inanimate, therefore this is inanimate.' The error is treating a non-definitive premise (not all dead things are inanimate) as definitive.
Humans are dead but have a spirit, so the premise is uncertain.
4
Distinguish material from formal error
Note that these errors are in the content (premises) not the structure. The syllogistic form may be valid, but the premises are false.
5
Recognise the common element
Both types involve a premise that is false but resembles truth, leading to iltibas (concealment).
This commonality will be debated in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْحُكْمُ لِلْجِنْسِ بِحُكْمِ النَّوْعِ وَجَعْلِ كَالْقَطْعِيِّ غَيْرِ الْقَطْعِيِ
قَدْ بَحَثَ سَيِّدِي سَعِيدٌ فِي كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ : بِأَنَّ الخَطَأ فِيمَا جُعِلَتْ فِيهِ النَّتِيجَةُ إِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَاتِ لَيْسَ فِي المَعْنَى لِاِلْتِبَاسِ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ ؛ لِصِدْقِ كُلِّ مِنَ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، بَلْ لَيْسَ مِنْ جِهَةِ المَادَّةِ أَصْلاً ، وَإِنَّمَا هُوَ مِنْ جِهَةِ أَنَّ النَّتِيجَةَ لَيْسَتْ مُغَايرَةٌ لِلمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، وَالوَاجِبُ أَنْ تَكُونَ مُغَايِرَةٌ لَهُمَا ، كَمَا عُرِفَ فِي حَدِّ القِيَاسِ . وَأَجَابَ الشَّيْخُ المَلَّوِيُّ فِي شَرْحِهِ الكَبِيرِ حَيْثُ قَالَ : وَإِذَا دَقَقْتَ النَّظَرَ وَجَدْتَ إِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ كَاذِبَةٌ ؛ لِأَنَّ فِيهَا حَمْلُ الشَّيْءِ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ ، وَالحَمْلُ يَقْتَضِي المُغَايرَةَ، وَمُغَايرَةُ الشَّيْءِ لِنَفْسِهِ مُخَالِفَةٌ لِلوَاقِعِ ، فَتَكُونُ كَاذِبَةٌ شَبِيهَةٌ بِالصَّادِقَةِ، فَصَحَّ جَعْلُ ذَلِكَ مِنْ أَنْوَاعِ العِبَاسِ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ . وَلَا يَخْفَى مَا فِيهِ مِنَ التَّكَلُّفِ

قَوْلُهُ : (وَالحُكْمِ لِلْجِنْسِ بِحُكْمِ النَّوْعِ) اللَّامُ فِيهِ بِمَعْنَى : عَلَى. وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ : هَذَا حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ نَاطِقُ يَنْتُجُ : هَذَا نَاطِقٌ، فَإِنَّ الخَطَأ فِي ذَلِكَ فِي مَادَّتِهِ مِنْ حَيْثُ المَعْنَى بِسَبَبِ الحُكْمِ عَلَى الجِنْسِ بِحُكْمِ النَّوْعِ . وَبَحَثَ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ فِي كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ : بِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ فِي ذَلِكَ التِبَاسُ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ؛ لأَنَّ المُقَدِّمَةَ الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا عَلَى الجِنْسِ بِحُكْمِ النَّوْعِ لَيْسَتْ شَبيهَةٌ بِالصَّادِقَةِ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ كَاذِبَةٌ، وَحِينَئِذٍ فَلَا يَصِحُ جَعْلُ ذَلِكَ مِنْ أَنْوَاعِ الرِّبَاسِ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ ; قَالَ : وَيُمْكِنُ أَنْ يُقَالَ : اليَبَاسُ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ لَيْسَ عِلَّةٌ لِجَمِيعِ الخَطَأ، بَلْ لِمَجْمُوعِهَا، فَافْهَمْ

قَوْلُهُ : (وَجَعْلِ كَالقَطْعِيُّ غَيْرِ القَطْعِيْ) بِجَرِّ غَيْرِ) بِإِضَافَةِ جَعْلِ إِلَيْهِ، مِنْ إِضَافَةِ المَصْدَرِ لِمَفْعُولِهِ الأَوَّلِ، وَفَصَلَ بَيْنَ المُضَافِ وَالمُضَافِ إِلَيْهِ بِالمَفْعُولِ الثَّانِي وَهُوَ قَوْلُهُ: كَالقَطْعِي

وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ : هَذَا مَيِّتُ، وَكُلُّ مَيِّتٍ جَمَادٌ، يَنْتُجُ : هَذَا جَمَادٌ، فَإِنَّ الخَطَا فِي ذَلِكَ فِي مَادَّتِهِ مِنْ حَيْثُ المَعْنَى بِسَبَبِ جَعْلِ غَيْرِ القَطْعِيِّ كَالفَطْعِيِّ، وَيَأْتِي فِي هَذَا النَّوْعِ مَا ذُكِرَ فِي النَّوْعِ قَبْلَهُ بَحْثاً وَجَوَاباً، فَتَفَطَنْ.
And the judgment of the genus by the judgment of the species, and making the non-definitive like the definitive. Sayyid Sa'id investigated in the author's words: that the error in that in which the conclusion is made one of the premises is not in meaning due to the concealment of the false with the true; because each of the two premises is true, but rather it is not from the aspect of matter at all, but rather it is from the aspect that the conclusion is not different from the two premises, and it is necessary that it be different from them, as known in the definition of syllogism. And al-Shaykh al-Mallawi answered in his great commentary, where he said: And if you scrutinize, you find one of the two premises false; because in it there is predication of the thing upon itself, and predication requires difference, and the difference of a thing from itself is contrary to reality, so it is false resembling the true, so it is correct to make that among the types of concealing the false in the garb of truth. And what is in it of artificiality is not hidden. His saying: (And the judgment of the genus by the judgment of the species) the lam in it means 'upon'. An example of that is that you say: This is an animal, and every animal is rational, so it produces: This is rational. For the error in that is in its matter from the aspect of meaning due to the judgment upon the genus by the judgment of the species. And some investigators investigated in the author's words: that in that there is no concealment of false with true; because the premise in which judgment is made upon the genus by the judgment of the species is not similar to the true, even if it is false, and then it is not correct to make that among the types of concealing false with true. He said: And it can be said: The concealment of false with true is not a cause for all error, but rather for its totality, so understand. His saying: (And making the non-definitive like the definitive) with the genitive of 'ghayr' by the addition of 'ja'l' to it, from the addition of the verbal noun to its first object, and he separated between the added and the added-to by the second object which is his saying: 'like the definitive'. And an example of that is that you say: This is dead, and every dead thing is inanimate, so it produces: This is inanimate. For the error in that is in its matter from the aspect of meaning due to making the non-definitive like the definitive. And there will come in this type what was mentioned in the type before it of investigation and answer, so be perceptive.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage defines and discusses two types of material error (al-khaṭa' fī al-mādda) in syllogisms. The matn line states the two types: (1) judging the genus by the law of the species, and (2) making a non-definitive premise appear as definitive. The commentary begins with an objection by Sayyid Sa'id: He argues that the error in the first type is not material (fī al-ma'nā) but formal, because the premises themselves are true, but the conclusion is not different from them — violating the rule that the conclusion must be different. Al-Mallawi responds by showing that one premise is actually false because it involves predication of a thing to itself (e.g., 'every animal is rational' implies an identity that is false), making it false but resembling truth. Therefore, the error is material (fī al-mādda) due to concealment (iltibās). The commentator then explains the first type with an example: 'This is an animal, every animal is rational, therefore this is rational.' The error is that 'animal' (genus) is given the property 'rational' which belongs only to the species 'human'. Another investigator (possibly al-Sabban) objects that this premise is not similar to truth, so it does not constitute concealment. The commentator suggests a resolution: concealment is not the cause of all such errors but only of the aggregate. Finally, the second type is exemplified: 'This is dead, every dead thing is inanimate, therefore this is inanimate.' The error is treating a non-definitive (al-ghayr al-qaṭ'ī) claim — not all dead things are inanimate (humans are dead but not inanimate in the sense of lacking spirit) — as though it were definitive. The same investigation and answer apply to this type.

Key Terms
الخطأ في المادة
الحكم للجنس بحكم النوع
جعل غير القطعي كالقطعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of the second type (making non-definitive like definitive)?
A) This is an animal, every animal is rational, therefore this is rational.
B) This is dead, every dead thing is inanimate, therefore this is inanimate.
C) Every Muslim prays, every prayer is worship, so every Muslim worships.
D) Every Quran is revealed, every revealed book is true, so the Quran is true.

Key Takeaway
Material errors in syllogisms involve false premises that often appear true, specifically by attributing a species property to a genus or treating an uncertain claim as certain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
For each of the following syllogisms, identify which type of material error (if any) is present: (a) Every human is a biped; every biped is a bird; so every human is a bird. (b) Every created thing is temporal; the world is created; so the world is temporal. (c) This is a tree; every tree is animate; so this is animate.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
The Debate on Iltibas in Material Errors
Debate on whether material errors involve concealment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the argument of Sayyid Sa'id and al-Mallawi regarding whether material errors constitute 'iltibās al-kādhiba bi dhāt ṣidq' (concealing falsehood with truth).

Core Idea
Whether a false premise resembles truth determines if an error involves concealment.

After defining the two material errors, the commentator records an objection: Are these errors really due to concealment of falsehood? Some argue that the false premise is not similar to truth, so it is not concealment. Al-Mallawi defends the classification. This lesson analyses that debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection by Sayyid Sa'id
Find his reasoning: the error is not material but formal because the premises are true, but the conclusion is not different from them.
He says the error is in the form, not the matter.
2
Analyse al-Mallawi's response
He says one premise is false because it predicates a thing to itself (e.g., 'every animal is rational'), and such predication violates reality, making the premise false but resembling truth.
Thus the error is material and involves iltibas.
3
Examine the second objection
Another muhaqqiq objects that the premise 'every animal is rational' is not similar to truth, so it does not constitute concealment.
This challenges whether the premise appears true.
4
Understand the commentator's resolution
The concealment is not the cause of each individual error but of the aggregate; i.e., iltibas explains why these errors are commonly made.
This reconciles the views.
5
Apply to the second type
The same investigation applies: the premise 'every dead thing is inanimate' is not obviously true, so does it resemble truth?
The commentator says the same debate applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْحُكْمُ لِلْجِنْسِ بِحُكْمِ النَّوْعِ وَجَعْلِ كَالْقَطْعِيِّ غَيْرِ الْقَطْعِيِ
قَدْ بَحَثَ سَيِّدِي سَعِيدٌ فِي كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ : بِأَنَّ الخَطَأ فِيمَا جُعِلَتْ فِيهِ النَّتِيجَةُ إِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَاتِ لَيْسَ فِي المَعْنَى لِاِلْتِبَاسِ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ ؛ لِصِدْقِ كُلِّ مِنَ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، بَلْ لَيْسَ مِنْ جِهَةِ المَادَّةِ أَصْلاً ، وَإِنَّمَا هُوَ مِنْ جِهَةِ أَنَّ النَّتِيجَةَ لَيْسَتْ مُغَايرَةٌ لِلمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، وَالوَاجِبُ أَنْ تَكُونَ مُغَايِرَةٌ لَهُمَا ، كَمَا عُرِفَ فِي حَدِّ القِيَاسِ . وَأَجَابَ الشَّيْخُ المَلَّوِيُّ فِي شَرْحِهِ الكَبِيرِ حَيْثُ قَالَ : وَإِذَا دَقَقْتَ النَّظَرَ وَجَدْتَ إِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ كَاذِبَةٌ ؛ لِأَنَّ فِيهَا حَمْلُ الشَّيْءِ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ ، وَالحَمْلُ يَقْتَضِي المُغَايرَةَ، وَمُغَايرَةُ الشَّيْءِ لِنَفْسِهِ مُخَالِفَةٌ لِلوَاقِعِ ، فَتَكُونُ كَاذِبَةٌ شَبِيهَةٌ بِالصَّادِقَةِ، فَصَحَّ جَعْلُ ذَلِكَ مِنْ أَنْوَاعِ العِبَاسِ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ . وَلَا يَخْفَى مَا فِيهِ مِنَ التَّكَلُّفِ

قَوْلُهُ : (وَالحُكْمِ لِلْجِنْسِ بِحُكْمِ النَّوْعِ) اللَّامُ فِيهِ بِمَعْنَى : عَلَى. وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ : هَذَا حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ نَاطِقُ يَنْتُجُ : هَذَا نَاطِقٌ، فَإِنَّ الخَطَأ فِي ذَلِكَ فِي مَادَّتِهِ مِنْ حَيْثُ المَعْنَى بِسَبَبِ الحُكْمِ عَلَى الجِنْسِ بِحُكْمِ النَّوْعِ . وَبَحَثَ بَعْضُ المُحَقِّقِينَ فِي كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ : بِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ فِي ذَلِكَ التِبَاسُ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ؛ لأَنَّ المُقَدِّمَةَ الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا عَلَى الجِنْسِ بِحُكْمِ النَّوْعِ لَيْسَتْ شَبيهَةٌ بِالصَّادِقَةِ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ كَاذِبَةٌ، وَحِينَئِذٍ فَلَا يَصِحُ جَعْلُ ذَلِكَ مِنْ أَنْوَاعِ الرِّبَاسِ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ ; قَالَ : وَيُمْكِنُ أَنْ يُقَالَ : اليَبَاسُ الكَاذِبَةِ بِذَاتِ صِدْقٍ لَيْسَ عِلَّةٌ لِجَمِيعِ الخَطَأ، بَلْ لِمَجْمُوعِهَا، فَافْهَمْ

قَوْلُهُ : (وَجَعْلِ كَالقَطْعِيُّ غَيْرِ القَطْعِيْ) بِجَرِّ غَيْرِ) بِإِضَافَةِ جَعْلِ إِلَيْهِ، مِنْ إِضَافَةِ المَصْدَرِ لِمَفْعُولِهِ الأَوَّلِ، وَفَصَلَ بَيْنَ المُضَافِ وَالمُضَافِ إِلَيْهِ بِالمَفْعُولِ الثَّانِي وَهُوَ قَوْلُهُ: كَالقَطْعِي

وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ : هَذَا مَيِّتُ، وَكُلُّ مَيِّتٍ جَمَادٌ، يَنْتُجُ : هَذَا جَمَادٌ، فَإِنَّ الخَطَا فِي ذَلِكَ فِي مَادَّتِهِ مِنْ حَيْثُ المَعْنَى بِسَبَبِ جَعْلِ غَيْرِ القَطْعِيِّ كَالفَطْعِيِّ، وَيَأْتِي فِي هَذَا النَّوْعِ مَا ذُكِرَ فِي النَّوْعِ قَبْلَهُ بَحْثاً وَجَوَاباً، فَتَفَطَنْ.
And the judgment of the genus by the judgment of the species, and making the non-definitive like the definitive. Sayyid Sa'id investigated in the author's words: that the error in that in which the conclusion is made one of the premises is not in meaning due to the concealment of the false with the true; because each of the two premises is true, but rather it is not from the aspect of matter at all, but rather it is from the aspect that the conclusion is not different from the two premises, and it is necessary that it be different from them, as known in the definition of syllogism. And al-Shaykh al-Mallawi answered in his great commentary, where he said: And if you scrutinize, you find one of the two premises false; because in it there is predication of the thing upon itself, and predication requires difference, and the difference of a thing from itself is contrary to reality, so it is false resembling the true, so it is correct to make that among the types of concealing the false in the garb of truth. And what is in it of artificiality is not hidden. His saying: (And the judgment of the genus by the judgment of the species) the lam in it means 'upon'. An example of that is that you say: This is an animal, and every animal is rational, so it produces: This is rational. For the error in that is in its matter from the aspect of meaning due to the judgment upon the genus by the judgment of the species. And some investigators investigated in the author's words: that in that there is no concealment of false with true; because the premise in which judgment is made upon the genus by the judgment of the species is not similar to the true, even if it is false, and then it is not correct to make that among the types of concealing false with true. He said: And it can be said: The concealment of false with true is not a cause for all error, but rather for its totality, so understand. His saying: (And making the non-definitive like the definitive) with the genitive of 'ghayr' by the addition of 'ja'l' to it, from the addition of the verbal noun to its first object, and he separated between the added and the added-to by the second object which is his saying: 'like the definitive'. And an example of that is that you say: This is dead, and every dead thing is inanimate, so it produces: This is inanimate. For the error in that is in its matter from the aspect of meaning due to making the non-definitive like the definitive. And there will come in this type what was mentioned in the type before it of investigation and answer, so be perceptive.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage defines and discusses two types of material error (al-khaṭa' fī al-mādda) in syllogisms. The matn line states the two types: (1) judging the genus by the law of the species, and (2) making a non-definitive premise appear as definitive. The commentary begins with an objection by Sayyid Sa'id: He argues that the error in the first type is not material (fī al-ma'nā) but formal, because the premises themselves are true, but the conclusion is not different from them — violating the rule that the conclusion must be different. Al-Mallawi responds by showing that one premise is actually false because it involves predication of a thing to itself (e.g., 'every animal is rational' implies an identity that is false), making it false but resembling truth. Therefore, the error is material (fī al-mādda) due to concealment (iltibās). The commentator then explains the first type with an example: 'This is an animal, every animal is rational, therefore this is rational.' The error is that 'animal' (genus) is given the property 'rational' which belongs only to the species 'human'. Another investigator (possibly al-Sabban) objects that this premise is not similar to truth, so it does not constitute concealment. The commentator suggests a resolution: concealment is not the cause of all such errors but only of the aggregate. Finally, the second type is exemplified: 'This is dead, every dead thing is inanimate, therefore this is inanimate.' The error is treating a non-definitive (al-ghayr al-qaṭ'ī) claim — not all dead things are inanimate (humans are dead but not inanimate in the sense of lacking spirit) — as though it were definitive. The same investigation and answer apply to this type.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Mallawi argue that the premise 'every animal is rational' is false but resembles truth?
A) Because rationality is a property of all animals.
B) Because it is a common saying.
C) Because it involves predication of a thing to itself, which is false but the form is similar to true predications.
D) Because the conclusion is true.

Key Takeaway
The debate on whether material errors involve concealment reveals the sophistication of Islamic logicians in analysing the nature of fallacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the first material error example (animal/natiq), do you think the premise 'every animal is rational' resembles truth? Why or why not? Write a brief argument supporting either Sayyid Sa'id or al-Mallawi.
?
Find a syllogism from your own knowledge of fiqh or kalam that might involve a non-definitive premise treated as definitive. Identify whether the error would be classified as material and whether it involves concealment.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Two Types of Formal Error in Syllogisms
Commentator defines two formal errors: missing the middle term and violating the condition of production.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of formal error in syllogisms and identify examples of each.

Core Idea
Formal errors break the rules of syllogistic form, not the truth of premises.

In this lesson, we explore two ways a syllogism can be formally invalid: by lacking a middle term (exiting the figures) or by violating a condition needed for the figure to produce a conclusion. Understanding these errors helps you construct sound syllogisms in Islamic reasoning. The commentator provides concrete examples to illustrate each error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
Examine the two premises. Look for a term that appears in both premises. If there is no such term, the syllogism lacks a middle term and 'exits the figures'.
In the first example, 'human' and 'stone' share no common term.
2
Determine the figure if a middle term exists
If a middle term is present, classify the syllogism into one of the four figures based on the position of the middle term.
The second example has 'stone' as middle term: major 'every stone is inanimate', minor 'no human is a stone', so it is second figure.
3
Recall the production conditions for that figure
Each figure has specific conditions for producing a valid conclusion (e.g., second figure requires a negative major and a positive minor).
The condition for second figure: the minor premise must be positive.
4
Check if the conditions are met
If a condition is violated, the error is 'leaving the condition of production'. Verify that the premises satisfy all conditions for the figure.
In the second example, the minor 'no human is a stone' is negative, violating the positivity condition.
5
Distinguish by example
The first error (exiting figures) has no middle term; the second error (leaving condition) has a middle term but fails a figure-specific condition.
Practice by creating your own examples for each error type.

وَهُوَ جَائِز ; لِاسْتِكْمَالِهِ الشُّرُوطَ، وَهِيَ كَونُ المُصَافِ شَبِيها بِالفِعْلِ فِي العَمَلِ، وَكَوْنُ الفَاصِلِ مَنْصُوبَهُ، وَكَوْنُهُ وَاحِداً .

قَوْلُهُ : (كَـ : الخُرُوجِ عَنْ أَشْكَالِهِ كَأَنْ لَمْ يُوْتَ فِيهِ بِالحَدِّ الوَسَطِ .

وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ: لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ، وَكُلُّ حَجَرٍ جَمَادٌ، فَإِنَّ الخَطَأ فِي ذَلِكَ فِي صُورَتِهِ بِسَبَبٍ شَرْطِ تَرْكِ إِنْتَاجِ الشَّكْلِ، وَهُوَ إِيجَابُ الصُّغْرَى. وَقَوْلُهُ : (مِنْ إِكْمَالِهِ يُحْتَمَلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ حَالاً مِنْ تَرْكِ، وَعَلَيْهِ فَالمَعْنَى : حَالَ كَوْنِ ذَلِكَ التَّرْكِ مِنْ إِكْمَالِ الثَّانِي . وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ حَالاً مِنْ شَرْطِ، وَعَلَيْهِ فَالمَعْنَى : حَالَ كَوْنِ ذَلِكَ الشَّرْطِ مِنْ إِكْمَالِ النَّتَّحِ. وَلَا يَخْفَى مَا فِي ذَلِكَ مِنْ حُسْنِ الِاخْتِتَامِ، وَهُوَ : أَنْ يَذْكُرَ المُؤَلِّفُ شَيْئاً يُشْعِرُ بِانْقِضَاءِ المَقْصُودِ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِ بَعْضِ الفُضَلَاءِ : [من الرجز] وَقُلْ بِدُلٌ: رَبِّ لَا تَقْطَعْنِي عَنْكَ بِقَاطِعٍ وَلَا تَحْرِمْـنِــي مِنْ سِرِّكَ الأَبْهَى المُزِيلِ لِلعَمَى وَاخْتِمْ بِخَيْرٍ يَا رَحِيمَ الرُّحَمَا
Conclusion. (127) And the second is like: exiting from its figures and leaving the condition of production from its completion. And it is permissible because it fulfills the conditions, which are: the added (al-muḍāf) being like the verb in operation, the separator (al-fāṣil) being in the accusative, and it being one. His saying: (And the second) meaning: that which is the error in form. His saying: (like: exiting from its figures) such that the middle term is not brought in it. An example of that is that you say: Every human is an animal, and every stone is inanimate, for the error in that is in its form due to exiting from the figures of the syllogism. His saying: (and leaving the condition of production) what is meant by 'al-natḥ' is production (intāj). An example of that is that you say: No human is a stone, and every stone is inanimate, for the error in that is in its form due to the condition of leaving the production of the figure, which is the positivity of the minor premise. And his saying: (from its completion) it is possible that it is a circumstantial qualifier of 'leaving', and according to that the meaning is: while that leaving is from the completion of the second. And it is possible that it is a circumstantial qualifier of 'condition', and according to that the meaning is: while that condition is from the completion of production. And what is in that of beauty of conclusion is not hidden, and it is: that the author mentions something that indicates the completion of the purpose; as in the saying of some virtuous: (in rajaz) And say with supplication: O Lord, do not cut me off from You with any cutter, and do not deprive me of Your most radiant secret that removes blindness, and conclude with good, O Most Merciful of the merciful.

Commentary
This passage defines two types of formal error (al-khaṭa' fī al-ṣūra) in syllogisms. The matn line introduces 'the second [error]' — meaning formal error — which includes: (1) exiting from the figures of the syllogism, and (2) leaving the condition of production. The commentator first notes that this type of error is 'permissible' (jā'iz) in the sense that it can still fulfill the conditions of a valid productive syllogism? Actually the text says 'wa huwa jā'iz' – this is a remark on the possibility of such error occurring, not that it is allowed. The conditions listed (the added being like a verb, the separator accusative, etc.) refer to the formal structure of a conditional syllogism perhaps? Actually, this section is about the error itself, and these conditions are prerequisites for a valid production? The commentary is a bit unclear; likely these conditions are for the second type of formal error. The commentator clarifies that 'the second' means the formal error. Then he explains the first subtype: exiting from the figures, meaning the syllogism lacks a middle term or does not follow any recognized figure. Example: 'Every human is an animal, every stone is inanimate' – there is no common middle term linking the two premises, so no conclusion follows. The second subtype: leaving the condition of production, meaning the syllogism violates a condition necessary for the figure to produce a conclusion. Example: 'No human is a stone, every stone is inanimate' – this is in the second figure where the minor premise must be positive, but here it is negative. The error lies in the form, not the matter. Finally, the passage ends with a beautiful conclusion, citing a poem asking for divine protection and blessing, indicating the completion of the book or section.

The Syllogism

Error in Form: Departure from Figures and Violating Conclusion Conditions
Bajuri defines error in form with two subtypes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish two subtypes of error in form in categorical syllogisms: departure from figures and violation of conclusion conditions.

Core Idea
Not all invalid syllogisms are fallacious in content — many fail due to their structure alone.

In demonstration, an argument can be invalid either because of its matter (content) or its form (structure). This lesson focuses on the second type: formal errors. Bajuri breaks these down into two kinds: when the syllogism does not belong to any valid figure (e.g., missing a middle term), and when it is in a valid figure but violates a condition required for that figure to produce a conclusion. Understanding these formal errors is crucial for constructing sound arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two-fold classification of error
The passage states that the second type of error in demonstration is error in form (al-khaṭa' fī al-ṣūra). This is distinct from error in matter (al-khaṭa' fī al-mādda).
Error in matter relates to the truth of premises; error in form relates to logical structure.
2
Understand the first subtype: departure from figures
Al-khurūj 'an ashkālihi occurs when the syllogism lacks a proper middle term, so it does not fit any of the four valid figures. Example: 'Every human is an animal, every stone is inanimate' — no common term.
The middle term must connect the two premises.
3
Understand the second subtype: leaving the condition of conclusion
Tark sharṭ al-natj occurs when the syllogism is in a valid figure but violates a condition necessary for that figure to produce a conclusion. For the first figure, the minor premise must be affirmative. Example: 'No human is a stone, every stone is inanimate' — minor premise negative.
Conditions vary by figure; here the focus is on the first figure.
4
Apply the distinction to the given examples
The passage provides two examples of formal error. Verify each: (a) 'Every human is an animal, every stone is inanimate' — departure from figures. (b) 'No human is a stone, every stone is inanimate' — violation of conclusion condition.
Compare with the example of material error: 'This is dead, every dead is inanimate' — here the form is valid but the premise is not definitive.
5
Recognize the importance of conditions for validity
Even if a syllogism fits a figure, it must meet additional conditions (like quality and quantity of premises) to produce a valid conclusion. This passage highlights that formal errors can be subtle and must be checked systematically.
In Islamic kalām, such conditions are rigorously applied to theological proofs.

(۱۲۷) وَ(الثَّانِ) كَ: الخُرُوجِ عَنْ أَشْكَالِهِ وَتَرْكِ شَرْطِ النَّفْجِ مِنْ إِكْمَالِهِ وَهُوَ جَائِزٌ؛ لِاسْتِكْمَالِهِ الشُّرُوطَ، وَهِيَ كَوْنُ المُصَافِ شَبِيها بِالفِعْلِ فِي العَمَلِ، وَكَوْنُ الفَاصِلِ مَنْصُوبَهُ، وَكَوْنُهُ وَاحِداً . وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ: هَذَا مَيِّتُ، وَكُلُّ مَيِّتٍ جَمَادٌ، يَنْتُجُ: هَذَا جَمَادٌ، فَإِنَّ الخَطَا فِي ذَلِكَ فِي مَادَّتِهِ مِنْ حَيْثُ المَعْنَى بِسَبَبٍ جَعْلِ غَيْرِ القَطْعِيِّ كَالْقَطْعِيِّ، وَيَأْتِي فِي هَذَا النَّوْعِ مَا ذُكِرَ فِي النَّوْعِ قَبْلَهُ بَحْثاً وَجَوَاباً، فَتَفَطَنْ. قَوْلُهُ: (وَالثَّانِ) أي: الَّذِي هُوَ الخَطَأ فِي الصُّورَةِ. قَوْلُهُ: (كَ: الخُرُوجِ عَنْ أَشْكَالِهِ كَأَنْ لَمْ يُوْتَ فِيهِ بِالحَدِّ الوَسَطِ. وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَكُلُّ حَجَرٍ جَمَادٌ، فَإِنَّ الخَطَأَ فِي ذَلِكَ فِي صُورَتِهِ بِسَبَبِ الخُرُوجِ عَنْ أَشْكَالِ القِيَاسِ . قَوْلُهُ: (وَتَرْكِ شَرْطِ النَّتْجِ المُرَادُ بِـ «النَّتْجِ: الإِنْتَاجُ . وَمِثَالُ ذَلِكَ أَنْ تَقُولَ: «لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ، وَكُلُّ حَجَرٍ جَمَادٌ»، فَإِنَّ الخَطَأ فِي ذَلِكَ فِي صُورَتِهِ بِسَبَبٍ شَرْطِ تَرْكِ إِنْتَاجِ الشَّكْلِ، وَهُوَ إِيجَابُ الصُّغْرَى. وَقَوْلُهُ: (مِنْ إِكْمَالِهِ يُحْتَمَلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ حَالاً مِنْ تَرْكِ، وَعَلَيْهِ فَالمَعْنَى: حَالَ كَوْنِ ذَلِكَ التَّرْكِ مِنْ إِكْمَالِ الثَّانِي. وَيُحْتَمَلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ حَالاً مِنْ شَرْطِ، وَعَلَيْهِ فَالمَعْنَى: حَالَ كَوْنِ ذَلِكَ الشَّرْطِ مِنْ إِكْمَالِ النَّتَّحِ. وَلَا يَخْفَى مَا فِي ذَلِكَ مِنْ حُسْنِ الِاخْتِتَامِ، وَهُوَ: أَنْ يَذْكُرَ المُؤلِّفُ شَيْئاً يُشْعِرُ بِانْقِضَاءِ المَقْصُودِ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِ بَعْضِ الفُضَلَاءِ: [من الرجز] وَقُلْ بِدُلٌ: رَبِّ لَا تَقْطَعْنِي عَنْكَ بِقَاطِعٍ وَلَا تَحْرِمْـنِــي مِنْ سِرِّكَ الأَبْهَى المُزِيلِ لِلعَمَى وَاخْتِمْ بِخَيْرٍ يَا رَحِيمَ الرُّحَمَا
(127) And the second [type] is like: departing from its figures and leaving the condition of the conclusion from its perfection. And it is permissible because it fulfills the conditions, which are: the premise being similar to the verb in action, the predicate being in the accusative, and it being one. An example of that is you say: 'This is dead, and every dead is inanimate,' producing: 'This is inanimate.' So the error in that is in its matter from the aspect of meaning due to making the non-definitive like the definitive. And in this type what was mentioned in the preceding type comes in terms of inquiry and answer, so be attentive. His statement: 'and the second' means the one which is the error in form. His statement: 'like: departing from its figures' as if the middle term is not brought in it. An example of that is you say: 'Every human is an animal, and every stone is inanimate.' So the error in that is in its form due to departing from the figures of the syllogism. His statement: 'and leaving the condition of the conclusion' — the intended meaning of 'conclusion' is production. An example of that is you say: 'No human is a stone, and every stone is inanimate.' So the error in that is in its form due to leaving the condition of production of the figure, which is the affirmation of the minor premise. And his statement: 'from its perfection' — it is possible that it is a circumstantial clause from 'leaving', and according to that the meaning is: that leaving being from the perfection of the second. And it is possible that it is a circumstantial clause from 'condition', and according to that the meaning is: that condition being from the perfection of the conclusion. And it is not hidden what is in that of beautiful closure, which is: that the author mentions something that indicates the completion of the intended purpose, as in the statement of some scholars: [from rajaz] 'And say with supplication: O Lord, do not cut me off from You with a cutter, and do not deprive me of Your highest secret that removes blindness, and seal with good, O Most Merciful of the merciful.'
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a definitional explanation of the second type of error in demonstration, which is error in form (as opposed to error in matter). The commentator (Bajuri) first states the matn: the second type is like departing from the figures of the syllogism or leaving a condition of the conclusion. He then explains that this type is possible if certain conditions are met (e.g., the premise resembles an action, the predicate is accusative, etc.). He gives an example: 'This is dead, every dead is inanimate; therefore this is inanimate.' This is an error in matter because it assumes a non-definitive premise as definitive. Then he clarifies each part of the matn: 'and the second' refers to error in form. He provides two examples of formal error: (1) departing from figures, e.g., 'Every human is an animal, every stone is inanimate' — here there is no middle term linking the two premises, so the syllogism is invalid; (2) leaving the condition of conclusion, e.g., 'No human is a stone, every stone is inanimate' — here the minor premise is negative, but the first figure requires an affirmative minor premise to produce a conclusion. Finally, he discusses the phrase 'from its perfection' and notes two possible grammatical interpretations, leading to a remark about the beauty of the author's closing. The inclusion of a poetic prayer illustrates the idea of a good conclusion.

Recognizing a Categorical Syllogism: The Basmala Proof
Syllogistic proof that this book's beginning should be with the basmala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three parts (major premise, minor premise, conclusion) of a categorical syllogism in an Islamic scholarly text.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism can prove even the basmala's necessity.

In this passage, the commentator uses a categorical syllogism to justify beginning the book with the basmala. By parsing the syllogism, you will gain practice in extracting logical structure from Arabic texts. The lesson focuses on identifying premises, conclusion, and the middle term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
Find the statement that is being proved. It appears after 'yantiju' (yields): 'This book requires its beginning with the basmala.'
2
Identify the major premise
The major premise is the general rule: 'Every important matter requires its beginning with the basmala.' It contains the predicate of the conclusion and the middle term.
3
Identify the minor premise
The minor premise is the specific case: 'The composition of this book is an important matter.' It contains the subject of the conclusion and the middle term.
4
Identify the middle term
The middle term is 'important matter' (amr dhī bāl / amr muhim). It appears in both premises but not in the conclusion.
5
Verify figure and mood
This is first figure (subject is middle term in major premise, predicate is middle term in minor premise), mood Barbara (AAA-1). Check that the form is valid: All M are P; all S are M; therefore all S are P.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللتأسي بكتاب الله تعالى، واشتمال الجملة الاسمية على ما ذكر، واقتضائها زيادة على ما ذكر : قصر الحمد على الله تعالى المشعر بقطع النظر عن ما سواه؛ أخذاً من تعريف طرفي الجملة، عدل النَّاظم عن الفعليَّة إلى الاسمية. قوله : (بَعْدَ البَسْمَلَةِ) يعني : أنَّ البسملة وجدت بخط المؤلّف قبيل الحمدلة، وإنَّما لم يأت بها منظومة ؛ لئلا تتغير عن أسلوبها . قوله : (كُلُّ أَمْرٍ ذِي بَالٍ) أي : ذي شأن عظيم وحالٍ يُهتم به، فـ «البال»: الشأن، والتنكير للتعظيم . فيقال: تَألِيفُ هَذَا الكِتَابِ أَمْرٌ مُهِمْ، وَ كُلُّ أَمْرٍ مُهِمْ يُطْلَبُ بَدَاءَتَهُ بِالبَسْمَلَةِ ينتج : : هَذَا الكِتَابُ يُطْلَبُ بَدَاءَتَهُ بِالبَسْمَلَةِ .
And out of following the example of Allah's Book, and because the nominal sentence includes what was mentioned, and because it necessitates in addition to what was mentioned the restriction of praise to Allah, suggesting disregard for anyone else – taken from the definiteness of both sides of the sentence – the author changed from the verbal to the nominal. His statement: 'After the basmala' means that the basmala was found in the author's handwriting before the praise, and he did not include it in the poem lest it deviate from its style. His statement: 'Every important matter' means: every matter of great significance and state that is cared for. So 'al-bāl' means 'state', and the indefinite is for magnification. So it is said: The composition of this book is an important matter, and every important matter requires its beginning with the basmala. This yields: This book requires its beginning with the basmala.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage contains a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) in the first figure. The commentator explains why the author changed from a verbal to a nominal sentence, then addresses the placement of the basmala. The proof that this book should begin with the basmala is presented as a syllogism: Major premise: 'Every important matter requires its beginning with the basmala.' Minor premise: 'The composition of this book is an important matter.' Conclusion: 'Therefore, this book requires its beginning with the basmala.' The minor premise is supported by the definition of 'important matter' (dhī bāl), which the commentator explains as a matter of great significance that is cared for. The syllogism follows the valid mood: Every A is B; C is A; therefore C is B. This is a concrete example of applying logic to a textual issue.

Key Terms
أمر ذي بال
البسملة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) This book
B) Important matter
C) Requires the basmala
D) The author

Key Takeaway
A categorical syllogism consists of a major premise, minor premise, and conclusion, with the middle term linking them.

The Syllogism

Deriving the Basmalah via Categorical Syllogism from the Hadith
Commentator's gloss and syllogistic proof for basmalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the categorical syllogism from the commentator's reasoning and identify its figure and mood.

Core Idea
A general prophetic principle plus a specific case yields a necessary conclusion via syllogism.

The commentator uses a well-known hadith 'Every important matter that is not begun with basmalah is cut off' to justify opening this book with basmalah. He does not simply quote the hadith; he constructs a categorical syllogism, showing how the general rule applies to the particular case of this book. Understanding this syllogism will sharpen your ability to see how scholars reason from revealed texts to specific applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise
Find the general rule from the hadith in the Arabic. The commentator states: 'Every important matter requires its beginning with the basmalah.' This is the major premise, a universal affirmative proposition.
2
Identify the minor premise
Find the specific claim about this book: 'The authorship of this book is an important matter.' This is the minor premise, also a universal affirmative.
3
Extract the conclusion
The conclusion follows necessarily: 'This book requires its beginning with the basmalah.' This is deduced from the two premises.
4
Locate the middle term
The middle term is 'important matter' (أمر مهم), which appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. It connects the subject (this book) and predicate (requires basmalah).
5
Determine figure and mood
This is a first-figure syllogism (middle term is subject of major and predicate of minor). The mood is Barbara (AAA) because all three propositions are universal affirmatives. This is a valid form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بَعْدَ البَسْمَلَةِ) يعني : أنَّ البسملة وجدت بخط المؤلّف قبيل الحمدلة، وإنَّما لم يأت بها منظومة ؛ لئلا تتغير عن أسلوبها . قوله : (كُلُّ أَمْرٍ ذِي بَالٍ) أي : ذي شأن عظيم وحالٍ يُهتم به، فـ «البال»: الشأن، والتنكير للتعظيم . فيقال: تَألِيفُ هَذَا الكِتَابِ أَمْرٌ مُهِمْ، وَ كُلُّ أَمْرٍ مُهِمْ يُطْلَبُ بَدَاءَتَهُ بِالبَسْمَلَةِ ينتج: هَذَا الكِتَابُ يُطْلَبُ بَدَاءَتَهُ بِالبَسْمَلَةِ .
His statement: (After the basmalah) means that the basmalah was found in the author's handwriting before the hamdalah, and he only did not bring it in versified form so that it would not deviate from its style. His statement: (Every important matter) means: every matter of great significance and situation that is cared about, and 'bal' means concern, and the indefinite is for magnifying. So it is said: The authorship of this book is an important matter, and every important matter requires its beginning with the basmalah, so it follows: This book requires its beginning with the basmalah.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's opening line 'After the basmalah'. The commentator explains that the basmalah was written by the author before the hamdalah (praise) but was not included in the poem to preserve its style. Then he glosses the phrase 'Every important matter' from the hadith, defining 'bal' as a great concern and noting the indefinite article for emphasis. Finally, he constructs a categorical syllogism: 'Every important matter requires beginning with the basmalah' (major premise from the hadith), 'The authorship of this book is an important matter' (minor premise), therefore 'This book requires beginning with the basmalah' (conclusion). This demonstrates how the hadith is applied to justify the basmalah at the start of the book. The syllogism is in the first figure (Barbara) with the middle term 'important matter'.

الحمدلة
كُلُّ أَمْرٍ ذِي بَالٍ

Critique and Resolution of the Division of Hamd into Absolute and Restricted
Author critiques the initial division of hamd into mutlaq and muqayyad and provides a corrected understanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the flaw in the initial division of hamd into mutlaq and muqayyad and correctly distinguish between the corrected meanings as praise of essence vs. praise for a specific attribute.

Core Idea
The division of hamd should be based on the object of praise, not on whether the object is mentioned.

The text begins by noting a widely used division of hamd into absolute (mutlaq) and restricted (muqayyad), but immediately raises a logical objection: since hamd is defined as praise of Allah, it is always restricted. After explaining the objection, the passage resolves it by redefining the terms: mutlaq means praise for the mere essence, muqayyad means praise for a specific attribute. In this lesson, we will walk through the logic of this critique and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial division and its definitions
Find where the text states the common division: mutlaq is hamd without mentioning the praised object, muqayyad is with mentioning it. Note these definitions.
The passage says: 'ومرادهم بالمقيد: ما ذكر فيه المحمود عليه، وبالمطلق: ما لم يذكر'.
2
Understand the logical objection
The objection is that hamd inherently includes the praised object in its definition (it is praise of Allah). So all hamd is muqayyad in reality, making the division impossible. Identify the reasoning: 'لأنه مأخوذ في تعريفه'.
The objection is based on definitional inclusion.
3
Find the resolution and corrected definitions
The resolution states that the intended meaning of muqayyad is not mentioning the object, but praising for a specific attribute (e.g., al-Raziq). Mutlaq is praising the essence alone. Locate this in the text.
'بل المراد بالمقيد عند بعضهم: الحمد للذات لأجل شيء'.
4
Evaluate the resolution
Check if this resolution avoids the initial objection: since the division now concerns the content (essence vs. attribute) rather than mentioning the object, it does not conflict with the definition of hamd.
The objection is nullified because the division no longer relies on whether the object is mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٢) - الثَّانِي : قَدِ اشْتُهِرَ هُنَا تَقْسِيمُ الحَمْدِ» إِلَى : مُطلَقٍ، وَمُقَيَّد . - وَمُرَادُهُمْ [ج/ ١] بـ «المُقَيَّدِ»: «مَا ذُكِرَ فِيهِ المَحْمُودُ عَلَيْهِ»، وَبِـ «المُطْلَقِ»: «مَا لَمْ يُذْكَرْ ، وَهُوَ تَقْسِيمٌ مَعَ قِلَّةِ جَدْوَاهُ يُوهِمُ انْفِكَاكَهُ عَنِ المَحْمُودِ عَلَيْهِ، وَهُوَ لَا يَصِحُ؛ لِأَنَّهُ مَأخوذُ فِي تَعْرِيفِهِ، فَهُوَ مُقَيَّدٌ أَبَداً ؛ سَوَاءٌ ذُكِرَ القَيْدُ أَوْ لَمْ يُذْكَرْ . قصارة وهو ثبوت جميع المحامد الله تعالى، وأمَّا حصول الحمد بها من المتكلم فإنَّما أوجده نطقه بها ، مع الإذعان لمدلولها، فهي إنشاء من هذه الحيثيَّة؛ بمعنى: أنَّ الحمد منه أوجده بعد أن لم يكن، لا بمعنى ما يقابل الخبر، وكذلك قولنا : زَيْدٌ عَالِم»، فاتصافه بالعلم حاصل ؛ سواءٌ تكلَّم المتكلّم بذلك أو لا، فهو خبرٌ ،محض، وأما قيام هذا المعنى بنفس المتكلم مع إذعانه له، فهو أمر حدث بعد أن لم يكن أصلاً ، أو بعد أن كان مغفولاً عنه، فهو منشأ بهذا الاعتبار ؛ أي موجد . [اهـ](١) . قال شيخنا اليازغي : وفي ذكر الشَّارح لهذا التنبيه هنا خروج عن الموضوع؛ إذ ليس كونها خبريَّةٌ وإنشائيّة من متعلقات الفنّ والصَّوابُ : أن يذكر ما هو من الفنّ، والصَّوابُ : أن يذكر ما هو من متعلقات الفنّ ككون جملة الحمد قضيَّة كلّيَّةٌ، أو جزئيَّة، أو شخصيَّةٌ، أو مهملةً، وبيان ذلك : أنا ننظر إلى «آل» الداخلة على «الحمد) : فإن قلنا : «إنّها للاستغراق»، فالقضيَّةُ كلِّيَّةٌ ; لأنَّ الحكم وقع فيها على جميع أفراد الحمد. - وإن قلنا : «إنَّها لتعريف الجنس، تكون طبيعيَّة ; لأنَّ الحكم وقع فيها على الماهية كقولك: الإنسان نوع، والحَيَوَان جنس». - وإن قلنا : «إنَّها عهديَّةٌ»، فتكون شخصيَّةً، والمعهود: حمد الله لنفسه في أزله. قوله : (الثَّانِي: قَدِ اشْتُهِرَ ... إلخ) أصلُ هذا الإشكال لسيدي عبد القادر الفاسي - نفعنا الله تعالى به ، وتبعه عليه ولده سيدي محمد في شرح الحصن، وتبعهما الهلالي في شرح القادرية»، والشارح هنا . وهو إشكال لا حاصل له ؛ إذ ليس المراد بـ «المقيَّد» ما ذكر فيه المحمود عليه، وبـ «المطلق» ما لم يذكر، كما توهم حتَّى يَرِدُ الإشكال، بل المراد بـ «المقيَّد» عند بعضهم: «الحمد للذات لأجل شيء؛ نحو: «الحَمْدُ للهِ الرَّازِقِ»، وبـ المطلق»: «الحَمْدُ عَلَى مُجَرَّدِ الذَّاتِ»، وعليه درج النفراوي في شرح الرسالة».
(2) The second: It has become widespread here the division of hamd into absolute and restricted. And their intended meaning by 'restricted' is 'that in which the praised object is mentioned', and by 'absolute' is 'that in which it is not mentioned'. And it is a division that, despite its little bearing, suggest that hamd can be separate from the praised object, and that is not correct; because the praised object is taken into its definition, so it is always restricted, whether the restriction is mentioned or not. Marginal note: And it is the establishment of all praiseworthy attributes to Allah, Exalted. As for the occurrence of hamd through it from the speaker, he only originated it by his utterance, with acknowledgment of its meaning, so it is performative in this respect; in the sense that the hamd from him originated it after it was not, not in the sense of what contrasts with khabar. And similarly our saying 'Zayd is knowledgeable': his attribute of knowledge exists whether the speaker speaks that or not, so it is pure khabar. But as for the occurrence of this meaning in the speaker's soul with his acknowledgment, it is a matter that came into being after it was not originally, or after it was forgotten, so it is originated in this consideration, i.e., produced. [End quote]. Our master al-Yazghi said: The commentator's mentioning this note here is a departure from the subject; for the issue of whether it is khabariyya or insha'iyya is not among the matters of the art. The correct thing is to mention what is from the matters of the art, such as whether the sentence 'al-hamdu' is universal, particular, singular, or indefinite. And the explanation of that: we look at the 'al' entering upon 'al-hamd': if we say it is for istighraq (all-inclusive), then the proposition is universal because the judgment applies to all individuals of hamd. If we say it is for definition of the genus, it is natural (tabi'iyya) because the judgment applies to the essence, like your saying 'man is a species' and 'animal is a genus'. If we say it is for 'ahd (specific), then it is singular, and the specific referent is Allah's praise of Himself in pre-eternity. His saying: (The second: ... etc.) The origin of this objection is from Sidi 'Abd al-Qadir al-Fasi – may Allah benefit us with him – and his son Sidi Muhammad followed him in his commentary on al-Hisn, and al-Hilali followed them in his commentary on al-Qadiriyya, and the commentator here. And it is an objection that has no headway; because the intended meaning by 'restricted' is not that the praised object is mentioned, as imagined such that the objection arises, rather the intended meaning by 'restricted' according to some is 'praise of the essence for the sake of something' like 'al-hamdu lillah al-raziq', and by 'absolute' is 'praise upon the mere essence'. And upon this is the way of al-Nafrawi in his commentary on al-Risala.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage critiques the common division of hamd into absolute (mutlaq) and restricted (muqayyad). The initial objection states that since the praised object is inherent in the definition of hamd, all hamd is always restricted, making the division invalid. A marginal note then re-explains the meaning of hamd in terms of performative vs. declarative, but is criticized by al-Yazghi as irrelevant to logic. Instead, al-Yazghi argues, the focus should be on the logical form of the sentence 'al-hamdu lillah' – i.e., determination of its quantifier (universal, particular, singular, indefinite) by analyzing the definite article 'al'. The passage then provides a resolution to the original objection: the terms 'mutlaq' and 'muqayyad' do not refer to the mention of the object but to different intentions: muqayyad means praise for a specific attribute (like 'the Provider'), while mutlaq means praise for the mere essence. This resolves the objection because the division concerns the content of the praise, not whether the object is mentioned.

Key Terms
الحمد المطلق
الحمد المقيد

D) Hamd that is indefinite.

Key Takeaway
The logical flaw in the initial division is resolved by redefining mutlaq and muqayyad based on the object of praise (essence vs. attribute) rather than mentioning the object.

Determining the Logical Form of 'Al-Hamdu Lillah'
Author explains how to analyze the logical quantification of the sentence 'al-hamdu lillah' using the definite article.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the proposition 'al-hamdu lillah' and determine whether it is universal, particular, singular, or indefinite based on the meaning of the definite article 'al'.

Core Idea
The type of proposition depends on the 'al' prefix: istighraq gives universal, jins gives natural, 'ahd gives singular.

In the latter part of the passage, al-Yazghi argues that the commentator's discussion of performative vs. declarative is irrelevant to logic. Instead, he says the correct logical analysis of the sentence 'al-hamdu lillah' is to determine its quantification by examining the definite article 'al'. In this lesson, we will follow his method and learn how to classify propositions based on the meaning of 'al'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three possible meanings of 'al' in 'al-hamdu'
The text lists three meanings: istighraq (all-inclusive), ta'rif al-jins (definition of genus), and 'ahdiyya (specific). Find these in the passage.
‘al’ can also be indefinite (muhmala) – but the text only explicitly mentions these three.
2
Determine the resulting proposition type for each meaning
For istighraq, the judgment applies to all individuals → universal. For jins, the judgment applies to the essence → natural (tabi'iyya). For 'ahd, the judgment applies to a specific referent → singular. Understand the reasoning in the text.
The text gives examples: 'man is a species' for natural, and 'Allah's praise of Himself in pre-eternity' for singular.
3
Apply the analysis to 'al-hamdu lillah'
Consider each possible meaning and see what it implies. The text does not decide which is correct, but shows how to analyze it.
This is a tool for exegesis and logical precision.

Source Text

(٢) - الثَّانِي : قَدِ اشْتُهِرَ هُنَا تَقْسِيمُ الحَمْدِ» إِلَى : مُطلَقٍ، وَمُقَيَّد . - وَمُرَادُهُمْ [ج/ ١] بـ «المُقَيَّدِ»: «مَا ذُكِرَ فِيهِ المَحْمُودُ عَلَيْهِ»، وَبِـ «المُطْلَقِ»: «مَا لَمْ يُذْكَرْ ، وَهُوَ تَقْسِيمٌ مَعَ قِلَّةِ جَدْوَاهُ يُوهِمُ انْفِكَاكَهُ عَنِ المَحْمُودِ عَلَيْهِ، وَهُوَ لَا يَصِحُ؛ لِأَنَّهُ مَأخوذُ فِي تَعْرِيفِهِ، فَهُوَ مُقَيَّدٌ أَبَداً ؛ سَوَاءٌ ذُكِرَ القَيْدُ أَوْ لَمْ يُذْكَرْ . قصارة وهو ثبوت جميع المحامد الله تعالى، وأمَّا حصول الحمد بها من المتكلم فإنَّما أوجده نطقه بها ، مع الإذعان لمدلولها، فهي إنشاء من هذه الحيثيَّة؛ بمعنى: أنَّ الحمد منه أوجده بعد أن لم يكن، لا بمعنى ما يقابل الخبر، وكذلك قولنا : زَيْدٌ عَالِم»، فاتصافه بالعلم حاصل ؛ سواءٌ تكلَّم المتكلّم بذلك أو لا، فهو خبرٌ ،محض، وأما قيام هذا المعنى بنفس المتكلم مع إذعانه له، فهو أمر حدث بعد أن لم يكن أصلاً ، أو بعد أن كان مغفولاً عنه، فهو منشأ بهذا الاعتبار ؛ أي موجد . [اهـ](١) . قال شيخنا اليازغي : وفي ذكر الشَّارح لهذا التنبيه هنا خروج عن الموضوع؛ إذ ليس كونها خبريَّةٌ وإنشائيّة من متعلقات الفنّ والصَّوابُ : أن يذكر ما هو من الفنّ، والصَّوابُ : أن يذكر ما هو من متعلقات الفنّ ككون جملة الحمد قضيَّة كلّيَّةٌ، أو جزئيَّة، أو شخصيَّةٌ، أو مهملةً، وبيان ذلك : أنا ننظر إلى «آل» الداخلة على «الحمد) : فإن قلنا : «إنّها للاستغراق»، فالقضيَّةُ كلِّيَّةٌ ; لأنَّ الحكم وقع فيها على جميع أفراد الحمد. - وإن قلنا : «إنَّها لتعريف الجنس، تكون طبيعيَّة ; لأنَّ الحكم وقع فيها على الماهية كقولك: الإنسان نوع، والحَيَوَان جنس». - وإن قلنا : «إنَّها عهديَّةٌ»، فتكون شخصيَّةً، والمعهود: حمد الله لنفسه في أزله. قوله : (الثَّانِي: قَدِ اشْتُهِرَ ... إلخ) أصلُ هذا الإشكال لسيدي عبد القادر الفاسي - نفعنا الله تعالى به ، وتبعه عليه ولده سيدي محمد في شرح الحصن، وتبعهما الهلالي في شرح القادرية»، والشارح هنا . وهو إشكال لا حاصل له ؛ إذ ليس المراد بـ «المقيَّد» ما ذكر فيه المحمود عليه، وبـ «المطلق» ما لم يذكر، كما توهم حتَّى يَرِدُ الإشكال، بل المراد بـ «المقيَّد» عند بعضهم: «الحمد للذات لأجل شيء؛ نحو: «الحَمْدُ للهِ الرَّازِقِ»، وبـ المطلق»: «الحَمْدُ عَلَى مُجَرَّدِ الذَّاتِ»، وعليه درج النفراوي في شرح الرسالة».
(2) The second: It has become widespread here the division of hamd into absolute and restricted. And their intended meaning by 'restricted' is 'that in which the praised object is mentioned', and by 'absolute' is 'that in which it is not mentioned'. And it is a division that, despite its little bearing, suggest that hamd can be separate from the praised object, and that is not correct; because the praised object is taken into its definition, so it is always restricted, whether the restriction is mentioned or not. Marginal note: And it is the establishment of all praiseworthy attributes to Allah, Exalted. As for the occurrence of hamd through it from the speaker, he only originated it by his utterance, with acknowledgment of its meaning, so it is performative in this respect; in the sense that the hamd from him originated it after it was not, not in the sense of what contrasts with khabar. And similarly our saying 'Zayd is knowledgeable': his attribute of knowledge exists whether the speaker speaks that or not, so it is pure khabar. But as for the occurrence of this meaning in the speaker's soul with his acknowledgment, it is a matter that came into being after it was not originally, or after it was forgotten, so it is originated in this consideration, i.e., produced. [End quote]. Our master al-Yazghi said: The commentator's mentioning this note here is a departure from the subject; for the issue of whether it is khabariyya or insha'iyya is not among the matters of the art. The correct thing is to mention what is from the matters of the art, such as whether the sentence 'al-hamdu' is universal, particular, singular, or indefinite. And the explanation of that: we look at the 'al' entering upon 'al-hamd': if we say it is for istighraq (all-inclusive), then the proposition is universal because the judgment applies to all individuals of hamd. If we say it is for definition of the genus, it is natural (tabi'iyya) because the judgment applies to the essence, like your saying 'man is a species' and 'animal is a genus'. If we say it is for 'ahd (specific), then it is singular, and the specific referent is Allah's praise of Himself in pre-eternity. His saying: (The second: ... etc.) The origin of this objection is from Sidi 'Abd al-Qadir al-Fasi – may Allah benefit us with him – and his son Sidi Muhammad followed him in his commentary on al-Hisn, and al-Hilali followed them in his commentary on al-Qadiriyya, and the commentator here. And it is an objection that has no headway; because the intended meaning by 'restricted' is not that the praised object is mentioned, as imagined such that the objection arises, rather the intended meaning by 'restricted' according to some is 'praise of the essence for the sake of something' like 'al-hamdu lillah al-raziq', and by 'absolute' is 'praise upon the mere essence'. And upon this is the way of al-Nafrawi in his commentary on al-Risala.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage critiques the common division of hamd into absolute (mutlaq) and restricted (muqayyad). The initial objection states that since the praised object is inherent in the definition of hamd, all hamd is always restricted, making the division invalid. A marginal note then re-explains the meaning of hamd in terms of performative vs. declarative, but is criticized by al-Yazghi as irrelevant to logic. Instead, al-Yazghi argues, the focus should be on the logical form of the sentence 'al-hamdu lillah' – i.e., determination of its quantifier (universal, particular, singular, indefinite) by analyzing the definite article 'al'. The passage then provides a resolution to the original objection: the terms 'mutlaq' and 'muqayyad' do not refer to the mention of the object but to different intentions: muqayyad means praise for a specific attribute (like 'the Provider'), while mutlaq means praise for the mere essence. This resolves the objection because the division concerns the content of the praise, not whether the object is mentioned.

Checkpoint
According to the text, if 'al' in 'al-hamdu' is for istighraq, what type of proposition is formed?
A) Singular
B) Natural
C) Universal
D) Indefinite

Key Takeaway
The logical quantification of any sentence beginning with 'al' depends on the meaning of the definite article, which can be istighraq (universal), jins (natural), or 'ahd (singular).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the sentence 'al-‘alimu ‘alimun' ('the knowledgeable is knowledgeable'), analyze the 'al' prefix and determine what type of proposition it would be if 'al' is for istighraq, jins, or 'ahd. Justify your answers using the categories from the lesson.
